Distinguishing Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Author defines categorical and conditional propositions and argues for the priority of the categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a categorical (hamli) and a conditional (sharti) proposition based on the independence of their parts.

Core Idea
The categorical is simpler and foundational; the conditional depends on it.

In this passage, the author lays the groundwork by defining two types of propositions based on the independence of their parts. Understanding this distinction is crucial for all subsequent logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of propositions
The author defines categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti) based on whether each part can be true or false independently.
2
Check independence of parts
For a categorical proposition like 'Man is an animal', both 'Man' and 'animal' can stand alone as concepts, but the proposition requires a relation. For a conditional, like 'If the sun rises, it is daytime', neither part is independently a complete proposition without the conditional particle.
3
Determine priority
Note the author's claim: the categorical is simpler and complete without the conditional, while the conditional depends on the categorical. This establishes the order of study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في تقديم النظر في الحملي وتحقيق الحملية وبيان مفرداتها. نقول: قد بان أن الأقوال الجازمة ما كانت دالة على العلوم التصديقية، وكما أن كل علم تصديقي فلا بد فيه من نسبة عقلية بين معنيين إما صادقة وإما كاذبة، فكذا كل قول جازم لا بد فيه من نسبة لفظية بين مفردين إما صادقة بأن تكون النسبة اللفظية على وفق النسبة المعنوية في نفس الأمر، أو كاذبة بأن تكون على خلافها، وهو إما أن يكون بحيث لا يصح لكل واحد من جزأي هذه النسبة أو يصح، فما مثل الأول يسمى (حَمْلياً) وما مثل الثاني يسمى (شرطياً) فإذن الحَمْلي أبسط من الشرطي، وهو يتم دون الشرطي، والشرطي لا يتم دونه فالنظر فيه يجب أن يكون أولاً وأولى. فنقول: وإذ قد عرف أن الحَمْلي هو ما كان من الأقوال الجازمة ليس ولا لواحد من جزأي النسبة الخبرية الحاصلة [الجزئه؟]، وهو ما يحكم فيه بشيء على شيء هو أو ليس هو فلا بد فيه من لفظين وهما غير كافيين فإنك لو قلت الإنسان حيوان لم یكن قولاً جازماً، بل لا بد فيه من نسبة واقعة بينهما توجب جعل أحدهما محكوماً به والآخر محكوماً عليه، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان أو ليس وليس ما لكل واحد من نسبته إلى الآخر هي نسبة الآخر إليه، بل نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر هي غير ما للآخر من النسبة إليه ولذلك تختلف جهة نسبة القضية عند العكس كما يأتي تعريفه فالحكم بنسبة المحكوم عليها إلى المحكوم به يسمى (وضعاً)، ولذلك له (موضوعاً) والحكم بنسبة المحكوم به إلى المحكوم عليه تسمى (حَمْلاً)، ولذلك سمي ما هي له محمولاً) وما هي له من الأقوال الجازمة (حَمْلياً)، لكن إطلاق اسم الحكم والحمل إنما هو للنسبة الإيجابية أولاً، وللنسبة السلبية ثانياً، فإن كل سلب حكم العقل به لا بد فيه من تقدير نسبة الإيجاب أولاً، إذ
Chapter One: On presenting the consideration of the categorical [proposition], and verifying the categorical and clarifying its components. We say: It has become clear that decisive statements are those which indicate assenting sciences. And just as every assenting science necessarily involves a mental relation between two meanings, either true or false, so too every decisive statement necessarily involves a linguistic relation between two terms, either true—by the linguistic relation being in accordance with the mental relation in reality—or false—by it being contrary to it. And that [relation] is either such that it is not valid for each one of the two parts of this relation, or it is valid. What is like the first is called 'categorical', and what is like the second is called 'conditional'. Thus the categorical is simpler than the conditional, and it is complete without the conditional, while the conditional is not complete without it. So the consideration of it must be first and prior. We say: And since it has been known that the categorical is that which among decisive statements does not have [a conditional particle] for either of the two parts of the predicative relation that occurs [in the part?], and it is that in which one judges something about something, either it is or it is not. So it necessarily involves two expressions, and they are not sufficient. For if you said 'Man is an animal' it would not be a decisive statement. Rather, a relation occurring between them is necessary, which makes one of them the thing judged by and the other the thing judged upon, like your saying 'Man is an animal' or 'is not'. And it is not the case that for each one, its relation to the other is the other's relation to it. Rather, the relation of each one to the other is other than what the other has of relation to it. Therefore the aspect of the proposition's relation differs upon conversion, as its definition will come. So the judgment of the relation of the thing judged upon to the thing judged by is called 'position', and therefore it has a 'subject'. And the judgment of the relation of the thing judged by to the thing judged upon is called 'predication', and therefore that which has it is called 'predicate'. And that which has it among decisive statements is called 'categorical'. But the application of the terms 'judgment' and 'predication' is initially for the affirmative relation, and secondarily for the negative relation. For every negation, the mind's judgment of it necessarily involves first supposing an affirmative relation, since...
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage opens the first chapter of the work, dedicated to the study of categorical propositions. The author begins by establishing a foundational point: every decisive statement (qawl jazim) that yields assent (tasdiq) involves two levels of relation—a mental relation between two concepts and a linguistic relation between two terms. The truth or falsity of the statement depends on whether the linguistic relation matches the mental one. The author then distinguishes between two types of statements based on whether the parts of the relation are independent. If neither part can stand alone as a complete proposition (i.e., they require a conditional particle like 'if'), it is a conditional (sharti) proposition. If both parts are independent, it is a categorical (hamli) proposition. The author stresses that the categorical is simpler and prior, being the foundation for the conditional. Next, the author defines the categorical proposition more precisely: it is a decisive statement that does not involve a conditional particle for either part, and it makes a judgment of something (the predicate) about something (the subject). Simply uttering two words like 'Man animal' does not form a statement; a relation (nisba) between them is required, which makes one the subject (mahkum 'alayh) and the other the predicate (mahkum bihi). The author then introduces technical terms: the judgment that relates the subject to the predicate is called wad' (position), giving rise to the term mawdu' (subject); the judgment relating the predicate to the subject is called haml (predication), giving rise to mahmul (predicate). A key insight is that the relation is not symmetrical: the subject's relation to the predicate is different from the predicate's relation to the subject, which explains why conversion ('aks) changes the aspect of the proposition. Finally, the author notes that the terms 'judgment' and 'predication' apply primarily to affirmative propositions, and secondarily to negative ones, because negation presupposes an affirmative relation in the mind. This passage establishes the essential conceptual tools for analyzing categorical propositions.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the categorical proposition simpler than the conditional?
A) It has fewer words.
B) It is complete without the conditional, while the conditional depends on it.
C) It always involves affirmation.
D) It has no relation between its parts.

Key Takeaway
The categorical proposition is the foundational building block of logic because it requires no conditional particle and can stand alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a categorical or conditional proposition: (a) 'If the prayer is valid, then wudu is required.' (b) 'Charity purifies wealth.'
?
Explain why the categorical is considered simpler and prior, using the author's reasoning.

Propositions
intermediate
6 min
Components of a Categorical Proposition
Author explains the components of a categorical proposition: subject, predicate, and the relations wad' and haml.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul) of a categorical proposition and explain the roles of wad' and haml.

Core Idea
The subject is what we judge about; the predicate is what we judge by; their relation is not symmetrical.

Having established what a categorical proposition is, the author now dissects its internal structure: the two terms and the relation between them. This lesson reveals the precise terminology for subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms needed
The author says a categorical proposition needs two expressions (لفظين), but they are insufficient alone. A relation (نسبة) must connect them.
2
Distinguish subject and predicate
The relation makes one term the subject (محكوم عليه) and the other the predicate (محكوم به). The judgment linking the subject to the predicate is called wad' (وضع), giving 'mawdu' (موضوع)', and the judgment linking the predicate to the subject is called haml (حمل), giving 'mahmul' (محمول).
3
Note the asymmetry
The relation is not symmetric: the subject's relation to the predicate is different from the predicate's relation to the subject. This explains why conversion ('aks) changes the proposition's aspect.
4
Understand affirmation as primary
The terms 'judgment' and 'predication' are used primarily for affirmative propositions; negation presupposes affirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Understanding Categorical vs Conditional Propositions
Author defines categorical proposition and argues for its priority over conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional and explain why the categorical is foundational for all other propositions.

Core Idea
The categorical proposition is the simplest and most basic form of assertive statement.

In this passage, the author defines the categorical proposition (ḥamlī) as an assertive statement that predicates something of a subject without condition. He contrasts it with the conditional (sharṭī) and argues that the categorical is simpler and epistemologically prior. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the building blocks of logical reasoning in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of propositions
Locate the terms حملي and شرطي in the Arabic. Note that the author is distinguishing two kinds of assertive statements based on how the verbal relation relates to its parts.
Categorical directly predicates; conditional involves a condition linking two propositions.
2
Understand the parallel between mental and verbal relations
The author states that just as assenting knowledge (علم تصديقي) has a mental relation (نسبة عقلية) between two meanings, so too an assertive statement (قول جازم) has a verbal relation (نسبة لفظية) between two terms. This parallels the truth-conditional structure.
The verbal relation must conform to the mental relation for truth.
3
Determine the criterion for classification
The key difference is whether the relation 'holds for each of the two parts individually'. For categorical (حملي), it does not hold—the subject and predicate are not each independently the whole relation. For conditional (شرطي), it does hold—each part can stand alone as a complete proposition.
For example, in 'Zaid is standing', 'Zaid' and 'standing' are not each a complete proposition. But in 'If Zaid is standing, then Amr is sitting', both 'Zaid is standing' and 'Amr is sitting' are complete propositions.
4
Follow the argument for priority
The author argues that the categorical is simpler (أبسط) because it is complete without the conditional, while the conditional cannot be understood without the categorical. Therefore, the categorical should be studied first.
This is like foundational principles in fiqh: usul are studied before furu’.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في تقديم النظر في الحملي وتحقيق الحملية وبيان مفرداتها. نقول: قد بان أن الأقوال الجازمة ما كانت دالة على العلوم التصديقية، وكما أن كل علم تصديقي فلا بد فيه من نسبة عقلية بين معنيين إما صادقة وإما كاذبة، فكذا كل قول جازم لا بد فيه من نسبة لفظية بين مفردين إما صادقة بأن تكون النسبة اللفظية على وفق النسبة المعنوية في نفس الأمر، أو كاذبة بأن تكون على خلافها، وهو إما أن يكون بحيث لا يصح لكل واحد من جزئي هذه النسبة أو يصح، فما مثل الأول يسمى (حَمْلياً) وما مثل الثاني يسمى (شرطياً) فإذن الحَمْلي أبسط من الشرطي، وهو يتم دون الشرطي، والشرطي لا يتم دونه فالنظر فيه يجب أن يكون أولاً وأولى.
First chapter: on introducing the consideration of the categorical, and verifying the categorical proposition and explaining its components. We say: It has become clear that assertive statements are what signify assenting knowledges. And just as every assenting knowledge necessarily has a mental relation between two meanings, either true or false, so too every assertive statement necessarily has a verbal relation between two terms, either true—by the verbal relation conforming to the mental relation in actuality—or false—by its being contrary to it. And it is either such that it does not hold for each of the two parts of this relation, or it does hold. What is like the first is called 'categorical' and what is like the second is called 'conditional'. Thus the categorical is simpler than the conditional, and it is complete without the conditional, while the conditional is not complete without it. Therefore, consideration of it must be first and prior.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition and establishes its priority over the conditional. The author begins by noting that assertive statements (al-aqwāl al-jāzima) convey assenting knowledge (al-‘ulūm al-taṣdīqiyya). Just as assenting knowledge requires a mental relation (nisba ‘aqliyya) between two concepts that is either true or false, so too an assertive statement requires a verbal relation (nisba lafẓiyya) between two terms that conforms to the mental relation. The author then distinguishes between two types of verbal relations: those where the relation does not hold for each of the two parts individually (i.e., the subject and predicate are not each independently related to the whole) and those where it does hold. The first type is called categorical (ḥamlī), meaning it predicates something of something directly; the second is called conditional (sharṭī), meaning it involves a condition. The author argues that the categorical is simpler (absaṭ) than the conditional, because the categorical is complete without the conditional, but the conditional cannot be understood without the categorical. Therefore, the categorical should be studied first. This sets the stage for a detailed analysis of categorical propositions, akin to how in fiqh, foundational principles (uṣūl) are studied before derived rulings (furū‘).

Key Terms
قول جازم
نسبة لفظية
حملي
شرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between categorical and conditional propositions?
A) Both are equally basic and independent.
B) The conditional is simpler than the categorical.
C) The categorical is complete without the conditional, but the conditional depends on the categorical.
D) The categorical is a type of conditional.

Key Takeaway
The categorical proposition is the foundational type; all other propositions, including conditionals, rely on it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as categorical or conditional and explain your reasoning: (a) 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' (b) 'If it rains, the ground becomes wet.' (c) 'Zakat is obligatory.'
?

The Three Components of a Categorical Proposition
Author explains the necessary components of a categorical proposition and the role of the copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and the relational link (copula) in any categorical proposition from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition is not just two terms; it requires a relation binding them.

In this passage, the author explains why simply putting two words together does not make a statement. He shows that a categorical proposition requires three elements: something judged upon (subject), something judged (predicate), and the judgment relation that connects them. This triad is the foundation for all logical analysis in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Take the expression 'Human animal'. It has two terms: 'human' and 'animal'. But this is not a proposition. Why? Because there is no relation asserted between them.
2
Add the copula
Now consider 'Human is animal'. Here 'is' indicates the relation. The proposition becomes complete. The subject is 'human', the predicate is 'animal', and the copula 'is' expresses the relation.
The copula can be explicit or implicit.
3
Recognize directionality
The relation is not symmetric. The subject is what the predicate is attributed to; the predicate is what is attributed. The judgment that relates them is called 'position' (waḍ‘) when seen from subject to predicate, and 'predication' (ḥaml) when seen from predicate to subject.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the proposition 'الصلاة واجبة' (Prayer is obligatory). Identify: subject = الصلاة, predicate = واجبة, relation = 'is' (understood). This is a categorical proposition used in fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: وإذ قد عرف أن الحَمْلي هو ما كان من الأقوال الجازمة ليس ولا لواحد من جزأي النسبة الخبرية الحاصلة الجزئه، وهو ما يحكم فيه بشيء على شيء هو أو ليس هو فلا بد فيه من لفظين وهما غير كافيين فإنك لو قلت الإنسان حيوان لم یکن قولاً جازماً، بل لا بد فيه من نسبة واقعة بينهما توجب جعل أحدهما محكوماً به والآخر محكوماً عليه، كقولك: الإنسان حيوان أو ليس وليس ما لكل واحد من نسبته إلى الآخر هي نسبة الآخر إليه، بل نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر هي غير ما للآخر من النسبة إليه ولذلك تختلف جهة نسبة القضية عند العكس كما يأتي تعريفه فالحكم بنسبة المحكوم عليها إلى المحكوم به يسمى (وضعاً)، ولذلك له (موضوعاً) والحكم بنسبة المحكوم به إلى المحكوم عليه تسمى (حَمْلاً)، ولذلك سمي ما هي له محمولاً) وما هي له من الأقوال الجازمة (حَمْلياً)، لكن إطلاق اسم الحكم والحمل إنما هو للنسبة الإيجابية أولاً، وللنسبة السلبية ثانياً، فإن كل سلب حكم العقل بـه لا بد فيه من تقدير نسبة الإيجاب أولاً، إذ السلب عدم الإيجاب، وليس الإيجاب عدم السلب وفهم عدم الشيء متوقف على سبق الفهم لذلك الشيء وليس فهمه مما يتوقف على سبق الفهم لعدمه، فالسلب هو سلب الإيجاب وليس الإيجاب إيجاباً للسلب، وإلا كانت الموجبة سالبة، ولا سلب السلب، وإلا كان الإيجاب هو عدم السلب. ولا يخفى ما فيه، وعلى هذا فإطلاق اسم الموضوع وهو ما يحكم عليه بشيء آخر أنه هو أو ليس هو هو، واسم المحمول وهو ما يحكم به على غيره أنه هو أو ليس هو هو، واسم الحَمْلية وهي القضية الكائنة منهما إنما هو أولاً لما اسم الحكم والحمل على ما النسبة الواقعة بينهما أولاً وهي نسبة الإيجاب، وليس يكفي في تحقيق هذه النسبة وجعل أحد الأمرين موضوعاً، والآخر محمولاً تقدم أحدهما على الآخر في النطق وإلا كان قولنا الإنسان الحيوان قولاً جازماً، بل وكما أنه لا بد من لفظ يدل على الموضوع ولفظ يدل على المحمول، فلا بد من لفظ يدل على النسبة الحاصلة لهما الجاعلة لأحدهما موضوعاً وللآخر محمولاً وهو المسمى بـ (الرابطة) لكن منه ما يدل على نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع مطلقاً من غير تقييد بزمان معين ومنه ما يدل على نسبته إليه في زمان معين، فالأول هو قالب الاسم المضمر كـ (هو) في قولك: الإنسان هو حيوان والثاني ما هو في قالب الكلمات الوجودية كـ (كان) ويكون وكائن ووجد ويوجد في قولك: (زيد كان قائماً) زيد يكون قائماً زيد يوجد (قائماً زيد) وجد قائماً. لكن ربما اكتفي في بعض اللغات كلغة العرب بفهم الذهن لهذا الارتباط والنسبة عن التصريح بما يدل عليها فإذن لا بد من تعقل النسبة أو التصريح بما يدل عليها، وإن كان المحمول كلمة أو عرضاً من الأعراض كما في قولك: زيد قام أو زيد ذو سواد فإن العرض وإن استدعى لذاته موضوعاً يضاف إليه، وكذلك الكلمة فليس يجب أن يكون ما وقع بطريق التعيين مذكوراً معه دون النظر إلى الرابطة، فإذن القضية الحَمْلية من حيث كذلك لكان نظر إلى وجودها في التعقل، فلا يتم إلا بحصول معنى الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة الحاصلة لهما في الذهن وإن نظر إلى وجودها في اللفظ فإنما يتم بذكر الموضوع والمحمول والرابطة، إن لم يكن ما دلت عليه من النسبة
We say: Since it has been known that the categorical is that which is among assertive statements, not [such that] it holds for each of the two parts of the predicative relation that occurs in its part? — and it is that in which one judges something of something, either it is it or it is not it — therefore it necessarily has two terms, but they are insufficient. For if you say 'Human animal' it is not an assertive statement. Rather, there must be a relation occurring between them that necessitates making one of them the judged-upon and the other the judged-by, as in your saying 'Human is animal' or 'is not'. And it is not the case that for each one its relation to the other is the other's relation to it; rather, the relation of each one to the other is different from the other's relation to it. Therefore, the direction of the proposition's relation differs upon conversion, as will come in its definition. So the judgment of relating the subject to the predicate is called 'position' (waḍ‘), and therefore the subject is called 'subject' (mawḍū‘); and the judgment of relating the predicate to the subject is called 'predication' (ḥaml), and therefore what has it is called 'predicate' (maḥmūl), and what has it among assertive statements is called 'categorical' (ḥamlī). However, the application of the term 'judgment' and 'predication' is primarily for the affirmative relation, and secondarily for the negative relation, for every negation that the mind judges necessarily requires first the estimation of an affirmative relation, since negation is absence of affirmation, not that affirmation is absence of negation. And understanding the absence of something depends on prior understanding of that thing, but understanding it does not depend on prior understanding of its absence. So negation is the negation of affirmation, not that affirmation is an affirmation of negation, otherwise the affirmative would be negative, nor negation of negation, otherwise affirmation would be absence of negation. And what is in it is not hidden. Based on this, the application of the term 'subject' — which is that which is judged upon by something else as being it or not being it — and the term 'predicate' — which is that which is judged upon other as being it or not being it — and the term 'categorical' — which is the proposition consisting of them — is primarily for what the term 'judgment' and 'predication' apply to, according to the relation occurring between them primarily, which is the affirmative relation. And it is not sufficient for realizing this relation and making one of the two things subject and the other predicate that one precedes the other in utterance, otherwise our saying 'Human animal' would be an assertive statement. Rather, just as there must be a term indicating the subject and a term indicating the predicate, so too there must be a term indicating the relation occurring between them that makes one subject and the other predicate — which is called the 'copula' (rābiṭa). But some of it indicates the relation of the predicate to the subject absolutely without restriction to a specific time, and some of it indicates its relation to the subject in a specific time. The first is the form of the implicit pronoun like 'huwa' in your saying 'al-insānu huwa ḥayawān', and the second is what is in the form of existential words like 'kāna', 'yakūnu', 'kā’in', 'wajada', 'yūjadu' in your saying 'Zayd kāna qā’iman', 'Zayd yakūnu qā’iman', 'Zayd yūjadu qā’iman', 'Zayd wajada qā’iman'. But sometimes in some languages like Arabic it suffices with the mind's understanding of this connection and relation without explicitly stating what indicates it. So it is necessary to either conceive the relation or explicitly state what indicates it, even if the predicate is a verb or an accident, as in your saying 'Zayd qāma' or 'Zayd dhū sawād', for the accident, although it requires in itself a subject to which it is attributed, and likewise the verb, so it is not necessary that what occurs by way of specification is mentioned with it without consideration of the copula. So the categorical proposition, as such, if one considers its existence in conception, is not complete except by the attainment of the meaning of the subject, the predicate, and the relation occurring between them in the mind. And if one considers its existence in utterance, then it is only completed by mentioning the subject, the predicate, and the copula, unless what is indicated by the relation...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage explains the necessary components of a categorical proposition: the subject (mawḍū‘), the predicate (maḥmūl), and the relation (nisba) between them, which is expressed by a copula (rābiṭa). The author begins by stating that a categorical proposition, as defined earlier (a statement that predicates something of something), necessarily requires two terms, but those two terms alone are insufficient — simply saying 'Human animal' is not a complete assertive statement. There must be a relation between them that makes one the subject (maḥkūm ‘alayh) and the other the predicate (maḥkūm bih). The author distinguishes between the judgment of relating the subject to the predicate (called 'position' waḍ‘) and the judgment of relating the predicate to the subject (called 'predication' ḥaml). The term 'categorical' (ḥamlī) derives from the latter. The discussion then turns to the priority of affirmative over negative judgments: negation is understood only after affirmation, so the terms 'subject', 'predicate', and 'categorical' are primarily applied to affirmative propositions. The author then emphasizes that the mere order of words is not enough; a copula is needed to indicate the relation. Two types of copula are described: one timeless (like the pronoun 'huwa' in 'al-insānu huwa ḥayawān') and one time-bound (like 'kāna', 'yakūnu', etc.). The author notes that in Arabic, the copula may be omitted (e.g., 'Zayd qā'im' where the relation is understood). Even when the predicate is a verb or an accident, the relation must still be conceived. Thus, in the mind, a categorical proposition requires understanding the subject, predicate, and relation; in speech, it requires mentioning them explicitly unless the relation is inferred. This analysis is fundamental to understanding how propositions are formed and evaluated, similar to how in grammar the concept of 'isnād' (attribution) is essential for a complete sentence.

محمول
رابطة
وضع
حمل

Checkpoint
In the expression 'الإنسان حيوان' (Human is animal), which elements are present?
A) Subject and predicate only
B) Subject, predicate, and explicit copula
C) Subject, predicate, and implicit relation
D) Only two words with no logical relation

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition requires a subject, a predicate, and a relation (explicit or implicit) that connects them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the Arabic phrase 'الصلاة واجبة' (Prayer is obligatory). Identify the subject, predicate, and the implicit relation. Explain why 'الصلاة' alone and 'واجبة' alone do not form a proposition, but together with the understood copula they do.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Copula (rābiṭa): Types and Omission
Author describes the copula and its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the timeless copula (huwa) and the temporal copula (kāna, etc.), and identify when a copula can be omitted.

Core Idea
The copula is the linguistic marker of the relation; it can be a pronoun or a temporal verb, and in Arabic it is often understood.

The author introduces the term 'rābiṭa' as the third necessary linguistic element. He explains that there are two forms: one timeless (like 'huwa') and one time-bound (like 'kāna'). He also notes that in Arabic, the copula can be omitted because the mind grasps the relation. This flexibility is crucial for understanding how Arabic propositions work in logic and in Islamic textual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula in an example
Look at 'al-insānu huwa ḥayawān'. The word 'huwa' is the copula. It indicates the relation without time reference.
This is a timeless copula.
2
Identify a temporal copula
In 'Zayd kāna qā’iman', the word 'kāna' is the copula, indicating that the relation held in the past.
Other temporal copulas include 'yakūnu' (future) and 'wajada' (existential).
3
Recognize omission of copula
In Arabic, a sentence like 'Zayd qā’im' is a complete proposition without an explicit copula. The mind supplies the relation.
The author says sometimes it suffices to understand the relation mentally.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
In 'البيع صحيح' (The sale is valid), the copula is omitted but understood. This is typical of Arabic nominal sentences used in legal rulings.
Even when the predicate is a verb ('Zayd qāma'), the relation is implicit.

الإيجاب، وليس يكفي في تحقيق هذه النسبة وجعل أحد الأمرين موضوعاً، والآخر محمولاً تقدم أحدهما على الآخر في النطق وإلا كان قولنا الإنسان الحيوان قولاً جازماً، بل وكما أنه لا بد من لفظ يدل على الموضوع قائماً) زيد يكون قائماً زيد يوجد (قائماً زيد) وجد قائماً. لكن ربما اكتفي في بعض اللغات كلغة العرب بفهم الذهن لهذا الارتباط والنسبة عن التصريح بما يدل عليها فإذن لا بد من تعقل النسبة أو التصريح بما يدل عليها، وإن كان المحمول كلمة أو عرضاً من الأعراض كما في قولك: زيد قام أو زيد ذو سواد فإن العرض وإن استدعى لذاته موضوعاً يضاف إليه، وكذلك الكلمة فليس يجب أن يكون ما وقع بطريق التعيين مذكوراً معه دون النظر إلى الرابطة، فإذن القضية الحَمْلية من حيث كذلك لكان نظر إلى وجودها في التعقل، فلا يتم إلا بحصول معنى الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة الحاصلة لهما في الذهن وإن نظر إلى وجودها في اللفظ فإنما يتم بذكر الموضوع والمحمول والرابطة، إن لم يكن ما دلت عليه من النسبة
We say: Since it has been known that the categorical is that which is among assertive statements, not [such that] it holds for each of the two parts of the predicative relation that occurs in its part? — and it is that in which one judges something of something, either it is it or it is not it — therefore it necessarily has two terms, but they are insufficient. For if you say 'Human animal' it is not an assertive statement. Rather, there must be a relation occurring between them that necessitates making one of them the judged-upon and the other the judged-by, as in your saying 'Human is animal' or 'is not'. And it is not the case that for each one its relation to the other is the other's relation to it; rather, the relation of each one to the other is different from the other's relation to it. Therefore, the direction of the proposition's relation differs upon conversion, as will come in its definition. So the judgment of relating the subject to the predicate is called 'position' (waḍ‘), and therefore the subject is called 'subject' (mawḍū‘); and the judgment of relating the predicate to the subject is called 'predication' (ḥaml), and therefore what has it is called 'predicate' (maḥmūl), and what has it among assertive statements is called 'categorical' (ḥamlī). However, the application of the term 'judgment' and 'predication' is primarily for the affirmative relation, and secondarily for the negative relation, for every negation that the mind judges necessarily requires first the estimation of an affirmative relation, since negation is absence of affirmation, not that affirmation is absence of negation. And understanding the absence of something depends on prior understanding of that thing, but understanding it does not depend on prior understanding of its absence. So negation is the negation of affirmation, not that affirmation is an affirmation of negation, otherwise the affirmative would be negative, nor negation of negation, otherwise affirmation would be absence of negation. And what is in it is not hidden. Based on this, the application of the term 'subject' — which is that which is judged upon by something else as being it or not being it — and the term 'predicate' — which is that which is judged upon other as being it or not being it — and the term 'categorical' — which is the proposition consisting of them — is primarily for what the term 'judgment' and 'predication' apply to, according to the relation occurring between them primarily, which is the affirmative relation. And it is not sufficient for realizing this relation and making one of the two things subject and the other predicate that one precedes the other in utterance, otherwise our saying 'Human animal' would be an assertive statement. Rather, just as there must be a term indicating the subject and a term indicating the predicate, so too there must be a term indicating the relation occurring between them that makes one subject and the other predicate — which is called the 'copula' (rābiṭa). But some of it indicates the relation of the predicate to the subject absolutely without restriction to a specific time, and some of it indicates its relation to the subject in a specific time. The first is the form of the implicit pronoun like 'huwa' in your saying 'al-insānu huwa ḥayawān', and the second is what is in the form of existential words like 'kāna', 'yakūnu', 'kā’in', 'wajada', 'yūjadu' in your saying 'Zayd kāna qā’iman', 'Zayd yakūnu qā’iman', 'Zayd yūjadu qā’iman', 'Zayd wajada qā’iman'. And if one considers its existence in utterance, then it is only completed by mentioning the subject, the predicate, and the copula, unless what is indicated by the relation...

Commentary
This passage explains the necessary components of a categorical proposition: the subject (mawḍū‘), the predicate (maḥmūl), and the relation (nisba) between them, which is expressed by a copula (rābiṭa). The author begins by stating that a categorical proposition, as defined earlier (a statement that predicates something of something), necessarily requires two terms, but those two terms alone are insufficient — simply saying 'Human animal' is not a complete assertive statement. The term 'categorical' (ḥamlī) derives from the latter. The Primacy of Affirmative Propositions
Author argues for the primacy of affirmation over negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why affirmative propositions are logically prior to negative ones and apply this principle in evaluating definitions.

Core Idea
Negation is defined as absence of affirmation; understanding 'is not' depends on understanding 'is'.

The author makes an important epistemological point: negation is understood only after affirmation. This means that the terms 'subject', 'predicate', and 'categorical' primarily apply to affirmative propositions. This principle has implications for how we define terms and how we form logical judgments. In Islamic theology and jurisprudence, it is crucial to first affirm what something is before negating what it is not.

Guided Path
1
Consider a negative proposition
Take 'Zayd is not standing'. To understand this, you must first know what 'standing' means. The negation is secondary.
2
Analyze author's argument
The author says: negation is absence of affirmation, not the other way around. Understanding absence depends on prior understanding of presence.
He gives a brief proof: if affirmation were absence of negation, then every affirmation would be a double negation, which is absurd.
3
Apply to logical terms
Thus, the terms 'subject', 'predicate', and 'categorical' are primarily used for affirmative propositions. Negative propositions are derived.
4
Apply to Islamic theology
When defining Allah's attributes, theologians first affirm positive attributes (knowledge, power) then negate deficiencies (ignorance, weakness). This follows the logical priority.
A proper definition should start with what something is, not merely what it is not.

فنقول: وإذ قد عرف أن الحَمْلي هو ما كان من الأقوال الجازمة ليس ولا لواحد من جزأي النسبة الخبرية الحاصلة الجزئه، وهو ما يحكم فيه بشيء على شيء هو أو ليس هو فلا بد فيه من لفظين وهما غير كافيين فإنك لو والحكم بنسبة المحكوم به إلى المحكوم عليه تسمى (حَمْلاً)، ولذلك سمي ما هي له محمولاً) وما هي له من الأقوال الجازمة (حَمْلياً)، لكن إطلاق اسم الحكم والحمل إنما هو للنسبة الإيجابية أولاً، وللنسبة السلبية لعدمه، فالسلب هو سلب الإيجاب وليس الإيجاب إيجاباً للسلب، وإلا كانت الموجبة سالبة، ولا سلب السلب، وإلا كان الإيجاب هو عدم السلب. ولا يخفى ما فيه، وعلى هذا فإطلاق اسم الموضوع وهو ما يحكم عليه بشيء آخر أنه هو أو ليس هو هو، واسم المحمول وهو ما يحكم به على غيره أنه هو أو ليس هو هو، واسم الحَمْلية وهي القضية الكائنة منهما إنما هو أولاً لما اسم الحكم والحمل على ما النسبة الواقعة بينهما أولاً وهي نسبة الإيجاب، وليس يكفي في تحقيق هذه النسبة وجعل أحد الأمرين موضوعاً، والآخر محمولاً تقدم أحدهما على الآخر في النطق وإلا كان قولنا الإنسان الحيوان قولاً جازماً، بل وكما أنه لا بد من لفظ يدل على الموضوع ولفظ يدل على المحمول، فلا بد من لفظ يدل على النسبة الحاصلة لهما الجاعلة لأحدهما موضوعاً وللآخر محمولاً وهو المسمى بـ (الرابطة) لكن منه ما يدل على نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع مطلقاً من غير تقييد بزمان معين ومنه ما يدل على نسبته إليه في زمان معين، فالأول هو قالب الاسم المضمر كـ (هو) في قولك: الإنسان هو حيوان والثاني ما هو في قالب الكلمات الوجودية كـ (كان) ويكون وكائن ووجد ويوجد في قولك: (زيد كان قائماً) زيد يكون قائماً زيد يوجد (قائماً زيد) وجد قائماً. لكن ربما اكتفي في بعض اللغات كلغة العرب بفهم الذهن لهذا الارتباط والنسبة عن التصريح بما يدل عليها فإذن لا بد من تعقل النسبة أو التصريح بما يدل عليها، وإن كان المحمول كلمة أو عرضاً من الأعراض كما في قولك: زيد قام أو زيد ذو سواد فإن العرض وإن استدعى لذاته موضوعاً يضاف إليه، وكذلك الكلمة فليس يجب أن يكون ما وقع بطريق التعيين مذكوراً معه دون النظر إلى الرابطة، فإذن القضية الحَمْلية من حيث كذلك لكان نظر إلى وجودها في التعقل، فلا يتم إلا بحصول معنى الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة الحاصلة لهما في الذهن وإن نظر إلى وجودها في اللفظ فإنما يتم بذكر الموضوع والمحمول والرابطة، إن لم يكن ما دلت عليه من النسبة
We say: Since it has been known that the categorical is that which is among assertive statements, not [such that] it holds for each of the two parts of the predicative relation that occurs in its part? — and it is that in which one judges something of something, either it is it or it is not it — therefore it necessarily has two terms, but they are insufficient. For if you say 'Human animal' it is not an assertive statement. Rather, there must be a relation occurring between them that necessitates making one of them the judged-upon and the other the judged-by, as in your saying 'Human is animal' or 'is not'. And it is not the case that for each one its relation to the other is the other's relation to it; rather, the relation of each one to the other is different from the other's relation to it. Therefore, the direction of the proposition's relation differs upon conversion, as will come in its definition. So the judgment of relating the subject to the predicate is called 'position' (waḍ‘), and therefore the subject is called 'subject' (mawḍū‘); and the judgment of relating the predicate to the subject is called 'predication' (ḥaml), and therefore what has it is called 'predicate' (maḥmūl), and what has it among assertive statements is called 'categorical' (ḥamlī). However, the application of the term 'judgment' and 'predication' is primarily for the affirmative relation, and secondarily for the negative relation, for every negation that the mind judges necessarily requires first the estimation of an affirmative relation, since negation is absence of affirmation, not that affirmation is absence of negation. And understanding the absence of something depends on prior understanding of that thing, but understanding it does not depend on prior understanding of its absence. So negation is the negation of affirmation, not that affirmation is an affirmation of negation, otherwise the affirmative would be negative, nor negation of negation, otherwise affirmation would be absence of negation. And what is in it is not hidden. Based on this, the application of the term 'subject' — which is that which is judged upon by something else as being it or not being it — and the term 'predicate' — which is that which is judged upon other as being it or not being it — and the term 'categorical' — which is the proposition consisting of them — is primarily for what the term 'judgment' and 'predication' apply to, according to the relation occurring between them primarily, which is the affirmative relation. And it is not sufficient for realizing this relation and making one of the two things subject and the other predicate that one precedes the other in utterance, otherwise our saying 'Human animal' would be an assertive statement. Rather, just as there must be a term indicating the subject and a term indicating the predicate, so too there must be a term indicating the relation occurring between them that makes one subject and the other predicate — which is called the 'copula' (rābiṭa). But some of it indicates the relation of the predicate to the subject absolutely without restriction to a specific time, and some of it indicates its relation to the subject in a specific time. The first is the form of the implicit pronoun like 'huwa' in your saying 'al-insānu huwa ḥayawān', and the second is what is in the form of existential words like 'kāna', 'yakūnu', 'kā’in', 'wajada', 'yūjadu' in your saying 'Zayd kāna qā’iman', 'Zayd yakūnu qā’iman', 'Zayd yūjadu qā’iman', 'Zayd wajada qā’iman'. But sometimes in some languages like Arabic it suffices with the mind's understanding of this connection and relation without explicitly stating what indicates it. So it is necessary to either conceive the relation or explicitly state what indicates it, even if the predicate is a verb or an accident, as in your saying 'Zayd qāma' or 'Zayd dhū sawād', for the accident, although it requires in itself a subject to which it is attributed, and likewise the verb, so it is not necessary that what occurs by way of specification is mentioned with it without consideration of the copula. So the categorical proposition, as such, if one considers its existence in conception, is not complete except by the attainment of the meaning of the subject, the predicate, and the relation occurring between them in the mind. And if one considers its existence in utterance, then it is only completed by mentioning the subject, the predicate, and the copula, unless what is indicated by the relation...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage explains the necessary components of a categorical proposition: the subject (mawḍū‘), the predicate (maḥmūl), and the relation (nisba) between them, which is expressed by a copula (rābiṭa). The author begins by stating that a categorical proposition, as defined earlier (a statement that predicates something of something), necessarily requires two terms, but those two terms alone are insufficient — simply saying 'Human animal' is not a complete assertive statement. There must be a relation between them that makes one the subject (maḥkūm ‘alayh) and the other the predicate (maḥkūm bih). The author distinguishes between the judgment of relating the subject to the predicate (called 'position' waḍ‘) and the judgment of relating the predicate to the subject (called 'predication' ḥaml). The term 'categorical' (ḥamlī) derives from the latter. The discussion then turns to the priority of affirmative over negative judgments: negation is understood only after affirmation, so the terms 'subject', 'predicate', and 'categorical' are primarily applied to affirmative propositions. The author then emphasizes that the mere order of words is not enough; a copula is needed to indicate the relation. Two types of copula are described: one timeless (like the pronoun 'huwa' in 'al-insānu huwa ḥayawān') and one time-bound (like 'kāna', 'yakūnu', etc.). The author notes that in Arabic, the copula may be omitted (e.g., 'Zayd qā'im' where the relation is understood). Even when the predicate is a verb or an accident, the relation must still be conceived. Thus, in the mind, a categorical proposition requires understanding the subject, predicate, and relation; in speech, it requires mentioning them explicitly unless the relation is inferred. This analysis is fundamental to understanding how propositions are formed and evaluated, similar to how in grammar the concept of 'isnād' (attribution) is essential for a complete sentence.

Key Takeaway
Affirmation is logically prior to negation; therefore, the basic elements of logic (subject, predicate, categorical) are first defined in terms of affirmative propositions.

Propositions

Position and Variation of the Copula in Categorical Propositions
Author discusses copula position and critiques negation-based identity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify permissible variations in copula position in Arabic categorical propositions and evaluate the flawed argument that sameness of negatives proves sameness of affirmatives.

Core Idea
The copula can shift positions without changing meaning, but identical negations do not prove identical affirmatives.

In this passage, the author explains that while the natural position of the copula is between subject and predicate, conventional usage allows it to be placed before the subject or after the predicate. He then critiques a sophisticated argument that attempts to prove the identity of two propositional forms by claiming their negations are identical. This lesson will help you understand these word order variations and recognize a common logical fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural position of the copula
The copula (e.g., 'yujadu') naturally comes between subject and predicate, as in 'al-insanu yujadu 'adlan'. This is the standard order.
2
Recognize permissible variations
The copula can be placed before the subject ('yujadu al-insanu 'adlan') or after the predicate ('al-insana 'adlan yujadu') without changing meaning. Subject and predicate can also be reordered, as in 'yujadu 'adlan al-insanu'.
These shifts are allowed by convention (al-ta'aruf), not by logical necessity.
3
Understand the proposed argument from negation unity
Someone might argue that since the negation of 'yujadu al-insanu 'adlan' is 'laysa yujadu al-insanu 'adlan', which is the same as the negation of 'yujadu 'adlan al-insanu', the two affirmatives must also be identical.
4
Analyze why the argument fails
The author rejects this reasoning: if the affirmatives are already in doubt, their negations are even more unclear. Sameness of negatives does not guarantee sameness of affirmatives because negation can obscure differences.
The principle: 'what is known by what it is from, not by what is like it or more obscure.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابطة وإن كان موضوعها الطبيعي التوسط بين الموضوع والمحمول كقولنا: (الإنسان يوجد عدلاً) فقد يعدل عنه بحسب التعارف من غير تأثير في المعنى، بأن يُذكر تارة قبل الموضوع كقولنا : (يوجد الإنسان عدلاً) وتارة بعد المحمول كقولنا: الإنسان عدلاً (يوجد وكذا أيضاً قد يعدل بالموضوع والمحمول غير موضوعه وإن كان طبيعياً بحسب التعارف من غير اختلال في المعنى، كما في قولنا: (يوجد عدلاً الإنسان) وإن كان الواجب نظراً إلى ما يقتضي كل واحد من المفردين هاهنا من الموضع الطبيعي أن يقال الإنسان يوجد عدلاً) لكنه مما لا يختلف المعنى بحسب التعارف، وربما تكلّف بيان اتحاد معناهما بأن سلبهما واحد فإن سلب (يوجد الإنسان عدلاً) ليس يوجد الإنسان عدلاً، وهو بعينه سلب (يوجد عدلاً الإنسان) فإنا إنما نسلب فيهما العدالة عن الإنسان، واتحاد السالبتين يدل على الموجبتين وليس بحق، فإنه إذا أشكل اتحاد الموجبتين هاهنا فالسلب أولى بالإشكال، والتي يعرف بما هو منه لا بما هو مثله أو أخفى
And the copula, although its natural position is between the subject and the predicate, as in our saying: 'al-insanu yujadu 'adlan' (the man exists just), may be shifted according to convention without effect on the meaning, by being mentioned sometimes before the subject, as in our saying: 'yujadu al-insanu 'adlan' (exists the man just), and sometimes after the predicate, as in our saying: 'al-insana 'adlan yujadu' (the man just exists). Similarly, the subject and predicate may also be shifted from their natural position according to convention without disruption to the meaning, as in our saying: 'yujadu 'adlan al-insanu' (exists just the man). Although the obligatory [order], considering what each of the singular terms here requires from the natural position, is that one should say 'al-insanu yujadu 'adlan', it does not differ in meaning according to convention. And perhaps one may strain to show the unity of their meanings by [claiming] that their negations are one. For the negation of 'yujadu al-insanu 'adlan' is 'laysa yujadu al-insanu 'adlan', and it is exactly the negation of 'yujadu 'adlan al-insanu'. For we negate in both of them the attribute of justice from the man. And the unity of the two negatives indicates the unity of the two affirmatives, but it is not correct. For if the unity of the two affirmatives is problematic here, then the negation is more deserving of being problematic. And that which is known by what it is from, not by what is like it or more obscure.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous discussion by examining the permissible variations in word order of a categorical proposition that includes an explicit copula. The author states that the natural position of the copula is between the subject and predicate, as in 'al-insanu yujadu 'adlan' (the man exists just). However, conventional usage allows the copula to be placed before the subject or after the predicate without changing the meaning. Similarly, the subject and predicate themselves can be rearranged. Despite the natural order, these variations are accepted. The author then critiques a potential argument that attempts to prove the identity of the two propositional forms (with different word orders) by claiming that their negations are identical. For example, the negation of 'yujadu al-insanu 'adlan' is 'laysa yujadu al-insanu 'adlan', which is the same as the negation of 'yujadu 'adlan al-insanu'. The proponent argues that since the negatives are the same, the affirmatives must also be the same. The author rejects this reasoning, stating that if the unity of the affirmatives is in doubt, the unity of the negatives is even more questionable. The passage ends with a general principle: something should be known by what it is from (its cause), not by what is like it or more obscure. This is a logical caution against using obscure equivalences.

Key Terms

التعارف
السلب

Key Takeaway
Permissible word order variations do not affect meaning, but inferring propositional identity from identical negations is a logical error.

Since the negations are identical, the two affirmatives must be identical.' Is this argument valid? Explain why or why not, referencing the author's critique.

Propositions

Three-Part and Two-Part Categorical Propositions
Author distinguishes three-part and two-part propositions and discusses copula placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is three-part (with explicit copula) or two-part (without), and recognize permissible word order variations that do not affect logical meaning.

Core Idea
The copula's presence defines the form; its position does not affect meaning.

In this lesson, we examine one of the most fundamental distinctions in categorical propositions: whether the copula (الرابطة) is stated explicitly or not. Understanding this helps you parse propositions correctly in Islamic logical texts. The author also shows that word order flexibility does not change the logical content—a crucial insight for reading arguments in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the three components of a categorical proposition
A categorical proposition has a subject (موضوع), a predicate (محمول), and a copula (رابطة) that links them. The copula is often a form of 'exists' (يوجد) in Arabic. Check if the copula is explicitly mentioned in the sentence.
If the copula is present, the proposition is three-part; if not, it is two-part.
2
2. Determine the proposition type
If you see a word like 'yujadu' (exists) explicitly, the proposition is three-part (ثلاثية). If there is no such word, it is two-part (ثنائية). For example, 'al-insaanu 'aadilun' (Man just) is two-part; 'al-insaanu yujadu 'aadilan' (Man exists just) is three-part.
Even in two-part propositions, the copula is implicitly understood.
3
3. Check for word order changes
The author explains that the copula's natural position is between subject and predicate, but it may be placed before the subject or after the predicate without changing the meaning. Also, the subject and predicate themselves may be reordered. Test if the resulting proposition is still logically equivalent to the original.
For example, 'yujadu al-insaanu 'aadilan' (Exists man just) is still a three-part proposition with the same meaning as 'al-insaanu yujadu 'aadilan'.
4
4. Evaluate the argument about negation unity
The author mentions a flawed attempt to prove meaning equivalence by noting that negations of two word orders are identical. He points out that if the affirmatives are problematic, the negations are even more problematic. Reflect on why this argument fails: it assumes that identity of negations implies identity of affirmatives, which is not always true.
This step is optional for parsing but deepens your understanding of logical equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية. معقولاً، فإن صرح بالرابطة سميت القضية ثلاثية وإلا فثنائية، والرابطة وإن كان موضوعها الطبيعي التوسط بين الموضوع والمحمول كقولنا: (الإنسان يوجد عدلاً) فقد يعدل عنه بحسب التعارف من غير تأثير في المعنى، بأن يُذكر تارة قبل الموضوع كقولنا : (يوجد الإنسان عدلاً) وتارة بعد المحمول كقولنا: الإنسان عدلاً (يوجد وكذا أيضاً قد يعدل بالموضوع والمحمول غير موضوعه وإن كان طبيعياً بحسب التعارف من غير اختلال في المعنى، كما في قولنا: (يوجد عدلاً الإنسان) وإن كان الواجب نظراً إلى ما يقتضي كل واحد من المفردين هاهنا من الموضع الطبيعي أن يقال الإنسان يوجد عدلاً) لكنه مما لا يختلف المعنى بحسب التعارف، وربما تكلّف بيان اتحاد معناهما بأن سلبهما ،واحد فإن سُلب (يوجد الإنسان عدلاً ) ليس يوجد الإنسان عدلاً، وهو بعينه سلب (يوجد عدلاً الإنسان فإنا إنما نسلب فيهما العدالة عن الإنسان، واتحاد السالبتين يدل على الموجبتين وليس بحق، فإنه إذا أشكل اتحاد الموجبتين هاهنا فالسلب أولى بالإشكال.
The first art: On categorical propositions. If the copula is explicitly stated, the proposition is called three-part; otherwise it is two-part. The copula, although its natural position is between subject and predicate as in our saying: 'Man is just', may be altered according to common usage without affecting the meaning, by sometimes being mentioned before the subject as in our saying: 'Exists man just', and sometimes after the predicate as in our saying: 'Man just exists'. Likewise, the subject and predicate may be moved from their natural position according to common usage without disturbing the meaning, as in our saying: 'Exists just man', although the requirement considering the natural position of each of the two terms here is to say 'Man exists just', but the meaning does not differ according to common usage. Perhaps the unity of their meanings is established by [arguing] that their negations are one: for the negation of 'Exists man just' is 'Not exists man just', and that is exactly the negation of 'Exists just man', since we negate justice from man in both. The unity of the two negations indicates the unity of the two affirmatives, but this is not correct, because if the unity of the two affirmatives is problematic here, the negation is more problematic.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of categorical propositions: three-part (with explicit copula) and two-part (without explicit copula). The author explains that although the copula's natural position is between subject and predicate, it can be placed before the subject or after the predicate without changing the meaning, as in Arabic examples like 'al-insaan yujadu adlan' (Man exists just) versus 'yujadu al-insaan adlan' (Exists man just). Similarly, the subject and predicate themselves can be reordered. The author then critiques a proposed argument that the unity of meaning can be proven by the fact that the negations of the two orderings are identical (both negate justice from man), noting that if the affirmatives themselves are unclear, the negations are even more problematic. This discussion highlights the importance of logical structure over surface syntax.

Key Terms
الرابطة
القضية الثلاثية
القضية الثنائية

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are classified as three-part (with explicit copula) or two-part (without), and word order changes do not affect logical meaning.

Unity of Propositions with Compound Predicates: Restrictive Qualification
Author examines when a predicate composed of multiple terms yields a single proposition or multiple ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a predicate composed of multiple terms forms a single logical predicate via restrictive qualification, and distinguish this from genuine multiple predications.

Core Idea
Multiple grammatical terms can form one logical predicate if they combine into a single nature.

In logic, we often encounter propositions where the predicate seems to contain multiple words, like 'Zayd is a rational animal.' Is this one predication or two? The author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq examines exactly this question, distinguishing between restrictive composition (where terms qualify each other to form a unified nature) and other types of composition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing the logical structure of propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Classify by term multiplicity
Determine whether both subject and predicate consist of single terms or multiple terms. If multiple, note which side has multiplicity (subject, predicate, or both).
In our passage, the focus is on predicate multiplicity with a single subject.
2
Identify restrictive qualification
Check if the multiple terms in the predicate are joined by restrictive qualification (tarkib al-taqyid). This occurs when one term qualifies another to form a single concept, e.g., 'rational' qualifies 'animal' to yield 'rational animal' = man.
Restrictive composition is contrasted with non-restrictive, where terms do not form a single nature.
3
Test for single nature
Determine whether the restricted combination results in a single essence or nature (tabi'a wahida). If yes, the predicate is logically one, even though grammatically multiple words appear.
The author gives the example: 'Zayd is a rational animal' predicates the single nature 'man', not two separate properties.
4
Apply the rule
If a single nature emerges, the proposition is considered one. If not (e.g., 'Zayd is a writer and a poet' without unification), the proposition is multiple.
This rule helps avoid confusing grammatical multiplicity with logical multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القضية إذا كانت ثنائية أو ثلاثية إما أن يكون لفظ الموضوع والمحمول فيها متحداً أو متكثراً، فإن كان متحداً كان الموضوع متقدماً في التلفظ كقولنا: (الإنسان هو حيوان، أو متأخراً كقولنا: (الحيوان محمول على الإنسان وإن كان متكثراً فإما أن يكون ذلك في جانب المحمول مع اتحاد الموضوع أو بالعكس أو فيهما، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون ذلك التأليف والتركيب من قبيل تركيب التقييد أو ليس فإن كان من قبيل تركيب التقييد فإما أن يجتمع من الكل طبيعة واحدة أو ليس فإن اجتمع من الكل طبيعة واحدة فقد قيل إن القضية تكون واحدة، إذ المقصود ليس إلا حمل الطبيعة المتحدة الكائنة من مدلولات الألفاظ المتعددة لا حمل كل واحد منها كما يقال زيد) هو حيوان ناطق أي الحيوان الذي هو الناطق وتلك هي طبيعة الإنسان المعبر عنها بلفظ الإنسان، فالمحمول واحد لا متعدد.
Then, if the proposition is two-part or three-part, either the words of subject and predicate are single or multiple. If they are single, the subject may be advanced in pronunciation as in our saying: 'Man is an animal', or delayed as in our saying: 'Animal is predicated of man'. If they are multiple, then that may be on the side of the predicate while the subject is single, or the opposite, or both. If the first, then that composition and combination is either of the type of restrictive qualification or not. If it is of the type of restrictive qualification, then either from the whole a single nature is formed or not. If a single nature is formed from the whole, then it has been said that the proposition is single, because the intent is only the predication of the united nature formed from the significations of the multiple words, not the predication of each one of them. For example, one says: 'Zayd is a rational animal', i.e., the animal that is rational, and that is the nature of man expressed by the word 'man'. So the predicate is one, not multiple.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions based on whether the subject and predicate terms are singular or multiple. The focus is on when the predicate has multiple terms while the subject is single. The author distinguishes between two types of composition: (1) restrictive qualification (tarkib al-taqyid) and (2) non-restrictive. In restrictive qualification, if the multiple terms combine to form a single nature (like 'rational animal' forming the nature 'man'), then the proposition is considered single because the predicate is actually that unified nature, not each term separately. The example 'Zayd is a rational animal' is interpreted as predication of the nature 'man' which is one, not two separate predications 'Zayd is an animal' and 'Zayd is rational'. This is a key distinction in understanding logical unity versus grammatical multiplicity.

Key Takeaway
The unity of a proposition does not depend on word count but on whether the combination of terms yields a single nature through restrictive qualification.

When a Sentence Becomes Multiple Propositions
Author explains that if multiple predicate terms do not combine into a single nature, the sentence expresses multiple propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sentence with multiple predicate terms expresses multiple distinct propositions by checking whether the terms form a single nature.

Core Idea
A grammatically single sentence can be logically multiple propositions if the predicate terms do not unify.

In the previous lesson, we saw how restrictive combination of predicate terms can yield a single nature, making the sentence one proposition. But what happens when the predicate contains terms like 'man', 'white', and 'learned'? These belong to different categories and cannot merge into one nature. As the author explains, such a sentence actually expresses multiple propositions. This distinction is vital for precise logical analysis of compound predicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate terms
List all the words that follow the subject in the sentence. In 'Zayd is man, white, learned', the predicate terms are 'man', 'white', and 'learned'.
2
Check ability to combine into a single nature
Ask whether these terms can be restrictively combined to form a unified concept. 'Man' is a substance, 'white' is a quality, 'learned' is a quality of a different kind. They belong to different categories and definitions, so they cannot combine into one nature.
This contrasts with terms like 'rational' and 'animal' which do combine.
3
Treat each term as a separate predication
If the terms do not form a single nature, the sentence is logically multiple propositions, each with the same subject but a different predicate. Here, we get three propositions: 'Zayd is man', 'Zayd is white', 'Zayd is learned'.
This is called a qadiya mutakaththira (multiple proposition).
4
Compare with the unified case
Recall the earlier example where predicate terms did form a single nature (e.g., 'Zayd is rational animal'), which yielded one proposition. The difference hinges on whether the terms can be combined restrictively into one essence.
The author explicitly says this case is contrary to what preceded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن لم يكن من المذكور طبيعة متحدة كما إذا قيل زيد إنسان أبيض عالم)، فقد قيل إن القضية تكون متكثرة، وحاصلها يرجع إلى ثلاث قضايا وهي: زيد إنسان) (زيد أبيض) زید) (عالم) لاختلاف طبيعة العام والإنسان والأبيض، وتعذر اجتماع طبيعة واحدة منهما، بخلاف ما سلف.
But if from the mentioned [terms] there is not a single nature, as when it is said: 'Zayd is man, white, learned', then it has been said that the proposition is multiple, and its outcome returns to three propositions: 'Zayd is man', 'Zayd is white', 'Zayd is learned', due to the difference in the nature of 'learned', 'man', and 'white', and the impossibility of a single nature from them, contrary to what preceded.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage addresses the case when the predicate comprises multiple terms that do not combine into a single nature, such as 'man', 'white', and 'learned'. These terms belong to different categories and cannot form a unified essence like 'rational animal' did in the previous example. Therefore, the sentence is considered to express multiple propositions: each term is predicated separately of the subject. The author notes that this is the opposite of the earlier case where a single nature emerged. This distinction is crucial for understanding when a sentence with a complex predicate is logically one or many.

Key Takeaway

Restrictive Qualification Unifies Propositions Beyond Single Nature
Author challenges the condition of single nature for propositional unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the condition of a single nature for propositional unity and construct examples where restrictive qualification creates unity despite differing natures.

Core Idea
Restrictive qualification, not a single nature, is the key to propositional unity.

In classical logic, a proposition is said to be one if its subject and predicate are each one. But what if the predicate consists of multiple terms with different natures? Some argue that these must collapse into a single nature for the proposition to be one. The author challenges this view, showing through counterexamples that restrictive qualification can bind multiple terms into a single predicate, preserving the unity of the proposition. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications for how we analyze compound predicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target condition
The condition being critiqued is the claim that for a proposition with multiple predicate terms to be one, those terms must form a single nature (e.g., a unified essence). Find this condition in the passage.
The condition is implicit: the author argues against it by showing counterexamples.
2
Analyze the first counterexample
The author gives 'Zayd is a rational animal' as a proposition that is undeniably one, yet 'animal' and 'rational' have different natures. This shows that different natures do not automatically split a proposition.
In Islamic logic, 'rational animal' is the definition of man, so it is a classic example of a composite predicate that is accepted as one.
3
Understand restrictive qualification (taqyid)
Restrictive qualification links the multiple terms so that they describe a single restricted entity. For example, 'white, learned man' can be read as 'the man who is white and learned', which is one described entity, not multiple separate claims.
The term 'restrictive' here means that the qualifying terms limit the subject to those with the combined attributes.
4
Apply to the second example
The author says 'Zayd is the white, learned man' is a single proposition when interpreted restrictively. Confirm that this interpretation makes the predicate about 'the man who is white and learned', a single nature created by combination.
The excepted case ('except for the man who is not...) shows that the unity holds as long as the predicate is taken as a restricted whole.
5
Evaluate the critique
The author's argument effectively refutes the necessity of a single nature. The real condition for propositional unity is the possibility of a restrictive interpretation that binds the terms into one described entity.
This critique refines the earlier doctrine by relaxing the condition, aligning with how composite predicates actually function in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر فإنه وإن كانت طبيعة الإنسان والأبيض والعالم مختلفة فليس يمنع اتحاد القضية، وإلا لمنع زيد حيوان ناطق لاختلاف طبيعة الحيوان والناطق، وليس امتناع حصول طبيعة واحدة من المجموع مما يمنع أيضاً من الاتحاد، ولا حصول الطبيعة الواحدة شرطاً في اتحاد القضية مهما أمكن التقييد، فإنه لا مانع من القول يكون إذ ذاك على زيد هو الإنسان الذي هو الأبيض العالم إلا الإنسان الذي ليس بذي بياض ولا علم.
But there is consideration [objection] to this: for although the natures of man, white, and learned are different, that does not prevent the unity of the proposition; otherwise it would also prevent 'Zayd is a rational animal' due to the difference in nature of animal and rational. Moreover, the impossibility of obtaining a single nature from the aggregate does not prevent unity either; nor is the obtaining of a single nature a condition for the unity of the proposition whenever restrictive qualification is possible. For there is no obstacle to saying then: 'Zayd is the man that is the white, learned' except for the man who is not possessing whiteness or knowledge.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a critical objection to the earlier claim that a proposition with multiple predicate terms is multiple unless a single nature emerges. The author argues that even if the natures differ, the proposition can still be one if the combination is restrictive (taqyidi). For instance, 'Zayd is a rational animal' involves different natures (animal and rational) yet it is a single proposition because 'rational animal' restricts to form 'man'. By analogy, 'Zayd is the white, learned man' can be understood restrictively as 'Zayd is the man who is white and learned', which is a single predication of that restricted nature. Thus the condition of a single nature is not necessary; what matters is the possibility of restrictive interpretation.

Key Takeaway

Restrictive vs Enumerative Composition in Propositions
Author argues that a proposition with multiple predicates can be one if composition is restrictive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition with multiple predicates is one or many based on composition type.

Core Idea
A proposition's unity depends on whether predicates are combined restrictively or enumerated.

Can a single proposition have multiple predicates without becoming multiple propositions? This passage addresses that puzzle by introducing a key distinction: restrictive composition (ta'līf taqyīdī) versus enumerative composition (ta'līf ta'dudī). The author shows that the unity of a proposition hinges on the type of composition, not on whether the predicates share a single nature. Let's explore how to apply this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition with multiple predicates
Look for a proposition where the subject is attributed more than one predicate, e.g., 'Zayd is human, white, scholar.' Note whether the predicates are joined without a conjunction (like 'and') or with one.
The passage distinguishes between two ways of combining predicates: restrictive and enumerative.
2
Determine the composition type
Ask: are the predicates meant to modify each other, or are they listed independently? If one predicate restricts another (e.g., 'the rational animal'), it is restrictive. If they are listed separately (e.g., 'Zayd is animal and rational'), it is enumerative.
Restrictive composition often uses adjective-noun structure; enumerative uses conjunctions like 'and' (waw).
3
Assess unity based on intention
The author explains that if the composition is restrictive and multiplicity is not intended, the proposition is one. If enumerative, it is multiple. The unity does not require the predicates to share a single nature.
Even if the predicates have different natures (e.g., 'human', 'white', 'scholar'), restrictive composition can yield one proposition.
4
Test with examples from the passage
Practice on 'Zayd is the white scholar' (restrictive, one proposition) versus 'Zayd is animal, rational, scholar, and white' (enumerative, multiple propositions).
The author also gives the example of 'the rational animal is human' as a unitary proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زيد ناطق ولا يمتنع ذلك بسبب صلاحية ما ذكر للتقييد، وأما إن لم يكن من المذكور طبيعة متحدة كما إذا قيل زيد إنسان أبيض عالم، فقد قيل إن القضية تكون متكثرة، وحاصلها يرجع إلى ثلاث قضايا وهي: زيد إنسان، زيد أبيض، زيد عالم، لاختلاف طبيعة العالم والإنسان والأبيض، وتعذر اجتماع طبيعة واحدة منهما، بخلاف ما سلف. وفيه نظر: فإنه وإن كانت طبيعة الإنسان والأبيض والعالم مختلفة فليس يمنع اتحاد القضية، وإلا لمنع زيد حيوان ناطق لاختلاف طبيعة الحيوان والناطق، وليس امتناع حصول طبيعة واحدة من المجموع مما يمنع أيضاً من الاتحاد، ولا حصول الطبيعة الواحدة شرطاً في اتحاد القضية مهما أمكن التقييد، فإنه لا مانع من القول: يكون إذ ذاك على زيد هو الإنسان الذي هو الأبيض العالم إلا الإنسان الذي ليس بذي بياض ولا علم. وأما إن كان التأليف ليس على طريق التأليف التقييدي بل على طريق التأليف التعددي كقولنا: زيد حيوان وناطق وعالم وأبيض، فالقضية متكثرة في جانب الموضوع دون المحمول، فقد قيل إن القضية بسبب ذلك متكثرة مطلقاً، والواجب إنما هو التفصيل على ما أشرنا إليه في جانب المحمول، فإنه إن كان التأليف فيه تأليف التقييد، ولم يرد به التعدد فالقضية واحدة، وذلك كما في قولنا: الحيوان الناطق هو الإنسان، ولا فرق بين أن يكون محمولاً أو موضوعاً، وسواء كان ذلك مما يتهيأ منه طبيعة واحدة كهذا المثال أو ليس كما يقال: العالم الماشي إنسان، أي العالم الذي هو ماش هو إنسان.
Zayd is a rational being and that is not prevented due to the suitability of what was mentioned for restriction. But if there is no single nature among the mentioned items, such as when it is said 'Zayd is a human, white, scholar,' then it has been said that the proposition becomes multiple, and its outcome reduces to three propositions: Zayd is human, Zayd is white, Zayd is scholar, due to the difference in the nature of scholar, human, and white, and the impossibility of a single nature combining them, unlike the previous case. And there is consideration: for even if the natures of human, white, and scholar are different, that does not prevent the unity of the proposition; otherwise, it would prevent 'Zayd is a rational animal' due to the difference in the nature of animal and rational. Nor does the impossibility of obtaining one nature from the aggregate prevent unity, nor is the attainment of a single nature a condition for the unity of the proposition whenever restriction is possible. For there is no impediment to saying that Zayd is the human who is the white scholar, except the human who is not possessing whiteness or knowledge. And if the composition is not according to the restrictive composition but according to the enumerative composition, such as our saying 'Zayd is animal, rational, scholar, and white,' then the proposition is multiple on the side of the subject without the predicate. It has been said that the proposition is thereby absolutely multiple, but the proper course is the detail we indicated regarding the side of the predicate. For if the composition in it is restrictive and multiplicity is not intended, then the proposition is one, as in our saying 'the rational animal is human'—there is no difference whether it is predicate or subject, and whether that results in a single nature like this example or not, as is said 'the walking scholar is human,' i.e., the scholar who is walking is human.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage discusses a logical problem: whether a single proposition can have multiple predicates without becoming multiple propositions. The author begins by stating a common view: when a subject has multiple predicates with different natures (e.g., Zayd is human, white, scholar), the proposition is actually three separate propositions because the natures cannot combine into one. The author then objects (fīhi naẓar) by pointing out that even in 'Zayd is a rational animal,' the natures of 'animal' and 'rational' are different, yet we consider it one proposition because the composition is restrictive (taqyīdī). The key point is that unity of the proposition does not require a single nature; it requires that the predicates be combined in a restrictive manner, where one modifies the other. The author then contrasts this with enumerative composition (ta'dudī), where the predicates are listed separately, such as 'Zayd is animal, rational, scholar, and white,' which yields multiple propositions because each predicate is asserted independently. The author concludes with examples to illustrate that when composition is restrictive and multiplicity is not intended, the proposition is one, regardless of whether the predicates yield a single nature or not, as in 'the rational animal is human' or 'the walking scholar is human.' The passage clarifies that the decisive factor is the intention of the speaker and the type of composition, not the metaphysical unity of the predicate natures.

تأليف تعددي
قضية متكثرة

C) When the composition is restrictive (taqyīdī) and multiplicity is not intended.
D) When the subject is singular.

Key Takeaway
The unity of a proposition with multiple predicates is determined by the type of composition (restrictive vs. enumerative), not by the metaphysical unity of the predicate natures.
