Truth Conditions for Predicate and Double Quantifiers
Classification of truth conditions for predicate-only and double quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a quantifier on the predicate causes distortion and evaluate the truth of propositions with predicate quantifiers and double quantifiers.

Core Idea
Quantifiers on the predicate often make a proposition false; double quantifiers can make it necessarily false.

Beyond quantifiers on the subject, quantifiers can also attach to the predicate or both subject and predicate. The author shows how these cases lead to distortion and even necessary falsehood. Understanding these rules prevents misinterpretation of propositions in Islamic doctrinal debates.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish quantifier on predicate
Notice when the quantifier (كل, بعض, لا واحد) appears immediately before the predicate term.
2
Understand the effect of universal affirmative predicate quantifier
It makes the proposition false in all matter (necessary, impossible, possible) because it claims the subject includes every individual of the predicate.
Example: 'Man is every animal' is false in all matters.
3
Identify a common subtlety
If the quantifier is part of the predicate (e.g., 'capable of every knowledge'), it is not a predicate quantifier; the proposition is not distorted.
4
Apply rules for other predicate quantifier types
For universal negative: true in impossible, false in necessary, suspended in possible. For particular affirmative: true in necessary, false in impossible, suspended in possible. For particular negative: true in all matters.
5
Handle double quantifiers
When both subject and predicate have universal affirmative quantifiers, the proposition is false in every matter because it is contradictory.
Example: 'Every human is every animal' is false in necessary, possible, and impossible.

وموقوفة في الممكن كقولنا: لا واحد من الإنسان كاتب وإن كان السور جزئياً فإما أن يكون موجباً أو سالباً، فإن كان موجباً فالقضية صادقة في الواجب وكاذبة في الممتنع وموقوفة في الممكن. وربما قيل أنها صادقة وليس بحق؛ إذ الممكنة لا تدل على الصدق والكذب دون النظر إلى الواقع، وإن كان السور جزئياً سالباً فالقضية صادقة في الممتنع وكاذبة في الواجب وموقوفة في الممكن. وربما قيل أنها صادقة، وليس بحق لما عرف. وإن كان السور للمحمول فقط، فالقضية إما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فإما كلية وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فإما أن يكون كلياً أو جزئياً، فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب أو سالب، فإن كان كلياً موجباً فالقضية كاذبة في كل مادة، كقولنا: الإنسان هو كل حيوان الإنسان هو كل كاتب الإنسان هو كل حجر أما في الممتنع فظاهر، وأما في الواجب والممكن فلاستحالة الحكم بكل آحاد الحيوان وآحاد الكاتب على واحد من الناس. نعم، ربما لم يجعل سور المحمول داخلاً عليه، بـل عـدل بـه عـن مـوضـعـه الطبيعي، وجعل جزءاً من المحمول، وظن مع ذلك أن القضية مسورة المحمول من غير انحراف، وذلك كما يقال الإنسان قابل كل علم والسور فيه غير داخل على المحمول بل جزء منه، إذ المحمول هو [حمل] قولنا: قابل كل علم وإنما يكون سور المحمول أن لو كان داخلاً على القابل بأن يقال هو كل قابل علم، وإذ ذاك فالقضية تكون منحرفة وإلا فلا، وإن كان السور كلياً سالباً صدقت القضية في الممتنع ، كقولنا: الإنسان هو لا واحد من الحجر وكذبت في الواجب، كقولنا: الإنسان هو لا واحد من الحيوان وموقوفة في الممكن ، وإن كان السور جزئياً فإما موجب أو سالب فإن كان موجباً صدقت في الواجب وكذبت في الممتنع وموقوفة في الممكن واعتبره. وربما قيل أنها صادقة في الممكن وليس بحق لما سلف، وإن كان السور جزئياً سالباً صدقت في الجميع، كقولنا : الإنسان هو كل حيوان الإنسان هو لا كل كاتب الإنسان هو لا كل حجر، وإن كانت القضية سالبة فالسور الداخل على المحمول إن كان كلياً موجباً صدقت القضية في كل مادة، كقولنا: الإنسان ليس هو كل حيوان ليس هو كل حجر ، ليس هو كل كاتب وإن كان جزئياً موجباً كذبت في الواجب وصدقت في الممتنع وموقوفة في الممكن ، وإن كان كلياً سالباً صدقت في الواجب وكذبت في الممتنع وموقوفة في الممكن، وإن كان جزئياً سالباً صدقت القضية مطلقاً واعتبره. وأما إن كان السور للموضوع والمحمول فسور الموضوع إما أن يكون كلياً وإما أن يكون جزئياً، فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب أو سالب، فإن كان موجباً فسور المحمول إما أن يكون كلياً وإما أن يكون جزئياً، فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب أو سالب فإن كان موجباً فالقضية كاذبة في الواجب والممكن والممتنع، كقولنا: كل إنسان هو كل حيوان كل إنسان هو كاتب كل إنسان هو حجر أما في الممتنع فظاهر، وأما في الواجب والممكن فلأنه ليس كل واحد من آحاد الناس هو كل حيوان أو الكاتب.
Third chapter: On what attaches to categorical propositions of distortion and error due to quantifiers and other things, and what is thought to be distortion but is not distorted. We say: The quantifier in a categorical proposition is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the quantifier either enters upon the subject only. Then the quantifier is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, then the proposition is not distorted, but rather true in the necessary, like our saying: Every human is an animal, and false in the impossible, like our saying: Every human is a stone, and suspended in the possible, like our saying: Every human is a writer. If the quantifier is universal negative, then the proposition is true in the impossible, like our saying: Not one of humans is a stone, and false in the necessary, like our saying: Not one of humans is an animal, and suspended in the possible, like our saying: Not one of humans is a writer. If the quantifier is particular, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the proposition is true in the necessary, false in the impossible, and suspended in the possible. And perhaps it is said that it is true, but it is not right; since the possible does not indicate truth and falsehood without considering reality. If the quantifier is particular negative, then the proposition is true in the impossible, false in the necessary, and suspended in the possible. And perhaps it is said that it is true, but it is not right for what has been known. If the quantifier is for the predicate only, then the proposition is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal affirmative, then it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, then the proposition is false in every matter, like our saying: Man is every animal, man is every writer, man is every stone. As for the impossible, it is obvious; as for the necessary and possible, it is because judging every individual of animal and every individual of writer upon one person is impossible. Yes, perhaps the quantifier of the predicate is not made to enter upon it, but rather it is diverted from its natural position and made part of the predicate, and it is thought with that that the proposition is quantified in the predicate without distortion. That is as is said: Man is capable of every knowledge. The quantifier in it does not enter upon the predicate but is part of it, since the predicate is the carrier of our statement: 'capable of every knowledge'. The quantifier of the predicate would be only if it entered upon the capability, i.e., it is said: he is every capable of knowledge. Then the proposition would be distorted, otherwise not. If the quantifier is universal negative, the proposition is true in the impossible, like our saying: Man is not one of stone, and false in the necessary, like our saying: Man is not one of animal, and suspended in the possible. If the quantifier is particular, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is true in the necessary, false in the impossible, and suspended in the possible—consider it. And perhaps it is said that it is true in the possible, but it is not right for what preceded. If the quantifier is particular negative, it is true in all, like our saying: Man is every animal, man is not every writer, man is not every stone. If the proposition is negative, then the quantifier entering upon the predicate: if it is universal affirmative, the proposition is true in every matter, like our saying: Man is not every animal, is not every stone, is not every writer. If it is particular affirmative, it is false in the necessary, true in the impossible, and suspended in the possible. If it is universal negative, it is true in the necessary, false in the impossible, and suspended in the possible. If it is particular negative, the proposition is true absolutely—consider it. As for if the quantifier is for both subject and predicate: the quantifier of the subject is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the quantifier of the predicate is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the proposition is false in the necessary, possible, and impossible, like our saying: Every human is every animal, every human is a writer, every human is a stone. As for the impossible, it is obvious; as for the necessary and possible, because not every one of individuals of humans is every animal or the writer.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of how quantifiers distort categorical propositions. The author begins by stating the topic: distortion due to quantifiers. He then divides based on whether the quantifier attaches to the subject only, predicate only, or both. For quantifier on subject only, he goes through universal affirmative/negative and particular affirmative/negative, stating truth conditions for necessary, impossible, and possible matter. He notes a common mistake: some claim a particular affirmative is true in possible matter, but it is not because possibility does not guarantee truth without actual occurrence. When the quantifier is on the predicate only, he explains that a universal affirmative predicate quantifier makes the proposition false in all matter because it tries to distribute the predicate over every individual, which is impossible. He then discusses a subtle case where the quantifier appears on the predicate but is actually part of the predicate (e.g., 'man is capable of every knowledge'), which is not distortion. For quantifier on both subject and predicate, a universal affirmative on both results in a contradiction, so false always. Throughout, the author uses examples from the Islamic intellectual tradition: 'every human is an animal' (necessary), 'every human is a stone' (impossible), 'every human is a writer' (possible).

المحمول
القضية الحملية
التحريف
الواجب
الممكن
الممتنع

A) True in necessary matter
B) False in all matter
C) True in impossible matter

Key Takeaway
Predicate quantifiers, especially universal affirmative, cause distortion because they predicate the entire genus of a single subject; double quantifiers yield necessary falsehood.

?

When Does a Quantifier Count as on the Predicate?
Author distinguishes genuine predicate quantifier from deviant form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a genuine predicate quantifier and a deviant (munharif) quantifier that is merely part of the predicate term.

Core Idea
A quantifier qualifies the predicate only when it directly attaches to the predicate term.

In this passage, the author clarifies an important nuance: not every quantifier that appears near the predicate counts as a predicate-quantifier. Sometimes the quantifier is embedded within the predicate term itself, making the proposition deviant (munharif). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying truth conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
Locate the quantifier (e.g., كل, بعض, لا واحد) in the proposition. Note its position relative to the subject and predicate.
In 'Man is capable of every knowledge', the quantifier 'every' is part of the predicate 'capable of every knowledge'.
2
Check if the quantifier qualifies the predicate directly
Ask: Does the quantifier directly modify the predicate term (the thing being predicated)? If the quantifier is inside a compound predicate, it is not a true predicate quantifier.
Compare 'Man is capable of every knowledge' (deviant) vs. 'Man is every capable of knowledge' (genuine predicate quantifier).
3
Classify as deviant or genuine
If the quantifier directly qualifies the predicate term, the proposition has a true predicate quantifier. Otherwise, it is deviant (qadiyya munharifa).
The author labels 'Man is capable of every knowledge' as non-deviant because the quantifier is part of the predicate, not qualifying it.

Source Text

Yes, perhaps he does not make the quantifier of the predicate enter upon it, but rather diverts it from its natural place, and makes it a part of the predicate, and thinks nonetheless that the proposition is quantifier-of-predicate without deviation, as when it is said: 'Man is capable of every knowledge', and the quantifier therein does not enter upon the predicate but is part of it, since the predicate is [carrying] our saying: 'capable of every knowledge'. The quantifier of the predicate would only be if it entered upon the capable such that it is said 'he is every capable of knowledge'. Then the proposition is deviant, otherwise not. And if the quantifier is universal negative, the proposition is true in the impossible, like our saying: 'Man is no one of stone', and false in the necessary, like our saying: 'Man is no one of animal', and suspended in the possible. And if the quantifier is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is true in the necessary, false in the impossible, and suspended in the possible; consider it. And perhaps it is said that it is true in the possible, which is not correct due to what preceded. And if the quantifier is particular negative, it is true in every matter, like our saying: 'Man is every animal', 'Man is not every writer', 'Man is not every stone'. And if the proposition is negative, then the quantifier entering upon the predicate: if it is universal affirmative, the proposition is true in every matter, like our saying: 'Man is not every animal', 'is not every stone', 'is not every writer'. If it is particular affirmative, it is false in the necessary and true in the impossible and suspended in the possible. If it is universal negative, it is true in the necessary and false in the impossible and suspended in the possible. And if it is particular negative, the proposition is true absolutely; consider it.

Commentary
This passage defines and lists truth conditions for categorical propositions where the quantifier is applied to the predicate rather than the subject. The author first clarifies a special case: when the quantifier is part of the predicate term (like 'capable of every knowledge'), the proposition is not considered deviant (munharif) unless the quantifier directly qualifies the predicate term itself (as in 'every capable of knowledge'). Then, for propositions with a quantifier on the predicate, the author enumerates truth values based on the quantifier type (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and the matter (necessary, possible, impossible). Each combination is given with an Islamic example: 'Man is no one of stone' (impossible matter) vs. 'Man is no one of animal' (necessary false). The author also corrects a misconception that particular affirmative is true in the possible. Finally, the same rules are applied to negative propositions (salibah) with quantifier on the predicate, again with examples. The repeated command 'اعتبره' (consider it) invites the student to verify the rules by testing with other examples.

Truth Values for Predicate-Quantified Propositions
Author enumerates truth conditions for propositions with quantifier on predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any categorical proposition with a quantifier on the predicate, based on its quantifier type (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and its matter (necessary, possible, impossible).

Core Idea
Truth of a predicate-quantified proposition depends on quantifier type and matter in a systematic way.

After clarifying when a quantifier truly qualifies the predicate, the author lays out detailed truth tables for all combinations of quantifier (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and proposition type (affirmative/negative) across three matters: necessary, possible, impossible. Mastering these rules is essential for evaluating logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier and its type
Determine whether the quantifier is universal (كل, لا واحد) or particular (بعض, ليس كل). Also note if it is affirmative (موجب) or negative (سالب).
In 'Man is no one of stone', the quantifier 'no one' is universal negative.
2
Determine the matter of the proposition
Classify the proposition as necessary (واجب), possible (ممكن), or impossible (ممتنع) based on what it asserts about the subject.
'Man is no one of stone' is impossible because man can be stone? Actually, it's impossible for man to be stone, so matter is impossible.
3
Apply the truth rule for the specific quantifier type
Use the author's rules: e.g., universal negative quantifier on predicate is true in impossible, false in necessary, suspended in possible.
Use the examples given to verify, like 'Man is no one of stone' (true in impossible).
4
Repeat for negative propositions if applicable
If the proposition itself is negative (e.g., 'Man is not every animal'), apply the separate rules for negative propositions with quantifier on predicate.
For negative propositions, universal affirmative quantifier is true in every matter.

Source Text

Yes, perhaps he does not make the quantifier of the predicate enter upon it, but rather diverts it from its natural place, and makes it a part of the predicate, and thinks nonetheless that the proposition is quantifier-of-predicate without deviation, as when it is said: 'Man is capable of every knowledge', and the quantifier therein does not enter upon the predicate but is part of it, since the predicate is [carrying] our saying: 'capable of every knowledge'. Truth Conditions for Universal Negative Subject
Author enumerates truth conditions when subject quantifier is universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any proposition with a universal negative subject quantifier ('no one of X') for any combination of predicate quantifier and logical matter.

Core Idea
When the subject is 'no one of X', the truth depends on the predicate's quantifier and the matter.

We have already studied propositions with single quantifiers. Now we add a quantifier to the predicate as well. When the subject is universal negative ('لا واحد من الإنسان'), the predicate's quantifier and quality decide whether the proposition is true, false, or suspended. This passage systematically lays out all four possible cases for the predicate: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative — each considered in necessary, impossible, and possible matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject quantifier
The subject is universal negative, meaning 'no one of X'. In the passage, it is expressed as 'لا واحد من الإنسان' (no one of mankind).
2
Determine the predicate quantifier and quality
The predicate quantifier is either universal or particular, and each can be affirmative or negative. This gives four combinations: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
3
Identify the logical matter
The matter is either necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), or possible (mumkin). The truth value depends on this classification.
4
Apply the truth condition from the passage
For each combination, the passage gives a ruling: e.g., universal affirmative predicate is true absolutely; universal negative predicate is true in necessary, false in impossible, and suspended in possible; particular affirmative predicate is true in impossible, false in necessary, suspended in possible; particular negative predicate is false absolutely.
Memorize the table: for subject universal negative, predicate universal affirmative -> always true; universal negative -> true in necessary, false in impossible, suspended in possible; particular affirmative -> true in impossible, false in necessary, suspended in possible; particular negative -> always false.
5
Test with the provided examples
Go through each example given: 'لا واحد من الإنسان هو كل كاتب' (universal affirmative predicate) is true; 'لا واحد من الإنسان هو لا واحد من الحيوان' (universal negative predicate) is true in necessary matter; etc. Verify that the example matches the rule.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان هو الموضوع كلياً سالباً فسور المحمول إما كلي وإما جزئي فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب وإما سالب فإن كان كلياً موجبا فالقضية صادقة على الإطلاق كقولنا (لا واحد من الإنسان هو كل كاتب، لا واحد من الإنسان هو كل حجر). وإن كان سور المحمول كلياً سالباً صدقت القضية في الواجب كقولنا (لا واحد من الإنسان لا واحد من الحيوان) معناه أنه لا يقال لواحد من الإنسان أي واحد كان إنه لا واحد من الحيوان، وكذبت في الممتنع كقولنا: (لا واحد من الإنسان هو لا واحد من الحجر). وأما في الممكن قال أفضل المتأخرين إنه كاذب إذ معناه أنه أي واحد من الناس أخذته كان موجبا عليه أنه واحد من الكتاب وهو ظاهر الكذب، وقال غيره إنه صادق، والحق في ذلك إنما هو الوقف فإنها لا تزيد على قولنا كل إنسان كاتب وهي موقوفة، ثم فكذلك ها هنا وأما إن كان سور المحمول جزئياً فإما موجب وإما سالب فإن كان جزئياً موجبا صدقت في الممتنع كقولنا: (لا واحد من الناس هو بعض الحجر) وكذبت في الواجب، كقولنا: (لا واحد من الناس هو بعض الحيوان) وموقوفة في الممكن، وإن كان سور المحمول جزئياً سالباً فالقضية كاذبة مطلقاً، كقولنا: (لا واحد من الناس هو لا كل حيوان، هو لا كل كاتب، هو لا كل حجر)؛ فإن تقديره أنه ما من واحد يفرض من آحاد الناس إلا ويكذب عليه أنه لا كل حيوان ولو كان كذلك لصدق على بعض آحاد الناس إنه كل حيوان وهو ظاهر.
And if the subject is universal negative, then the quantifier of the predicate is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, the proposition is true absolutely, like our saying: 'No one of mankind is every writer, no one of mankind is every stone'. And if the quantifier of the predicate is universal negative, the proposition is true in the necessary, like our saying: 'No one of mankind is no one of animal', meaning that it is not said of any individual man that he is no one of animal; and false in the impossible, like our saying: 'No one of mankind is no one of stone'. As for the possible, the best of the later scholars said it is false, because its meaning is that any individual you take from among people is affirmed that he is one of the writers, which is clearly false; and another said it is true. The truth in that is only suspension, for it does not exceed our saying: 'Every man is a writer', which is suspended; then likewise here. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is particular affirmative, it is true in the impossible, like our saying: 'No one of mankind is some stone', and false in the necessary, like our saying: 'No one of mankind is some animal', and suspended in the possible. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular negative, the proposition is false absolutely, like our saying: 'No one of mankind is not every animal, is not every writer, is not every stone'; for its estimation is that there is no individual assumed from among the individuals of mankind except that it is false of him that he is not every animal, and if that were so, then it would be true of some individuals that they are every animal, which is obvious.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of propositions with quantifiers on both subject and predicate, now considering the subject quantifier universal negative ('لا واحد من الإنسان' – no one of mankind). The author splits cases based on the predicate quantifier: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. For universal affirmative predicate, the proposition is true absolutely, e.g., 'No one of mankind is every writer' – this is always true because no single man is the whole set of writers. For universal negative predicate, the proposition is true in necessary matter (e.g., every individual is necessarily some animal, so 'no man is no animal' is true), false in impossible matter, and there is a dispute about possible matter: some say false, some say true, but the author opts for suspension (مَوْقوفة), analogizing it to 'Every man is a writer' which is suspended. For particular affirmative predicate, e.g., 'No one of mankind is some stone', it is true in impossible matter (since stones are not predicable of man), false in necessary, and suspended in possible. Finally, for particular negative predicate ('لا كل'), the proposition is false absolutely, as explaining the example: it would imply that every man is every animal, which is false. The author invites the reader to reflect on these logical nuances.

When subject is universal negative, the predicate quantifier and matter determine truth: universal affirmative predicate is always true, universal negative is true in necessary, false in impossible, and suspended in possible; particular affirmative is true in impossible, false in necessary, suspended in possible; particular negative is always false.

Truth Conditions for Quantified Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates truth values for propositions with various quantifier combinations on subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any categorical proposition in the three modalities by reading the quantifiers on subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Truth depends on four factors: subject quantifier, predicate quantifier, modality, and the logical relation between subject and predicate.

In this passage, the author systematically lays out the truth conditions for categorical propositions when both the subject and predicate carry quantifiers. This is a central skill in Islamic logic: you must be able to evaluate propositions used in kalam arguments, fiqh rulings, and usul al-fiqh reasoning. Let's work through the cases together.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate quantifiers
Look at the proposition: does it start with 'كل' (every), 'لا واحد' (no), or 'بعض' (some)? Also check the predicate: does it have a quantifier like 'كل' or 'بعض' or 'لا واحد'? For example, in 'كل إنسان هو بعض الحيوان', the subject quantifier is universal affirmative ('كل') and the predicate quantifier is particular affirmative ('بعض').
The subject quantifier can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The predicate quantifier similarly.
2
Classify the modality
Determine whether the predicate is necessary (واجب), impossible (ممتنع), or possible (ممكن) for the subject. Use standard examples: 'human is animal' is necessary, 'human is stone' is impossible, 'human is writer' is possible. The text uses these examples consistently.
Modality here is about the relation itself, not the entire proposition.
3
Apply the author's rule for the combination
For the given combination of subject quantifier, predicate quantifier, and modality, the author gives a truth value (true, false, or suspended). For instance, when subject is universal affirmative and predicate quantifier is particular affirmative, the proposition is true in necessary and impossible, suspended in possible. Verify with the text: 'صدقت القضية في الواجب كقولنا كل إنسان هو بعض الحيوان وكذلك في الممتنع كقولنا كل إنسان هو بعض الحجر وموقوفة في الممكن'.
The text systematically lists all combinations; refer to the passage for each case.
4
Test with your own example
Construct a proposition with the same quantifier combination but a different subject and predicate (e.g., 'Every writer is some human' – necessary? impossible? possible?). Use the same rules to predict its truth value, then check your reasoning against the pattern given by the author.
Practice makes the classification intuitive.

دقائق الحقائق: الجزء الأول / المقالة الثالثة: في بارمنياس
وإن كان سور المحمول كلياً سالباً صدقت في الممتنع، كقولنا: (كل إنسان هو لا واحد من الحجر) وكذبت في الواجب كقولنا كل إنسان هو لا واحد من الحيوان.
وفي الممكن قد قيل أنها كاذبة، والحق أنها موقوفة.
وإن كان سور المحمول جزئياً فإما موجب وأما سالب، فإن كان جزئياً موجباً صدقت القضية في الواجب كقولنا كل إنسان هو بعض الحيوان أي كل واحد من الناس هو بعض الحيوان وكذلك في الممتنع كقولنا كل إنسان هو بعض الحجر وموقوفة في الممكن وإن كان هو المحمول جزئياً سالباً صدقت القضية مطلقاً واعتبره بنفسك، وإن كان هو الموضوع كلياً سالباً فسور المحمول إما كلي وإما جزئي فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب وإم سالب فإن كان كلياً موجبا فالقضية صادقة على الإطلاق كقولنا لا واحد من الإنسان هو كل كاتب لا واحد من الإنسان هو حجر، وإن كان سور المحمول كلياً سالباً صدقت القضية في الواجب كقولنا لا واحد من الإنسان لا واحد من الحيوان معناه أنه لا يقال لواحد من الإنسان أي واحد كان إنه لا واحد من الحيوان، وكذبت في الممتنع كقولنا: (لا) واحد من الإنسان هو لا واحد لا واحد من الحجر).
وأما في الممكن قال أفضل المتأخرين إنه كاذب إذ معناه أنه أي واحد من الناس أخذته كان موجبا عليه أنه واحد من الكتاب وهو ظاهر الكذب وقال غيره إنه صادق، والحق في ذلك إنما هو الوقف فإنها لا تزيد على قولنا كل إنسان كاتب وهي موقوفة، ثم فكذلك ها هنا وأما إن كان سور المحمول جزئياً فإما موجب وإما سالب فإن كان جزئياً موجبا صدقت في الممتنع كقولنا: (لا) واحد من الناس هو بعض الحجر) وكذبت في الواجب، كقولنا : (لا) واحد من الناس بعض الحيوان وموقوفة في الممكن ، وإن كان سور المحمول جزئياً سالباً فالقضية كاذبة مطلقاً، كقولنا: (لا) واحد من الناس هو لا كل حيوان، هو لا كل كاتب، هو لا كل حجر)؛ فإن تقديره أنه ما من واحد يفرض من آحاد الناس إلا ويكذب عليه أنه (لا) كل حيوان ولو كان كذلك لصدق على بعض آحاد الناس إنه كل حيوان وهو ظاهر الكذب، وأما إن كان سور الموضوع جزئياً فإما موجب أو سالب، فإن كان موجباً فسور المحمول إما كلي وإما جزئيّ، فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب وإما سالب فإن كان كلياً موجباً فالقضية كاذبة مطلقاً، كما لو كان سور الموضوع كلياً موجباً، كقولنا: (بعض) الإنسان هو كل حيوان (بعض الإنسان هو كل كاتب بعض الإنسان هو كل حجر)، وإن كان سور المحمول كلياً سالباً فالحكم كما لو كان سور الموضوع كلياً موجباً، وإن كان سور المحمول جزئياً موجباً أو جزئياً سالباً فالحكم أيضاً كما لو كان سور الموضوع كلياً موجباً، وإن كان سور الموضوع جزئياً سالباً فالحكم أيضاً كما لو كان سوره كلياً سالباً واعتبره بنفسك.
Daqaiq Haqaiq: Part One / Article Three: On Parmenides. And if the quantifier of the predicate is universal negative, it is true in the impossible, as in our saying: 'Every human is not one of stone' and false in the necessary, as in our saying: 'Every human is not one of animal.' As for the possible, it has been said that it is false, but the truth is that it is suspended. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is particular affirmative, the proposition is true in the necessary, as in our saying: 'Every human is some animal' i.e., every person is some animal, and likewise in the impossible, as in our saying: 'Every human is some stone', and suspended in the possible. And if the predicate is particular negative, the proposition is true absolutely; consider it yourself. And if the subject is universal negative, then the quantifier of the predicate is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, the proposition is true absolutely, as in our saying: 'No human is every writer, no human is stone.' And if the quantifier of the predicate is universal negative, the proposition is true in the necessary, as in our saying: 'No human is not one of animal', meaning that it is not said of any human, whichever it is, that he is not one of animal; and it is false in the impossible, as in our saying: '(No) human is not one, not one of stone.' As for the possible, the most excellent of the later scholars said it is false because its meaning is that any person you take is affirmed that he is one of the writers, which is clearly false. And others said it is true. The truth is that it is suspended, for it is not more than our saying 'Every human is a writer', which is suspended; likewise here. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is particular affirmative, it is true in the impossible, as in our saying: '(No) person is some stone', and false in the necessary, as in our saying: '(No) person is some animal', and suspended in the possible. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular negative, the proposition is false absolutely, as in our saying: '(No) person is not every animal, is not every writer, is not every stone'; for its import is that there is no one among individuals of people except that it is false of him that he is not every animal; and if that were so, it would be true of some individuals of people that he is every animal, which is clearly false. And if the quantifier of the subject is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the quantifier of the predicate is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, the proposition is false absolutely, just as if the quantifier of the subject were universal affirmative, as in our saying: 'Some human is every animal, some human is every writer, some human is every stone.' And if the quantifier of the predicate is universal negative, the judgment is as if the quantifier of the subject were universal affirmative. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular affirmative or particular negative, the judgment is also as if the quantifier of the subject were universal affirmative. And if the quantifier of the subject is particular negative, the judgment is also as if its quantifier were universal negative; consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of the truth conditions for categorical propositions (qadaya hamliyya) based on the quantifiers (sawr) of the subject and predicate, along with the modality (necessary, impossible, possible). The author begins with the case where the subject is universal affirmative (implied by the initial context) and the predicate quantifier is universal negative: such a proposition is true only for impossible matters (like 'every human is not any stone') because it asserts a universal negative which cannot be true in necessary or possible cases. Next, when the predicate quantifier is particular affirmative, the proposition is true in the necessary and impossible, but suspended in the possible. When the predicate quantifier is particular negative, the proposition is true absolutely. The author then shifts to the case where the subject is universal negative, and exhaustively examines all combinations of predicate quantifiers (universal affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative). For each, he gives examples and explains the truth value. For instance, with subject universal negative and predicate universal negative, the proposition is true in the necessary (e.g., 'no human is not an animal' meaning every human is an animal) but false in the impossible. There is a disagreement regarding the possible modality, with the correct view being suspension. Finally, the author treats the case where the subject is particular. He explains that with subject particular affirmative, all combinations of predicate quantifiers yield false propositions, analogous to when the subject is universal affirmative. With subject particular negative, the judgment is as when the subject is universal negative. Throughout, the author uses examples from 'human', 'animal', 'stone', 'writer' to illustrate the modalities. The careful reader should verify these classifications by testing them.

Understanding Restriction with 'Innama'
Author explains how 'innama' restricts subject or predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the particle 'innama' restricts the subject or the predicate in a given Arabic proposition by analyzing the assumed prior negative proposition.

Core Idea
The particle 'innama' does not just mean 'only'—it restricts either the subject or the predicate, guided by the intended refutation.

In Islamic logic, the analysis of propositions goes beyond truth and falsehood; sometimes a proposition carries an additional layer of restriction (hasr) due to the particle 'innama'. Understanding this device is crucial for correctly interpreting statements in the Qur'an, Hadith, and scholarly debates. The key is to recognize that 'innama' always assumes a prior negative proposition, and the restriction applies either to the subject or the predicate, depending on what is being refuted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'innama' proposition
Locate the statement that contains 'innama'. For example, 'إنما أنا بشر' (I am only a human). This is the proposition under analysis.
2
Determine the intended refutation
Ask: What claim is being denied or corrected? In the example, the speaker is refuting the claim that he is a king or a god. This refutation gives context for the restriction.
The refutation is often directed against an opponent's assertion.
3
Formulate the assumed prior negative
Construct the negative proposition that logically precedes the 'innama' statement. For the example: 'I am not a king or a god' (لست ملكًا ولا إلهًا). This negative sets up the restriction.
The negative must be about the same subject or predicate that 'innama' modifies.
4
Identify what is restricted
If the subject of the negative matches the subject of the 'innama' statement, then the restriction is of the subject to the predicate. If the predicate matches, then the restriction is of the predicate to the subject. In 'إنما أنا بشر', the negative's subject 'I' is the same as the subject in 'innama'; therefore, the subject 'I' is restricted to 'human'.
The author's example 'إنما أنا الله إله' shows predicate restriction: the predicate 'god' is restricted to 'Allah'.
5
Verify meaningfulness
Check that without the assumed restriction, the statement would be meaningless. The author emphasizes that if the supposed subject were not restricted to the predicate, or vice versa, the speech would be nonsense or useless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما لحق التحريف لا من جهة الكذب بل من جهة ما يلحقها من أحكام خارجة عنها كحصر الموضوع في المحمول، والمحمول في الموضوع، أو الملازمة بينهما، أو الاتحاد لحقيقتهما بسبب ما اقترن بها من لفظة (إنما)، وإلا في محمول السالبة، أما (إنما) فإنها تفيد حصر الموضوع في المحمول تارة، وحصر المحمول في الموضوع أخرى، وذلك حسب سياق المفهوم مما يحسن تقديره من الأقوال الجازمة قبلها، فإنها إنما تحسن بعد تقدير قضية سالبة، وهي أنه ليس كذا كذا وإنما كذا كذا فإن كان المقصود منها هو الموضوع في المحمول في معرض الرد على من أوجب دخول الموضوع في غير المحمول المفروض، وكان الموضوع في القضية السالبة هو موضوع القضية الموجبة المقترن بها لفظة (إنما)، وكان المقصود إنما هو حصر المحمول في الموضوع وسالبه عما سواه في معرض الرد على من أثبته لغيره، كان محمول القضية السالبة هو محمول القضية الموجبة، فالأول كقولنا: (إنما أنا بشر) فإن الموضوع فيها إنما هو آنيته الموضوعة في القضية السالبة المقدرة وهي إني لست بملك أو إله، ليرده على من زعم أنه ملك أو إله، ولو لم يكن الموضوع الفروض منحصراً في المحمول المفروض وإلا كان الكلام هذراً. وأما الثاني فكقوله (إنما أنا الله إله) فإن المحمول فيها هو المحمول في القضية السالبة المقدرة وهي أنه ليس عيسى مثلاً إله ليرده على القائل بإلاهيته، وإذ ذاك فلو كان المحمول غير منحصر في الموضوع لما كان الكلام مفيداً.
And sometimes distortion afflicts the proposition not from the aspect of falsity but from the aspect of external judgments that attach to it, such as the restriction of the subject to the predicate, or of the predicate to the subject, or the implication between them, or the unification of their reality due to the word 'innama' that has accompanied it, except in the predicate of the negative. As for 'innama', it sometimes conveys the restriction of the subject to the predicate, and sometimes the restriction of the predicate to the subject, depending on the context of what is understood from the preceding assertive statements. For it only fits after assuming a negative proposition, namely 'it is not such and such, but such and such.' If the intended meaning is to restrict the subject to the predicate in the context of refuting someone who affirms the inclusion of the subject in something other than the supposed predicate, and the subject in the negative proposition is the subject of the affirmative proposition accompanied by 'innama', and the intended meaning is to restrict the predicate to the subject and its negative to other than it in the context of refuting someone who affirms it for other than it, then the predicate of the negative proposition is the predicate of the affirmative proposition. The first is like our saying: 'I am only a human' (innama ana bashar); for the subject in it is the 'I-ness' posited in the assumed negative proposition, namely 'I am not a king or a god', to refute someone who claims that he is a king or a god. And if the supposed subject were not restricted to the supposed predicate, the speech would be nonsense. As for the second, it is like His saying: 'I am only the god Allah' (innama ana Allahu ilah); for the predicate in it is the predicate in the assumed negative proposition, namely 'Jesus, for example, is not a god', to refute the one who asserts his divinity. And if so, if the predicate were not restricted to the subject, the speech would not be useful.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle point: a proposition may be affected by distortion not due to falsehood but due to external considerations like restriction (hasr) of the subject to the predicate or vice versa, or implication, or identity, especially when the particle 'innama' is used. The author first notes that sometimes a proposition's truth is not at issue; rather, the additional implications from 'innama' create a different type of logical analysis. He explains that 'innama' functions to restrict either the subject to the predicate or the predicate to the subject, depending on the context of preceding statements. Specifically, 'innama' is appropriate after a negative proposition has been assumed, indicating that what follows is an exclusive assertion. If the intended restriction is to limit the subject to the predicate—used to refute someone who claims the subject belongs to something else—then the subject of the negative proposition is the same as the subject of the affirmative 'innama' proposition. For example, 'I am only a human' (innama ana bashar) implies a prior negative: 'I am not a king or a god.' The 'I' (the subject) is being restricted to humanness, contradicting claims of divinity. On the other hand, if the restriction is of the predicate to the subject—refuting someone who attributes the predicate to something other than the subject—then the predicate of the negative proposition is the same as the predicate of the affirmative. Example: 'I am only the god Allah' (innama ana Allahu ilah) implies a prior negative: 'Jesus is not a god,' restricting the predicate 'god' exclusively to Allah. The author emphasizes that without such restriction, the speech would be meaningless.

Key Terms
حصر
إنما
قضية سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The subject 'I' is restricted to the predicate 'human'; prior negative: 'I am not a king or a god'.
C) The subject 'I' is restricted to the predicate 'human'; prior negative: 'I am not a writer'.
D) The predicate 'human' is restricted to the subject; prior negative: 'No one is human'.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'innama' as a restriction device depends on the context of refutation, not on a fixed grammatical rule.

Truth Conditions for Quantified Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates truth conditions for categorical propositions with various quantifier combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value (true/false) of any categorical proposition by analyzing its subject and predicate quantifiers according to the rules given in this passage.

Core Idea
A proposition with a particular subject can be false even when the predicate is universal—the quantifiers interact in counterintuitive ways.

This passage from the Daqaiq al-Haqaiq breaks down the conditions under which a categorical proposition is false when the subject is particular. Many students assume that only universal subject propositions can be false, but this section reveals that particular subject propositions also have definite truth conditions that depend on the predicate's quantifier. By parsing these conditions, you will gain precise control over proposition evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject quantifier type
Determine whether the subject is particular (جزئي) affirmative or negative. This is the starting point for the rules that follow.
2
Identify the predicate quantifier type
For an affirmative subject, look at the predicate: is it universal or particular? If universal, is it affirmative or negative?
3
Apply the rule for universal affirmative predicate
If the predicate is universal affirmative, the proposition is absolutely false, just like when the subject is universal affirmative. The author gives examples: 'Some human is every animal' is false.
This is the key rule to memorize.
4
Extend the rule to other predicate quantifiers
The author states that if the predicate is universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative, the same ruling applies (the proposition is false). If the subject is particular negative, treat it as if the subject were universal negative.
All combinations except possibly when both subject and predicate are particular? The passage implies all are false.
5
Verify with an example
Test the example: 'Some human is every animal' – is it false? Yes, because not all animals are humans. Use this method to internalize the rule.
Practice with other examples from Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية الكذب، وأما إن كان سور الموضوع جزئياً فإما موجب أو سالب، فإن كان موجباً فسور المحمول إما كلي وإما جزئيّ، فإن كان كلياً فإما موجب وإما سالب فإن كان كلياً موجباً فالقضية كاذبة مطلقاً، كما لو كان سور الموضوع كلياً موجباً، كقولنا: (بعض) الإنسان هو كل (حيوان) (بعض الإنسان هو كل كاتب بعض الإنسان هو كل حجر)، وإن كان سور المحمول كلياً سالباً فالحكم كما لو كان سور الموضوع كلياً موجباً، وإن كان سور المحمول جزئياً موجباً أو جزئياً سالباً فالحكم أيضاً كما لو كان سور الموضوع كلياً موجباً، وإن كان سور الموضوع جزئياً سالباً فالحكم أيضاً كما لو كان سوره كلياً سالباً واعتبره بنفسك.
The first art: On categorical propositions. Falsehood, and if the quantifier of the subject is particular, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then the quantifier of the predicate is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then it is either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, then the proposition is absolutely false, just as if the quantifier of the subject were universal affirmative, like our saying: (Some) human is every (animal) (Some human is every writer Some human is every stone). And if the quantifier of the predicate is universal negative, then the ruling is as if the quantifier of the subject were universal affirmative. And if the quantifier of the predicate is particular affirmative or particular negative, then the ruling is also as if the quantifier of the subject were universal affirmative. And if the quantifier of the subject is particular negative, then the ruling is also as if its quantifier were universal negative. And consider it yourself.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions for categorical propositions when the subject quantifier is particular. The author first states that the subject quantifier can be either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, we look at the predicate quantifier, which can be universal or particular. For universal predicate, it can be affirmative or negative. The rule given is that when the subject is particular affirmative and the predicate is universal affirmative, the proposition is absolutely false. The author then gives examples: 'Some human is every animal' (clearly false because not all animals are human), 'Some human is every writer' (false because not all writers are human), 'Some human is every stone' (false). He then states that if the predicate quantifier is universal negative, or particular affirmative, or particular negative, the same ruling applies as if the subject were universal affirmative—meaning the proposition is also false. If the subject quantifier is particular negative, then the ruling is as if the subject were universal negative. This passage is dense and requires careful parsing; the student should memorize the combinations and verify them by reflection.

The Definite Article on the Predicate: Restriction, Generality, Entailment, and Identity
Author examines the definite article's role in restriction, entailment, and identity for negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical effects of the definite article on the predicate in affirmative and negative propositions, and identify whether it indicates restriction, generality, entailment, or identity based on context.

Core Idea
The definite article on the predicate is not always restrictive; it can indicate generality, entailment, or identity.

In this passage from the third article on Parmenias, the author examines a common assumption: that the definite article (al) on the predicate always restricts the predicate to the subject. He corrects this, showing that the definite article can have different logical functions depending on context. For affirmative propositions, it may indicate restriction or generality; for negatives, it signals either entailment (mulazama) or identity (ittihad). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic logical and legal discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definite article on the predicate
Look for the definite article (al) attached to the predicate in an Arabic proposition. This is the linguistic marker whose function you will analyze.
2
Determine if the proposition is affirmative or negative
The effect of the definite article differs between affirmative and negative propositions. Negative propositions often involve the particle 'laysa' or 'ma'.
3
If affirmative: Check for restriction vs. generality
If the definite article is intended for restriction (hasr), the proposition is false unless the predicate is unique to the subject. If it is for generality (ihmal), the proposition is true in a general sense. The author gives the example 'Zayd is the scholar in this town' – if meant restrictively, it's false because Zayd is not the only scholar.
Restriction makes the proposition more specific and often leads to falsity if not accurate.
4
If negative: Distinguish entailment from identity
In negative propositions, the definite article can indicate either entailment (mulazama) – where the predicate is a necessary concomitant of the subject – or identity (ittihad) – where subject and predicate share the same reality. The author gives examples: 'Man is nothing but the laughing one' (entailment) and 'Man is nothing but the human being' (identity).
Entailment means the predicate is essential; identity means they are the same thing.
5
Apply the classification to a new proposition
Take a proposition such as 'ليس الطبيب إلا زيد' (The doctor is nothing but Zayd) and determine whether it indicates entailment or identity based on the context. This step solidifies your ability to use the tool.
Practice with multiple examples to internalize the distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة: في بارمنياس وربما ظن حصر المحمول في الموضوع بدخول الألف واللام على المحمول كقولنا: زيد هو العالم في هذه البلدة وإنما يكون كذلك أن لو كانت للحصر، وإذ ذاك فإنها تكون كاذبة منحرفة، وإلا فإن كان للإهمال فلا؛ إذ الفرق بينها وبين قولنا: إنه عالم إلا في ما أسلفناه من الفرق، وأما إذا دخلت على محمول السالبة فإنها قد تفيد أحد أمرين إما الملازمة كما في قولنا: ليس الإنسان إلا الضاحك وإما اتحاد الحقيقة كقولنا : (ليس الإنسان إلا البشر.
The third article: On Parmenias. And perhaps restriction of the predicate to the subject is thought from the entry of the definite article on the predicate, like our saying: Zayd is the scholar in this town. And it is only like that if it is for restriction. Then it is false and distorted. Otherwise, if it is for generality, then no. For the difference between it and our saying: He is a scholar, except in what we have previously mentioned of the difference. And if it enters on the predicate of the negative, then it may indicate one of two things: either entailment, as in our saying: Man is nothing but the laughing one? (laysa al-insan illa al-dahik) Or identity of reality, like our saying: Man is nothing but the human being (laysa al-insan illa al-bashar).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage, from the third article on Parmenias, discusses the definite article (alif and lam) when attached to the predicate. The author warns that one might mistakenly think the definite article always indicates restriction of the predicate to the subject. Using the example 'Zayd is the scholar in this town', he explains that if the definite article is intended for restriction (hasr), the proposition is false because Zayd is not the only scholar. But if it is for generality (ihmal), it is not false. The difference between 'Zayd is the scholar' (with definite article) and 'Zayd is a scholar' (without) lies in the intended meaning. When the definite article enters the predicate of a negative proposition, it can indicate one of two things: entailment (mulazama) as in 'Man is nothing but the laughing one' (i.e., laughter is essential to man), or identity (ittihad) as in 'Man is nothing but the human being' (i.e., humanity and being human are the same). This passage clarifies the logical effects of the definite article, which is often misunderstood.

Key Terms
الألف واللام
الإهمال
الملازمة
الاتحاد

Key Takeaway
The definite article on the predicate can indicate restriction, generality, entailment, or identity; its logical function must be determined from context.

The Definite Article on Affirmative Predicates
Author clarifies the logical function of the definite article on affirmative predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the definite article on an affirmative predicate indicates restriction or generality, and determine the truth value accordingly.

Core Idea
Putting 'al-' on a predicate does not automatically restrict it to the subject.

A common mistake in logic is to assume that when the definite article appears on the predicate of a proposition like 'Zayd is the learned one in this town', it always means the predicate belongs exclusively to the subject. The author explains that this depends on the intention: if it is for restriction, the proposition may be false; if for generality, it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's structure
Note that the predicate has the definite article 'al-'. Example: 'Zayd is the learned one' – 'al-ʿālim' is definite.
2
Determine the intended meaning of 'al-'
Is it meant for restriction (hasr) or for generality (al-ihmāl)? The author says if for restriction, the proposition is false and deviant.
3
Check the truth value
If 'al-' is for restriction, the proposition asserts exclusivity; if false, the proposition is false. If for generality, no exclusivity is claimed, so it may be true or false depending on the subject.
4
Contrast with universal affirmative without 'al-'
Compare 'Zayd is learned' (no restriction) with 'Zayd is the learned one' (with 'al-'). The difference is exactly what the author mentions: the presence or absence of restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما ظن حصر المحمول في الموضوع بدخول الألف واللام على المحمول كقولنا: زيد هو العالم في هذه البلدة وإنما يكون كذلك أن لو كانت للحصر، وإذ ذاك فإنها تكون كاذبة منحرفة، وإلا فإن كان للإهمال فلا؛ إذ الفرق بينها وبين قولنا: إنه عالم إلا في ما أسلفناه من الفرق، وأما إذا دخلت على محمول السالبة فإنها قد تفيد أحد أمرين إما الملازمة كما في قولنا: ليس الإنسان إلا الضاحك وإما اتحاد الحقيقة كقولنا : (ليس الإنسان إلا البشر.
And perhaps it is thought that the restriction of the predicate to the subject [is indicated] by the entry of 'al' and 'lam' on the predicate, like our saying: 'Zayd is the learned one in this town.' And that would be so only if they were for restriction. If that is the case, then they would be false and deviant. Otherwise, if they are for generality, then no; for the difference between it and our saying 'He is learned' is only in what we have previously mentioned of the difference. And if they enter on the predicate of a negative, then they may convey one of two things: either implication, as in our saying: 'Man is nothing but laughing,' or identity of reality, as in our saying: 'Man is nothing but human.'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses a common logical misunderstanding: the assumption that putting the definite article 'al-' on the predicate of a categorical proposition necessarily indicates restriction (حصْر) of the predicate to the subject. The author explains that this is not always the case. If the definite article is intended for restriction, then the proposition becomes false and deviant (because it would imply exclusivity that may not hold). If it is intended for generality or indifference, then no restriction is meant. The author then distinguishes between this and a universal negative proposition. Finally, when the definite article enters on the predicate of a negative proposition, it can indicate either implication (ملازمة) or identity of reality (اتحاد الحقيقة), as in the examples given.

الألف واللام

Key Takeaway
The definite article on a predicate does not automatically indicate restriction; its function depends on the speaker's intention, affecting truth value.

The Two Meanings of 'Al-' on Negative Predicates
Author explains the two functions of the definite article on the predicate of a negative proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between implication and identity of reality when the definite article appears on the predicate of a negative proposition.

Core Idea
In negative propositions, 'al-' on the predicate can indicate necessary implication or essential identity.

When the definite article appears on the predicate of a negative proposition, it does not indicate restriction. Instead, it conveys one of two meanings: implication (mulāzama) or identity of reality (ittiḥād al-ḥaqīqa). The author gives examples to illustrate this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative proposition
It has a negative copula, like 'laysa' or 'mā ... illā'. Example: 'mā al-insānu illā al-ḍāḥiku' (Man is nothing but laughing).
2
Check if the predicate has the definite article
In 'mā al-insānu illā al-ḍāḥiku', the predicate 'al-ḍāḥik' has 'al-'.
3
Determine the intended meaning
Does it convey implication (laughing is a necessary concomitant of humanity) or identity of reality (the reality of man is identical to the reality of human)? The author gives both examples.
4
Recognize the difference
Implication means the predicate necessarily accompanies the subject but is not its essence. Identity means they are the same reality.
The examples: 'Man is nothing but laughing' (implication) vs 'Man is nothing but human' (identity).

The Simple Categorical Proposition: Definition and Types
Author defines the simple categorical proposition and enumerates its forms by quantity and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given categorical proposition is simple (بسيطة) or privative (عدمية), and classify it by subject type and quality.

Core Idea
A simple proposition has a positive reality as its predicate, regardless of quality or subject type.

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational classification of categorical propositions. Understanding the distinction between simple and privative propositions is the first step in analyzing definitions and arguments. We will trace the author's division and then apply it to examples from kalām and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Primary division of categorical propositions
Note that the author first divides categorical propositions into affirmative (محصلة) and negative. Affirmative ones are 'stripped of the negative particle' that is part of the copula. This is your first distinction.
The negative particle is not the same as a negative predicate.
2
Subdivision of affirmative propositions
Affirmative propositions are further divided: the predicate is either a positive reality (ذات) or a privation (عدم ذات). If it is a positive reality, the proposition is called 'simple' (بسيطة). Examples from the text: 'Zayd is seeing' (simple) vs. 'Zayd is blind' (privative).
Privative propositions are also affirmative in quality but have a privative predicate.
3
Exhaustive enumeration of simple propositions
The author lists all possible simple propositions by subject type (personal, indefinite, quantified universal, quantified particular) and quality (affirmative or negative). Count the eight combinations: two qualities × four subject types = eight. The predicate remains a positive reality in all cases.
This enumeration shows that 'simple' is not about quantity or quality, but only about the predicate's nature.
4
Identifying simple vs. privative in practice
Given a proposition, check whether the predicate expresses a positive attribute (e.g., seeing, knowing, existing) or a privation (e.g., blind, ignorant, non-existent). If positive, it is simple; if privation, it is privative. The quality (affirmative/negative) does not matter for this classification.
A negative simple proposition like 'Zayd is not seeing' is still simple because the predicate 'seeing' is a positive reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول : إن القضية الحَمْلية إما أن يتجرد جزئها عن حرف السالب على وجه لا يكون كالجزء من أحدهما أو ليس فإن كان الأول فتسمى (محصلة) وهي إما أن يكون المحمول في نفسه ذاتاً أو عدم ذات، فإن كان ذاتاً فالقضية تسمى (بسيطة) وسواء كان موضوعها شخصياً أو مهملاً أو محصوراً، وسواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، فالشخصية الموجبة منها كقولنا : زيد) بصير) والسالبة زيد) ليس هو بصير) والموجبة المهملة منها كقولنا: الإنسان هو بصير) والسالبة الإنسان ليس هو بصيراً) والمحصورة الكلية الموجبة منها كقولنا كل إنسان هو بصير) والسالبة (لا شيء من الإنسان بصيرا) والموجبة الجزئية منها كقولنا بعض الناس هو بصير) والسالبة ليس كل إنسان بصيراً).
We say: The categorical proposition either is such that its part is stripped of the negative particle in a way that it is not like a part of either of them, or not. If the first, it is called 'affirmative' (muhaṣṣala). And it is either that the predicate in itself is a positive reality or a negation of reality. If it is a positive reality, then the proposition is called 'simple' (basīṭa). It does not matter whether its subject is personal, indefinite, or quantified, and whether it is affirmative or negative. The affirmative personal among them is like our saying: 'Zayd is seeing.' The negative: 'Zayd is not seeing.' The affirmative indefinite: 'Man is seeing.' The negative indefinite: 'Man is not seeing.' The quantified universal affirmative: 'Every man is seeing.' The universal negative: 'No man is seeing.' The particular affirmative: 'Some people are seeing.' The particular negative: 'Not every man is seeing.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter by dividing the categorical proposition. First, it distinguishes propositions that are stripped of the negative particle (affirmative) from those that are not. The affirmative (محصلة) is then divided based on whether the predicate itself is a positive reality (ذات) or the negation of a reality (عدم ذات). If it is a positive reality, the proposition is called simple (بسيطة). The author then lists all possible forms of simple propositions regardless of subject type (personal, indefinite, quantified) and quality (affirmative or negative). Eight examples are given: affirmative/negative personal, affirmative/negative indefinite, universal affirmative/negative, and particular affirmative/negative. This enumeration is exhaustive for simple propositions.

The Privative Categorical Proposition
Author defines the privative proposition and enumerates its forms, with an additional common interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a privative categorical proposition and classify its forms (affirmative/negative, personal/indefinite/quantified) according to the strict definition.

Core Idea
A privative proposition's predicate is a negation of a reality, not a positive attribute.

Now that we have covered simple categorical propositions with positive predicates, we move to a special type: the privative proposition ('adamiyya). The key difference is that its predicate is not a positive reality but the negation of one, like 'blind' meaning 'not seeing'. Understanding this distinction is crucial in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with absence-based rulings (e.g., 'the debtor is not paying'). In this lesson, we will parse the definition and see how the same eight forms apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate type
Look at the predicate of the proposition. If it is a term that denotes the negation or absence of a positive reality (e.g., 'blind' = not seeing, 'unjust' = not just), then the proposition may be privative. However, note that according to the strict definition in this passage, only predicates that are explicitly the negation of a reality count, not those that are merely the inferior of two contraries in a positive sense.
Example: 'Zayd is blind' – predicate 'blind' = 'not seeing', so it is privative. 'Zayd is seeing' – predicate 'seeing' is positive, so it is not privative.
2
Check subject and quality
Determine whether the subject is personal (e.g., Zayd), indefinite (e.g., man), universal (e.g., every man), or particular (e.g., some man). Also note whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. The passage shows that privative propositions can be affirmative or negative regardless of subject type.
For example: 'Zayd is blind' (affirmative personal), 'Zayd is not blind' (negative personal), 'Every man is blind' (affirmative universal), 'No man is blind' (negative universal).
3
Distinguish the two interpretations
The passage mentions that in common usage, 'privative' is sometimes interpreted as having the predicate be the inferior of two contraries, which may include positive realities (e.g., injustice relative to justice). Note that the author's strict definition requires the predicate to be a negation of a reality, not just any inferior contrary.
Under the common interpretation, 'Zayd is unjust' would be privative because injustice is the inferior of justice. Under the strict definition, only predicates like 'blind' (meaning lack of sight) qualify. Be aware of which definition is being used in the text you are studying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان المحمول عدم ذات أما بالمعنى المشهوري أو الخاصي سميت القضية (عدمية) وسواء كان موضوعها أيضاً شخصياً أو مهملاً أو محصوراً، وسواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، فالموجبة الشخصية منها كقولنا : زيد) هو أعمى والسالبة كقولنا : زيد) ليس هو (أعمى والموجبة المهملة كقولنا : (الإنسان هو أعمى) والسالبة كقولنا: الإنسان ليس هو أعمى والموجبة الكلية منها كقولنا: (كل إنسان هو أعمى) والسالبة (لا شيء من الإنسان هو أعمى) وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الأمر في الجزئيتين الموجبة والسالبة. وربما فسرت العدمية في المشهور بما محمولها أخس المتقابلين كان ذاتاً كالجور بالنسبة إلى العدل، أو عدماً كالعمى بالنسبة إلى البصر.
And if the predicate is the negation of a reality, whether in the common sense or the specific sense, the proposition is called 'privative' ('adamiyya). It does not matter whether its subject is personal, indefinite, or quantified, and whether it is affirmative or negative. The affirmative personal among them is like our saying: 'Zayd is blind.' The negative: 'Zayd is not blind.' The affirmative indefinite: 'Man is blind.' The negative indefinite: 'Man is not blind.' The quantified universal affirmative: 'Every man is blind.' The universal negative: 'No man is blind.' And on this, the matter is not hidden regarding the two particular positive and negative. And perhaps the privative is interpreted in common usage as that whose predicate is the inferior of the two contraries, whether it is a positive reality like injustice relative to justice, or a privation like blindness relative to sight.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage completes the division of categorical propositions by defining the privative proposition (عدمية). Unlike the simple proposition whose predicate is a positive reality, the privative has a predicate that is the negation of a reality, such as 'blind' which means 'not seeing'. The author enumerates the same eight forms as before, replacing the positive predicate with a privative one. Additionally, the passage notes a common interpretation: some define privative propositions as those whose predicate is the inferior of two contraries, which could be either a positive reality (like injustice) or a privation (like blindness). This shows that the definition of 'عدمية' can vary, but the author sticks to the stricter definition based on the predicate being a negation of a reality.

Distinguishing Simple, Privative, and Modified Categorical Propositions
Author defines three types of categorical propositions and provides examples for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify categorical propositions as simple (basīṭa), privative (ʿadamiyya), or modified (muʿaddala) based on the nature of the predicate and the presence of negation particles.

Core Idea
The predicate's nature (essence, non-essence, or negated term) determines the proposition type.

In Islamic logic, categorical propositions are classified not only by quantity and quality but also by the character of the predicate. This distinction is crucial for understanding entailments and oppositions. The passage introduces three types: simple (predicate is a positive essence), privative (predicate is a non-essence or the baser of opposites), and modified (one or both terms contain a negation particle). We'll learn to parse each type using the author's criteria.

Guided Path
1
Check for negation particles in terms
Examine the subject and predicate: if either contains a negation particle (such as 'ghayr' or 'la' as part of the word), the proposition is modified (muʿaddala). For example, 'Zayd is not-just' (ghayr ʿadl) is modified.
If the negation particle negates the connection (e.g., 'laysa' before predicate), it is negative simple, not modified.
2
Identify the predicate's nature if no negation in terms
If the proposition is affirmative (muhassala) and no term contains a negation particle, look at the predicate: is it a positive essence (like 'seeing', 'rational') or a non-essence (like 'blindness', 'injustice')? Positive essence = simple (basīṭa); non-essence = privative (ʿadamiyya).
Privative may also be defined as having the baser of two opposites as predicate, even if that baser is a positive term (e.g., injustice).
3
Apply to all quantified forms
The classification applies regardless of quantity (individual, unspecified, universal, particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). For each proposition, follow steps 1 and 2 to determine its type.
Negative simple propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing') have a negation particle that negates the connection, not a term.
4
Beware of confusion between affirmative modified and negative simple
The affirmative modified proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not-just') is often mistaken for the negative simple ('Zayd is not just'). In the modified, 'not-just' is a positive attribute (injustice); in the negative simple, 'not just' simply denies justice. They are logically distinct.
Use the author's warning to avoid this common error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في انقسام القضية الحملية باعتبار محمولها إلى البسيطة والمعدولة والعدمية وبيان أحكامها من العموم والخصوص والتلازم والتقابل. نقول: إن القضية الحملية إما أن يتجرد جزئها عن حرف السالب على وجه لا يكون كالجزء من أحدهما أو ليس، فإن كان الأول فتسمى (محصلة). وهي إما أن يكون المحمول في نفسه ذاتاً أو عدم ذات، فإن كان ذاتاً فالقضية تسمى (بسيطة) وسواء كان موضوعها شخصياً أو مهملاً أو محصوراً، وسواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، فالشخصية الموجبة منها كقولنا: زيد بصير، والسالبة: زيد ليس هو بصير، والموجبة المهملة: الإنسان هو بصير، والسالبة: الإنسان ليس هو بصيراً، والمحصورة الكلية الموجبة: كل إنسان هو بصير، أخسهما وهي العدمية. وهذه العدمية هي ما نريد بيان حكمها هاهنا لعمومها. وأما إن كان جزئا القضية مجردين عن حرف السلب، بل كل واحد من جزأيهما أو أحدهما مركب من اسم محصل أو كلمة محصلة وحرف سلب سميت القضية:
