Chapter Four: On the division of the categorical proposition with respect to its predicate into the simple, the modified, and the privative, and an explanation of their properties of generality, particularity, entailment, and opposition. We say: The categorical proposition is either that whose parts are free from the negation particle in such a way that it is not like a part of one of them or not. If it is the first, it is called 'affirmative' (muhassala). And it is either that the predicate in itself is a positive essence or a non-essence. If it is a positive essence, the proposition is called 'simple' (basita), whether its subject is individual, unspecified, or quantified, and whether it is affirmative or negative. The affirmative individual from it is like our saying: 'Zayd is seeing'; the negative: 'Zayd is not seeing'; the affirmative unspecified: 'Man is seeing'; the negative: 'Man is not seeing'; the affirmative universal quantified: 'Every man is seeing'; the negative: 'No man is seeing'; the affirmative particular: 'Some men are seeing'; the negative: 'Not every man is seeing'. As for if the predicate is a non-essence, either in the common sense or the specific sense, the proposition is called 'privative' (adamiyya), whether its subject is also individual, unspecified, or quantified, and whether it is affirmative or negative. The affirmative individual from it is like our saying: 'Zayd is blind'; the negative: 'Zayd is not blind'; the affirmative unspecified: 'Man is blind'; the negative: 'Man is not blind'; the affirmative universal: 'Every man is blind'; the negative: 'No man is blind'. And on this, the matter is not hidden for the two particular propositions, affirmative and negative. Perhaps the privative is interpreted in common usage as that whose predicate is the baser of the two opposites, whether it is a positive essence like injustice relative to justice, or a privation like blindness relative to sight. And accordingly, the division of the affirmative proposition is not into that whose predicate is a positive essence and that whose predicate is a non-essence, but rather into that whose predicate is the nobler of the two opposites, which is the simple, and that which is the baser of them, which is the privative. And this privative is what we intend to explain its properties here due to its generality. As for if the two parts of the proposition are not free from the negation particle, but rather each one of its two parts or one of them is composed of a positive noun or positive word and a negation particle, the proposition is called 'modified' (mu'addala) and 'changed' (mutaghayyira). And it does not matter whether that which the negation particle is attached to from among the positive terms is a positive essence or a privation. So on this, the negation particle either comes after the copula or before it. If it comes after it, it is part of the predicate. If it comes before it, then it either comes after the subject or before it. If it comes after the subject, it is not a part of either of them, neither the subject nor the predicate, but rather an indicator of the negation of the connection between them, and that is as in our saying: 'Man is not a stone'. If it comes before the subject, then the proposition is either quantified or not. If it is quantified, then the negation particle either comes before the quantifier or after it. If it comes before it, it is for negation, not for modification, as in our saying: 'Not every man is a stone'. If the quantifier comes before the negation particle, it is for modification, not for negation, as in our saying: 'Every not-man or some not-man is a stone'. And if there is no quantifier, then knowing whether it is part of the subject or negating the connection is known only by intention or by the word 'ghayr', since in the Arabic language it is not used generally except for modification, as in our saying: 'Ghayr Zayd is seeing'. But the absolute modified proposition (al-mu'addala al-mutlaqa) is the one in which the negation particle is part of the predicate, whether the subject is modified or not, and whether its subject is individual, unspecified, or quantified, and whether it is affirmative or negative. The affirmative individual from it is like our saying: 'Zayd is not just'; the affirmative unspecified: 'Man is not just'; the negative: 'Man is not not just'; the affirmative universal: 'Every man is not just'; the negative: 'No man is not just'; the affirmative particular: 'Some man is not just'; the negative particular: 'Not every man is not just'. But perhaps the affirmative of it is confused with the negative of the simple, and it is thought that there is no difference between our saying 'Zayd is not'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of categorical propositions based on the nature of the predicate and the presence of negation. The author begins by stating the subject: the division of the categorical proposition into simple, privative, and modified, and that their properties concerning generality, particularity, entailment, and opposition will be explained. He then clarifies the first type: if the proposition is free of negation as a part of its terms, it is called 'affirmative' (muhassala). Within that, if the predicate is a positive essence (like 'seeing'), the proposition is 'simple' (basita). He provides examples for all quantifier forms: individual, unspecified, universal, particular, both affirmative and negative. Next, if the predicate is a non-essence (like 'blindness'), the proposition is 'privative' (adamiyya), again with examples for all forms. He notes an alternative interpretation: the privative is defined as having the baser of two opposites as its predicate, whether that baser is a positive term (like injustice) or a privation (like blindness). Consequently, the division of affirmative propositions is not into essence and non-essence, but into nobler predicate (simple) and baser predicate (privative). The author then turns to the third type: if either the subject or predicate (or both) includes a negation particle as part of its makeup (like 'not-just'), the proposition is 'modified' (mu'addala) or 'changed' (mutaghayyira). He explains the placement of the negation particle relative to the copula and the subject, distinguishing cases where it modifies the predicate (part of predicate), indicates negation of connection (after subject but before copula), or modifies the subject (before subject with quantifier after). For quantified propositions, if the negation precedes the quantifier, it is negation; if the quantifier precedes, it is modification. If no quantifier, the word 'ghayr' typically indicates modification. Finally, he defines the 'absolute modified' as having the negation particle as part of the predicate, and gives examples. He warns of a common confusion: the affirmative modified proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not just') is easily mistaken for the negative simple proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'), but they are distinct because 'not-just' is a positive term? Actually careful: 'Zayd is not just' (Zayd huwa ghayr 'adl) is affirmative modified, while 'Zayd is not just' using 'laysa' would be negative simple? The author indicates that people may think there is no difference, but there is. This warning is crucial for parsing propositions correctly.

Key Terms
بسيطة
عدمية
معدولة
حرف السلب
المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a modified categorical proposition (معدولة) as defined in the passage?
A) كل إنسان هو ناطق (Every man is rational)
B) زيد ليس هو أعمى (Zayd is not blind)
C) بعض الناس هو غير عادل (Some men are unjust)
D) لا شيء من الإنسان هو حجر (Nothing of man is a stone)

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is classified based on its predicate: if it is a positive essence, it is simple; if a non-essence or baser opposite, privative; if any term contains a negation particle, modified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Identifying Ma'dula Propositions and Distinguishing Them from Simple Negatives
Author defines ma'dula propositions and refutes a misconception about their distinction from simple negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is a simple negative or an affirmative modified (ma'dula) based on the position of the negation particle, and explain why the semantic criterion based on the 'nature' of the attribute is incorrect.

Core Idea
The distinction between 'Zaid is not just' and 'Zaid is not-just' is purely syntactic, not semantic.

In this passage, the author defines the modified proposition and addresses a common confusion: the difference between a simple negative and an affirmative modified. He provides a clear syntactic rule based on the position of the negation particle relative to the copula, and refutes a mistaken semantic view with logical examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle
Look for harf al-salb (e.g., la, laysa, ghayr) in the proposition.
2
Find the copula
In a tripartite proposition, locate the copula (e.g., huwa, laysa).
3
Check position of negation relative to copula
If negation is after the copula, it is part of the predicate → affirmative modified (e.g., Zaid huwa ghayru 'adl). If negation is before the copula and after subject, it is a simple negative (e.g., Zaid laysa 'adlan).
For bipartite propositions, use 'ghayr' as an indicator of modification.
4
Handle quantified propositions
If negation is before the quantifier, it is a simple negative; if after the quantifier, it modifies the subject.
Example: 'Not every man is a stone' vs. 'Every non-man is a stone'.
5
Apply to refutation examples
Examine the syllogisms about 'not-existing in a subject' and 'not-impossible' to see why the semantic criterion fails.
Note that the syllogisms are valid in form; the issue is with the truth of premises under the semantic criterion.

مقترن به حرف السلب مما قد يثبت للموضوع لذاته أو لذاتي له أو ليس، فالفرق على ما نجعله. أما من جهة اللفظ فلأن حرف السلب متأخر عن الرابطة في الموجبة المعدولة، ومتقدم عليها في السالبة كما عرف ذلك. [الظاهر] إن كانت القضية ثلاثية وإن كانت ثنائية فقد يعرف أن حرف السلب فيها للعدول دون السلب بلفظ (غير)، فإنها لا تستعمل في لغة العرب لغير العدول إذ لا يقال زيد غير هو عدل بل هو غير عدل وإلا فبالنية.
And therefore the division of the affirmative [proposition] is not into what has a positive attribute as predicate and what has a privation of attribute as predicate, but rather into what has the nobler of the two opposite predicates, and that is the simple [proposition], and into what is its sister, and that is the privative. And this privative is what we intend to explain its judgment here due to its generality. And as for if the parts of the proposition are free from the negation particle, but rather each of its two parts or one of them is composed of a positive noun or a positive verb and a negation particle, the proposition is called 'modified' and 'changed'. And it is the same whether what is accompanied by the negation particle from the positive terms is an attribute or a privation. On this basis, the negation particle is either after the copula or before it. If it is after it, then it is a part of the predicate. And if it is before it, then it is either after the subject or before it. If it is after the subject, then it is not a part of either of them, neither the subject nor the predicate, but rather it is a term indicating the negation of the connection between them, as in our saying: 'Man is not a stone.' And if it is before the subject, then the proposition is either quantified or not. If it is quantified, then the negation particle is either before the quantifier or after it. If it is before it, then it is for negation, not for modification, as in our saying: 'Not every man is a stone.' And if the quantifier is before the negation particle, then it is for modification, not for negation, as in our saying: 'Every non-man' or 'Some non-man is a stone.' And if there is no quantifier, then knowing whether it is a part of the subject or a negator of the connection is only known by intention or by the word 'ghayr' (other), since it is not used in the language of the Arabs mostly for other than modification, as in our saying: 'Other than Zaid is seeing.' But the absolutely modified proposition is that in which the negation particle is a part of the predicate, whether the subject is modified or not, and whether its subject is individual or indefinite or quantified, and whether it is affirmative or negative. The individual affirmative of them is like our saying: 'Zaid is not-just.' The indefinite affirmative is like our saying: 'Man is not-just.' The negative is: 'Man is not not-just.' The universal affirmative is: 'Every man is not-just.' The negative is: 'No man is not-just.' The particular affirmative is like our saying: 'Some man is not-just.' The particular negative is: 'Not every man is not-just.' But perhaps the affirmative of them is confused with the simple negative, and it is thought that there is no difference between our saying: 'Zaid is not just' and our saying: 'Zaid is not-just.' And the difference between them is not as was said: that the simple negative indicates the negation of a thing from a thing, whether the negated thing is of such a nature that it would be affirmed of the subject by itself or by something essential to it or not, while the affirmative modified proposition indicates the absence of a thing that is of such a nature that it would be affirmed of the thing by itself, so that when one says 'not-just' or 'not-just', it is only said of one who has justice in his nature or an essential nature. And it was said in refuting it: If the matter were as was said, then what follows from our saying: 'Every body is not-existing in a subject, and everything not-existing in a subject is a substance' — that the body is a substance — would be false, because 'not-existing in a subject' does not indicate the absence of something that is of such a nature to be affirmed of the body or the substance, nor of its genus, since there is no genus — unless absolute existence is taken as a genus for it, and even if it is closer, it is still not correct. Otherwise, what follows from our saying: 'The existence of the world is not-impossible, and everything whose existence is not-impossible is either necessary or possible' — that the existence of the world is either necessary or possible — would be false, because 'not-impossible' does not indicate the absence of something that is of such a nature to be affirmed of the world, or the necessary, or the possible, not by consideration of their essences, nor of what is predicated of them, whether essential or accidental. But it is not correct, for it is not considered that the lack of necessary truth of the conclusion from the mentioned syllogism is due to the lack of truth of the premise, even if the conclusion is true in itself. Rather the truth is that even if that is the terminology of a group, we only intend by 'modified' that in which the negation particle is a part of its predicate, whether what is accompanied by the negation particle is such that it may be affirmed of the subject by itself or by something essential to it or not. So the difference is according to what we make it. As for from the aspect of wording, the negation particle is after the copula in the affirmative modified proposition, and before it in the negative, as was known. [It is apparent that] if the proposition is tripartite, and if it is bipartite, then it can be known that the negation particle in it is for modification, not for negation, by the word 'ghayr', for it is not used in the language of the Arabs for other than modification, since one does not say 'Zaid is not he is just' but rather 'Zaid is not-just', otherwise [it is known] by intention.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and clarification of 'modified propositions' (al-qadaya al-ma'dula) in Aristotelian logic, as understood within the Islamic tradition. The author begins by revisiting the division of affirmative propositions (al-muhassala) into simple (basita) and privative (adamiyya), but then corrects a previous notion: the division is not based on whether the predicate is a positive attribute or its absence, but rather on whether the predicate is the nobler of two opposites (for simple) or its opposite (privative). Then the author defines the modified proposition (ma'dula): it is a proposition in which at least one part (subject or predicate) is composed of a positive term and a negation particle, making it 'modified' or 'changed'. The negation particle can appear in various positions. If it follows the copula (the linking verb), it is part of the predicate. If it precedes the copula but follows the subject, it negates the connection entirely (simple negation). If it precedes the subject in a quantified proposition, its position relative to the quantifier determines whether it is a negation (if before the quantifier) or modification (if after). In indefinite propositions, intention or the word 'ghayr' (other) distinguishes modification from negation. The author enumerates all types of modified propositions based on quantifier and affirmation/negation. A key confusion arises: some think 'Zaid is not-just' (affirmative modified) is the same as 'Zaid is not just' (simple negative). The author rejects a proposed criterion based on the 'nature' (sha'n) of the attribute. The refutation uses examples: if the criterion were that the affirmative modified should indicate the absence of something that would naturally belong (by essence), then the syllogism about 'body is not-existing in a subject' would lead to a false conclusion, because 'not-existing in a subject' does not refer to something that naturally belongs to body. Similarly for 'not-impossible'. The author argues the fallacy is in the premise, not the criterion. The correct distinction is purely formal: in affirmative modified propositions, the negation particle is after the copula; in simple negatives, it is before the copula. For bipartite propositions (without a separate copula), the word 'ghayr' signals modification. Otherwise, intention determines.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the correct way to distinguish a simple negative proposition from an affirmative modified proposition is:
A) The simple negative uses the word 'laysa' while the affirmative modified uses 'ghayr'.
B) In the simple negative, the negation particle precedes the copula; in the affirmative modified, it follows the copula and is part of the predicate.
C) The simple negative denies something that would naturally belong, while the affirmative modified denies something that would not.
D) The simple negative is always universal; the affirmative modified is always particular.

Key Takeaway
The affirmative modified proposition (e.g., 'Zaid is not-just') has the negation particle as part of the predicate, while the simple negative (e.g., 'Zaid is not just') has the negation particle before the copula, negating the connection.

Answers: (a) Simple negative, because 'not' precedes copula. (b) Affirmative modified, because 'non' modifies the subject. (c) Affirmative modified, because 'not' is part of predicate 'not-obligatory'.

Distinguishing Simple Negative from Modified Affirmative
Author defines and distinguishes simple negative from modified affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is a simple negative or a modified affirmative based on the position of the negation particle and the existential requirement.

Core Idea
Structural distinction: negation before copula = simple negative; negation in predicate = modified affirmative, requiring existence.

In this passage, the author corrects a common misunderstanding about the difference between simple negatives and modified affirmatives. He rejects a view based on natural potential and provides counterexamples. We will walk through his correction and learn the precise criteria: the position of the negation particle and the truth conditions regarding subject existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate where the author defines the simple negative (السالبة البسيطة) and the modified affirmative (الموجبة المعدولة). Note that the rejected view focuses on whether the negated attribute could naturally belong to the subject.
The author says the difference is 'not as was said'.
2
Understand the rejected view
The prior view: a modified affirmative indicates absence of a thing that could be affirmed of the subject in itself (e.g., 'unjust' only applies to one who could be just). The simple negative does not require such potential.
3
Examine the author's counterexample
The author gives a syllogism: 'Every body is not existing in a subject; everything not existing in a subject is a substance; therefore body is substance.' Under the rejected view, the conclusion would be false because 'not existing in a subject' is not something that could naturally belong to body. But the syllogism is actually valid.
This shows the rejected view leads to incorrect assessment of validity.
4
Extract the author's correct definition
The author states: 'We only mean by modified that whose negation particle is part of its predicate.' In a simple negative, negation is part of the copula. Thus, the structure determines the type.
The negation particle 'غير' in Arabic is specifically for modification, not simple negation.
5
Apply the verbal and meaning criteria
Verbally: in modified affirmative, negation particle comes after the copula; in simple negative, it comes before. Meaningfully: modified affirmatives require the subject to exist (e.g., 'sharik Allah is unjust' is false), while simple negatives can be true even if the subject does not exist (e.g., 'sharik Allah is not permissible' is true).
This existential condition follows from the nature of affirmation.

الفن الأول: في القضايا الحَمْلية

هو من عدلاً وبين قولنا زيد) هو غير عدل، والفرق بينهما لا بما قيل من أن البسيطة السالبة تدل على سلب شيء عن شيء كان المسلوب من شأنه أن يكون ثابتاً للمسلوب عنه لذاته أو لذاتي له أم لا، والمعدولة الموجبة هي ما تدل على عدم أمر شأنه أن يكون ثابتاً للشيء لذاته حتى إنه إذا قيل لا عادل أو غير عادل، إنما يصح على من له العدل في طبيعته أو طبيعة ذاتية. وربما قيل في إبطاله: إنه لو كان الأمر على ما قيل لكان ما لزم عن قولنا: (كل) جسم هو غير موجود في موضوع ، وكل ما هو غير موجود في موضوع جوهر من أن الجسم جوهر غير صادق، إذ غير الموجود في الموضوع ليس يشير إلى عدم شيء من شأنه أن يكون ثابتاً للجسم أو الجوهر ولا لجنسه، إذ لا جنس اللهم إلا أن يؤخذ مطلق الوجود كالجنس له فهو وإن كان أقرب فغير صحيح أيضاً، وإلا كان ما يلزم عن قولنا: (وجود العالم غير مستحيل، وكـل مـا وجـوده غیر مستحيل، فإما واجب وإما ممكن - من أن وجود العالم إما واجب أو ممكن - غير صادق، إذ غير المستحيل لا يدل على عدم أمر من شأنه أن يكون ثابتاً للعالم، أو الواجب، أو الممكن، لا باعتبار ذاتيهما، ولا مـا يحمل عليهما ذاتياً كان أو عرضياً. له. وليس بحق، فإنه ليس يعد القول بعدم لزوم صدق النتيجة من القياس المذكور لعدم صدق المقدمة، وإن كانت النتيجة صادقة في نفس الأمر. بل الحق أنه وإن كان ذلك مصطلح فريق فنحن إنما نريد بالمعدولة ما حرف السلب فيها جزء من محمولها كان مقترن به حرف السلب مما قد يثبت للموضوع لذاته أو لذاتي له أو ليس، فالفرق على ما نجعله. أما من جهة اللفظ فلأن حرف السلب متأخر عن الرابطة في الموجبة المعدولة، ومتقدم عليها في السالبة كما عرف ذلك. [الظاهر] إن كانت القضية ثلاثية وإن كانت ثنائية فقد يعرف أن حرف السلب فيها للعدول دون السلب بلفظ (غير)، فإنها لا تستعمل في لغة العرب لغير العدول إذ لا يقال زيد غير هو عدل بل هو غير عدل وإلا فبالنية.
وأما من جهة المعنى فلأن السالبة أعم من الموجبة المعدولة، إذ السالبة قد تصدق مع وجود الموضوع كقولنا: الباري تعالى ليس بجائز)، ومع عدمه كقولنا: شريك الله ليس بجائز، والموجبة المعدولة لا تصدق إلا مع وجود الموضوع حتى لو قيل شريك الله هو غير عادل لم يكن صادقاً، وإنما أوجبنا كون الموضوع موجود إلا أن قولنا: (غير عادل يقتضي وجوده، بل لأن حقيقة الإيجاب هو الحكم بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، والحكم بثبوت شيء لما ليس بثابت محال.
The first art: on categorical propositions. It is from justly and between our saying (Zaid) is not just, and the difference between them is not as was said that the simple negative indicates the negation of something from something, whether the negated thing is such that it could be affirmed of the subject in itself or by an essential attribute or not, and the modified affirmative is that which indicates the absence of a thing that is such that it could be affirmed of the thing in itself, such that when it is said 'not just' or 'unjust', it is only valid for one who has justice in his nature or essential nature. And perhaps it was said in refutation of this: If the matter were as stated, then what follows from our saying 'Every body is not existing in a subject, and everything not existing in a subject is a substance'—that the body is a substance—would not be true, since 'not existing in a subject' does not refer to the absence of something that could be affirmed of the body or substance or its genus, because there is no genus, unless absolute existence is taken as its genus, though it is closer, it is still not correct. Otherwise, what follows from our saying 'The existence of the world is not impossible, and everything whose existence is not impossible is either necessary or possible'—that the existence of the world is either necessary or possible—would not be true, since 'not impossible' does not indicate the absence of something that could be affirmed of the world, the necessary, or the possible, whether by virtue of their essences or what is predicated of them, whether essential or accidental. It is not correct that it is not considered a lack of necessary truth of the conclusion from the mentioned syllogism due to the lack of truth of the premise, even if the conclusion is true in itself. Rather, the truth is that even if that is the terminology of some groups, we only mean by 'modified' that whose negation particle is part of its predicate, connected with the negation particle, concerning what may be affirmed of the subject in itself or by an essential attribute or not; the difference according to what we make it. As for the aspect of wording, the negation particle comes after the copula in the modified affirmative, and before it in the negative, as is known. [Apparently] if the proposition is tripartite or if it is bipartite, it may be known that the negation particle in it is for modification without negation by the word 'غير', for it is not used in Arabic for anything other than modification, since one does not say 'Zaid is not he is just' but rather 'he is unjust', otherwise by intention. As for the aspect of meaning, the negative is more general than the modified affirmative, for the negative may be true with the existence of the subject, as in our saying 'The Most High is not permissible', and with its non-existence, as in our saying 'The partner of God is not permissible', while the modified affirmative is only true with the existence of the subject, so that if one said 'The partner of God is unjust', it would not be true. We only required the subject to exist, not because our saying 'unjust' implies its existence, but because the reality of affirmation is the judgment of the predicate's affirmation of the subject, and judging the affirmation of something for what does not exist is impossible.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and corrective discussion of two types of categorical propositions: the simple negative (السلبية البسيطة) and the modified affirmative (الموجبة المعدولة). The author begins by rejecting a prior view that distinguishes them based on whether the negated attribute could naturally belong to the subject. He provides two counterexamples using syllogisms: one about 'body' and 'substance', another about 'existence of the world'. These show that the prior view leads to false conclusions. The author then gives his own correct definition: the modified affirmative has the negation particle as part of the predicate, whereas the simple negative has negation as part of the copula. He explains the difference in two ways: verbally (the position of the negation particle relative to the copula) and meaningfully (the modified affirmative requires existence of the subject, while the simple negative does not). For the verbal aspect, he notes that in Arabic the word 'غير' is exclusively used for modification, not negation. For the meaning aspect, he illustrates with examples: 'God is not permissible' is true even if the subject exists, while 'The partner of God is unjust' is false because the subject does not exist; affirmation requires a subject that exists in some sense.

Key Terms
سالبة بسيطة
موجبة معدولة
حرف السلب
الرابطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Simple negative: 'The partner of God is not permissible'
B) Modified affirmative: 'The partner of God is unjust'
C) Both A and B are false when the subject does not exist
D) Neither A nor B require existence

Key Takeaway
A simple negative negates the copula and is true even if the subject is non-existent; a modified affirmative has negation in the predicate and requires an existing subject.

The Objection Against the Condition of Existence for Affirmative Judgments
Objector presents a dilemma against the condition of subject existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's dilemma regarding the existence condition for affirmative judgments and identify the two horns of the argument.

Core Idea
The objector uses a dilemma: if existence means external, no judgment possible; if mental, affirmation and negation collapse.

This lesson presents the first part of a dialectical exchange where an opponent challenges the claim that affirmative judgments require an existing subject. You will learn to identify the two horns of the dilemma and the underlying logical moves that set up the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The opponent is responding to the author's earlier statement: that affirmative judgments require the subject to exist. Find this claim in the passage (implied by 'قولكم').
2
Examine the first horn
The opponent says: if you mean external existence, then what is not known (non-existent externally) cannot be judged at all, which contradicts our ability to judge non-existents mentally.
The opponent uses the example of 'universal human is an animal' to show that mental judgments are possible despite external non-existence.
3
Examine the second horn
If you mean mental existence, then both affirmation and negation are valid for mentally existing subjects, so the distinction between simple negatives and modified affirmatives collapses.
The opponent argues that if the subject exists mentally, then both affirmative and negative judgments apply equally, removing the need for the condition.
4
Analyze the additional argument
The opponent adds a further point: for a non-existent subject, either the absence of existential predicates is true or not. If true, the author's condition fails; if not, then the existence of those predicates must be true, which is impossible.
This is a separate logical attack based on the principle of bivalence for existential predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: قولكم السلب) يصح عن المعدوم إن أردتم به المعدوم في الذهن ) . والوجود الخارجي فهو محال، فإن ما لا يكون معلوماً لا يكون محكوماً عليه بسلب ولا إيجاب. وإن أردتم به المعدوم في الأعيان الموجودة في الأذهان فلم يبق بين الإيجاب والسلب فرق؛ ضرورة صحة الحكم بالإيجاب على ما كان موجوداً في الذهن وإن كان معدوماً في الخارج، ولهذا يصح أن يقال (الإنسان الكلي حيوان) وإن كان الكلي لا وجود له في الخارج. وقولكم بأن الحكم الإيجابي لا يكون إلا على موضوع موجود غير صحيح؛ لأن الموضوع المعدوم إما أن يصدق عليه عدم المحمولات الوجودية أو لا يصدق، فإن كان الأول فقد بطل ما ذكرتموه، وإن كان الثاني وجب أن يصدق عليه وجود تلك المحمولات، ويلزم منه اتصاف المعدوم بالموجود وهو محال. قلنا: إما الإشكال الأول فإنما يلزم أن لو صدق قوله: (إن) كل ما لا وجود له في الأذهان لا يكون محكوماً عليه بنفي ولا إثبات وليس كذلك، فإن اجتماع الضدين من المحالات الغير متصورة في الذهن وهي محكوم عليها بالنفي بالبديهة. وأما الإشكال الثاني فباطل أيضاً، فإنا نقول يصدق على الموضوع المعدوم سلب الأمور الوجودية، ولا يصدق عليه إيجاب وجودها ولا عدمها، ولا يلزم من عدم صدق الحكم بعدمها عليه عند الإيجاب صدق الحكم بوجودها عليه؛ لأن وجود الموضوع كما هو شرط في صدق إيجاب المعدومات فهو شرط في صدق إيجاب وجودها، بل أولى، وعند هذا فنعود إلى المقصود من بيان الأعم والأخص
If it is said: Your statement 'negation' is valid for the non-existent if you mean by it the non-existent in the mind. And external existence is impossible, for what is not known cannot be judged by negation or affirmation. And if you mean by it the non-existent among essences that exist in minds, then there remains no difference between affirmation and negation; necessarily, the judgment of affirmation is valid for what exists in the mind even if it is non-existent externally, and for this reason it is valid to say 'The universal human is an animal' even though the universal has no existence externally. And your statement that affirmative judgment is only upon an existing subject is not correct, because the non-existent subject is either such that the absence of existential predicates is true of it or not. If the first, then what you mentioned is invalid; if the second, then the existence of those predicates must be true of it, requiring the non-existent to be characterized by the existent, which is impossible. We say: As for the first problem, it would only follow if the statement 'Everything that has no existence in minds cannot be judged by negation or affirmation' were true, but it is not, for the conjunction of two opposites is among impossible things not conceived in the mind, and they are judged by negation by intuition. As for the second problem, it is also false, for we say: The negation of existential matters is true of the non-existent subject, but the affirmation of their existence or non-existence is not true of it, and the lack of the truth of the judgment of their non-existence regarding it upon affirmation does not entail the truth of the judgment of their existence regarding it, because the existence of the subject, just as it is a condition for the truth of the affirmation of non-existents, is a condition for the truth of the affirmation of their existence, even more so. And at this point we return to the intended goal of explaining the more general and the more specific.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author addresses an objection to his claim that modified affirmatives require the existence of the subject. The objector argues that if 'existence' means external existence, then we cannot judge non-existent things at all, which is false because we do judge them mentally (e.g., 'universal human is an animal'). If 'existence' means mental existence, then both affirmative and negative judgments apply to mentally existing things, so the distinction between simple negatives and modified affirmatives collapses. The objector further claims that for a non-existent subject, either the negation of existential predicates is true or not; if true, the author's view fails; if not, then the affirmation of those predicates must be true, implying the non-existent has existence—a contradiction. The author responds by distinguishing levels: first, he rejects the premise that what is not in the mind cannot be judged, citing that impossible things like the conjunction of opposites are intuitively negated. Second, he argues that for a non-existent subject, only the negation of existential predicates is true (e.g., 'The partner of God is not just'), but neither affirmation nor negation of their existence is applicable because the subject lacks the condition for such judgments. Thus, the modified affirmative's requirement of existence is preserved: it requires mental existence, not external.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
حكم إيجابي

C) The subject must exist externally.
D) The non-existent becomes existent.

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that requiring existence for affirmative judgments leads to a dilemma that must be resolved by distinguishing mental and external existence.

Resolving the Objection: Distinguishing Mental and External Existence
Author's response to the dilemma on existential import

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the author resolves the objection by distinguishing mental existence and showing that negation of existential predicates is possible for non-existent subjects.

Core Idea
The author shows that even impossible concepts can be negated intuitively, and negation of existential predicates does not entail affirmation of existence.

In this lesson, we see how the author systematically dismantles the objector's dilemma by appealing to intuitive negation and clarifying the conditions for affirmative judgments. You will learn the precise logical moves that preserve the requirement of mental existence for affirmative propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two problems the author addresses
The author separates the objection into two 'problems' (إشكالين). Read the response and locate where he addresses each.
The first problem corresponds to the horn about external existence; the second to the horn about mental existence and the additional argument.
2
Analyze the response to the first problem
The author rejects the premise that what has no mental existence cannot be judged. He gives the example of 'the conjunction of two opposites', which is an impossible thing not even conceived in the mind, yet we intuitively judge that it is negated (i.e., it does not exist).
This shows that negation does not require the subject to exist in the mind; even impossible concepts can be negated by intuition.
3
Analyze the response to the second problem
For a non-existent subject, the author argues that negation of existential predicates (like 'is not just') is true, but affirmation of existence ('is just') or negation of existence ('is not not-just') is not applicable because the subject lacks the condition for such judgments. The existence of the subject is a condition for both affirmation of existence and affirmation of non-existence.
The author says: 'we say the negation of existential matters is true of the non-existent subject, but the affirmation of their existence or non-existence is not true of it.'
4
Connect to the original claim
The author concludes that the modified affirmative requires the subject to exist in the mind, but not externally. Thus, the objection is resolved, and the original condition stands.
The lesson ends with a return to the main topic of explaining the more general and more specific (الأعم والأخص).

Why the Negative is More General than the Affirmative Modified
Author argues that the negative is more general than the affirmative modified, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the truth conditions of negative and affirmative modified propositions and explain why the negative is more general in scope.

Core Idea
A negative can be true even if the subject doesn't exist; an affirmative modified cannot.

In this passage, the author compares two types of propositions: the simple negative (e.g., 'Not-P') and the affirmative modified (e.g., 'is not-P'). He argues that the negative has broader applicability because it can be true even when the subject does not exist. This distinction matters when evaluating statements about non-existent entities, such as theological claims about partners of God. We will walk through the author's argument, the objection raised against it, and his response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Locate the author's thesis: 'السالبَة أعم من الموجبة المعدولة' (the negative is more general than the affirmative modified). Understand that 'more general' means the negative is true in more situations: both when the subject exists and when it does not.
The claim is about the range of truth conditions, not about the form of the propositions.
2
Examine the supporting examples
The author gives two examples: 'الباري تعالى ليس بجائز' (the Exalted Creator is not unjust) with an existing subject, and 'شريك الله ليس بجائز' (God's partner is not unjust) with a non-existent subject. Both are true. Then contrast with the affirmative modified: 'شريك الله غير عادل' (God's partner is unjust) is false because the subject does not exist.
Note that the affirmative modified requires subject existence because it affirms a predicate (even if that predicate is negative in phrasing).
3
Analyze the first objection
The objector argues: If by 'non-existent' you mean outside the mind, then negation cannot apply because what is unknown cannot be judged. If you mean non-existent in external reality but present in the mind, then there is no difference between affirmation and negation because both can be judged about mental existents.
This objection tries to collapse the distinction between affirmative and negative judgments regarding non-existent subjects.
4
Understand the author's response to the first objection
The author replies that the objector's premise 'everything not in the mind cannot be judged' is false. For example, the conjunction of two opposites is impossible and not even imagined in the mind, yet we intuitively judge it as impossible (negative judgment). So negation can apply to what is not in the mind.
The author uses a case of impossible concepts to show that negation can operate beyond mental existence.
5
Analyze the second objection
The objector claims: If a non-existent subject can have negations of existential predicates applied, then it would also have to have affirmations of those predicates, leading to the absurdity of the non-existent being qualified by existence. The author responds that negation of existential matters applies, but affirmation does not, because existence of the subject is a condition for any affirmative judgment.
The author maintains that lacking a thing's affirmation does not imply its negation; the condition for affirmation (subject existence) is not met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من جهة المعنى فلأن السالبة أعم من الموجبة المعدولة، إذ السالبة قد تصدق مع وجود الموضوع كقولنا: الباري تعالى ليس بجائز، ومع عدمه كقولنا: شريك الله ليس بجائز، والموجبة المعدولة لا تصدق إلا مع وجود الموضوع حتى لو قيل شريك الله هو غير عادل لم يكن صادقاً، وإنما أوجبنا كون الموضوع موجوداً لا لأن قولنا غير عادل يقتضي وجوده، بل لأن حقيقة الإيجاب هو الحكم بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، والحكم بثبوت شيء لما ليس بثابت محال.
فإن قيل: قولكم السلب يصح عن المعدوم إن أردتم به المعدوم في الذهن، والوجود الخارجي فهو محال، فإن ما لا يكون معلوماً لا يكون محكوماً عليه بسلب ولا إيجاب. وإن أردتم به المعدوم في الأعيان الموجودة في الأذهان فلم يبق بين الإيجاب والسلب فرق؛ ضرورة صحة الحكم بالإيجاب على ما كان موجوداً في الذهن وإن كان معدوماً في الخارج، ولهذا يصح أن يقال الإنسان الكلي حيوان وإن كان الكلي لا وجود له في الخارج.
وقولكم بأن الحكم الإيجابي لا يكون إلا على موضوع موجود غير صحيح؛ لأن الموضوع المعدوم إما أن يصدق عليه عدم المحمولات الوجودية أو لا يصدق، فإن كان الأول فقد بطل ما ذكرتموه، وإن كان الثاني وجب أن يصدق عليه وجود تلك المحمولات، ويلزم منه اتصاف المعدوم بالموجود وهو محال. قلنا: إما الإشكال الأول فإنما يلزم أن لو صدق قوله: إن كل ما لا وجود له في الأذهان لا يكون محكوماً عليه بنفي ولا إثبات وليس كذلك، فإن اجتماع الضدين من المحالات الغير متصورة في الذهن وهي محكوم عليها بالنفي بالبديهة. وأما الإشكال الثاني فباطل أيضاً، فإنا نقول يصدق على الموضوع المعدوم سلب الأمور الوجودية، ولا يصدق عليه إيجاب وجودها ولا عدمها، ولا يلزم من عدم صدق الحكم بعدمها عليه عند الإيجاب صدق الحكم بوجودها عليه؛ لأن وجود الموضوع كما هو شرط في صدق إيجاب المعدومات فهو شرط في صدق إيجاب وجودها، بل أولى.
As for the aspect of meaning, the negative is more general than the affirmative modified, because the negative may be true with the existence of the subject, as in our statement: 'The Exalted Creator is not unjust', and with its non-existence, as in our statement: 'The partner of God is not unjust'. But the affirmative modified is not true except with the existence of the subject, such that if one said 'The partner of God is unjust' it would not be true. We have required that the subject exist not because our statement 'unjust' necessitates its existence, but because the reality of affirmation is the judgment of the predicate's attribution to the subject, and judging the attribution of something to what is not established is impossible. If it is said: Your statement that the negative is valid regarding the non-existent if you mean the non-existent in the mind, then regarding external existence it is impossible, for what is not known cannot be judged by negation or affirmation. And if you mean the non-existent among externally existing things that are in minds, then there is no difference between affirmation and negation due to the necessity of the validity of affirmation regarding what exists in the mind even if it is non-existent externally, and for this reason it is valid to say 'Man in the universal sense is an animal' even though the universal has no external existence. Your statement that affirmative judgment does not occur except on an existing subject is not correct, because the non-existent subject either the negation of existential predicates applies to it or not; if the first, then what you have mentioned is false; if the second, then existence of those predicates must apply to it, necessitating that the non-existent is qualified by existence, which is impossible. We say: As for the first objection, it would only follow if his statement that everything that has no existence in the mind cannot be judged by negation or affirmation were true, but it is not so, for the conjunction of two opposites is an impossibility not imagined in the mind, yet it is judged by negation intuitively. As for the second objection, it is also false, for we say: the negation of existential matters applies to the non-existent subject, but the affirmation of its existence or its non-existence does not apply to it; nor does the lack of the judgment of non-existence upon it in the case of affirmation necessitate the judgment of existence upon it, because the existence of the subject, just as it is a condition for the truth of the affirmation of non-existents, is a condition for the truth of the affirmation of its existence, even more so.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and dialectical discussion comparing the logical scope of negative propositions (saliba) and affirmative modified propositions (mujiba ma'dula). The author begins by stating that the negative is more general (a'am) than the affirmative modified. He supports this with two examples: 'The Creator is not unjust' (where the subject exists) and 'God's partner is not unjust' (where the subject does not exist). The negative is true in both cases. In contrast, the affirmative modified, e.g., 'God's partner is unjust', would be false because the subject 'partner of God' does not exist. The author then clarifies that the requirement of subject existence for affirmative modified is not because the predicate 'unjust' implies existence, but because affirmative judgment requires the subject to be real in some sense. An objection is raised: if by 'non-existent' you mean in the mind, then external existence is irrelevant; but then there is no difference between affirmation and negation since both can be applied to mental existents. The author responds by distinguishing between what is unknown (cannot be judged) and what is impossible (like the conjunction of opposites) which can be judged by negation intuitively. He further argues that the non-existent subject can have negation of existential predicates applied to it, but not affirmation, and this does not entail existence. The passage tightly connects logical theory with metaphysical commitments about existence and judgment.

Key Terms
سالبة
موجبة معدولة
موضوع
حكم إيجابي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true when the subject does not exist?

B) The affirmative modified proposition 'The associate of God is unjust' is true.
C) Both are false.

Key Takeaway
A negative proposition can be true even if the subject does not exist, but an affirmative modified proposition requires subject existence because affirmation is a judgment of attribution that presupposes a real subject.

Classifying Predicates by Opposition and Middle Term
Author classifies predicates by opposition and middle terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any predicate in a categorical proposition based on the presence of an opposite and a middle term.

Core Idea
Predicates are classified by their opposites and whether a middle exists between them.

In Islamic logic, the truth of a categorical proposition depends on the nature of its predicate. This lesson dissects the author's classification: predicates may have opposites, and those opposites may have a middle term (like gray between black and white) or not. Additionally, the subject may contain the opposite potentially. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing truth conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify if predicate has an opposite
Check the predicate (e.g., 'just') and see if it has a direct opposite (e.g., 'unjust'). If it does, proceed; if not, the predicate is simple and classification ends here.
Examples from the passage: 'white' has opposite 'black'; 'blindness' has opposite 'sight'.
2
Check for a middle term between opposites
If there is an opposite, determine whether a middle term exists between them. For example, between white and black there is gray; between blindness and sight there is no middle in the organ that cannot see.
The author mentions cases with and without a middle.
3
Evaluate potentiality in the subject
Consider whether both opposites or neither exist in the subject potentially. For example, in an organ not suitable for sight, blindness and sight are not present even potentially; in the soul, blackness and whiteness are not present potentially.
This step is about the capacity of the subject to receive the opposites.
4
Apply classification to examples
Using the author's examples, classify predicates like 'justice' (has opposite 'injustice', middle 'partially just'?), 'whiteness' (opposite 'blackness', middle 'gray') and note the subject's potentiality.
Practice with examples from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'haram' has opposite 'halal', and there are middle terms like 'makruh'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية
في الصدق والكذب من هذه القضايا وما هو المتقابل منها وما يلزم من صدقه الكذب أو من كذبه الصدق من غير عكس، وقبل هذا فلا بدّ من بيان جهات الصدق والكذب.
فنقول: إن كل قضية لا بد فيها من موضوع و محمول، والموضوع إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً مأخوذاً من حيث هو معدوم، والمحمول إما أن يكون له ضد أو لا يكون له ضد فإن كان له ضد فإما أن يكون بينهما واسطة أو لا يكون، وإن كان بينهما واسطة فإما أن يكونا موجودين في الموضوع بالقوة كـ (العمى والبصر) في الجزء الذي أوانه غير قابل ولا لواحد منها كـ السواد والبياض بالنسبة إلى النفس، فإن كان الموضوع موجوداً وأخذ بإزائه ما يحمل عليه فإما أن يكون هو فيه، أو ضده، أو الواسطة، أو يكونا فيه بالقوة، أو لا يكونا فيه ولا بالقوة، فما كان يصدق من هذه القضايا في ما يصدق فيه غيرها من الجهات وزيادة فهي أعم صدقاً وأخص كذباً، وما كانت أعم كذباً فهي أخص صدقاً، وما كان أخص صدقاً من غيره فمقابله أعم من مقابل ما هو أخص منه كما يأتي. ولما كانت هذه القضايا على ما عرف بالقسمة منها شخصية ومهملة ومحصورة وجب أن نرسم لتحقيق كل قسم منها لوحاً.
اللوح الأول: في بيان أحكام الشخصيات، وليكن وضعه هكذا:
زيد هو عدل
زيد ليس هو عدلاً
يصدق إذا كان عدلاً، وتكذب في ما يكذب في ما يصدق فيه مقابله وهو سوى ذلك، أي عند عدم الموضوع عند وجود العدل، ويصدق في كل ما ووجود الضد والواسطة، وعند كونه يكذب فيه مقابله، فيصدق مع عدم غير موجود في الموضوع بالقوة، وعند الموضوع، ووجود الضد والواسطة، كون الموضوع غير قابل له واعتبر وعند كون الموضوع قابلاً له بالقوة أو أمثلتها بنفسك.
غير قابل
The first art: on categorical propositions. Concerning truth and falsehood of these propositions, what is their opposite, and what follows from its truth as falsehood or from its falsehood as truth without conversion. Before this, it is necessary to explain the aspects of truth and falsehood. We say: Every proposition necessarily has a subject and a predicate. The subject is either existent or non-existent taken as non-existent. The predicate either has an opposite or has no opposite. If it has an opposite, then either there is a middle between them or not. If there is a middle, then either both exist in the subject in potentiality, like blindness and sight in a part that is unsuitable, or neither of them exists, like blackness and whiteness in relation to the soul. If the subject exists and what is predicated of it is taken, then either it is in it, or its opposite, or the middle, or both are in it potentially, or neither in it nor potentially. So, whichever of these propositions is true in what others are true in, with additional aspects, it is more general in truth and more specific in falsehood. Whichever is more general in falsehood is more specific in truth. Whichever is more specific in truth than another, its opposite is more general than the opposite of what is more specific, as will come. Since these propositions, as known by division, are personal, indefinite, and quantified, we must set a table for the verification of each division. The first table: on the rulings of personal propositions, let its arrangement be thus: Zayd is just. Zayd is not just. It is true if he is just, and is false in what its opposite is true, which is other than that, i.e., in the absence of the subject when justice is present, and it is true in every case where its opposite is false, so it is true with the non-existence of what is not existent in the subject potentially, and with the subject, and the existence of the opposite and the middle, and when the subject is not receptive to it, and consider when the subject is receptive to it potentially, or its examples yourself. Not receptive.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of categorical propositions (qadaya hamliyya). The author begins by stating the need to explain the aspects (jihāt) of truth and falsehood before discussing propositions' opposites and reciprocal implications. He then lays out the fundamental elements: every proposition has a subject (mawḍūʿ) and a predicate (maḥmūl). The subject may be existent or non-existent (taken as such). The predicate may have an opposite (ḍidd) or not; if it has an opposite, there may be a middle (wāsiṭa) between them or not. If there is a middle, the two extremes may exist in the subject potentially, as blindness and sight in an unsuitable organ, or neither exists, like blackness and whiteness in relation to the soul (i.e., the soul is receptive to neither). When the subject exists and a predicate is considered, the predicate may be actually in it, its opposite, the middle, both potentially, or neither potentially nor actually. Based on these conditions, propositions vary in generality and specificity of truth and falsehood. The author notes that propositions are divided into personal (shakhṣiyya), indefinite (muhmala), and quantified (maḥṣūra), and he proceeds to set a table for each. The first table covers personal propositions, taking the example 'Zayd is just' and 'Zayd is not just'. The explanation describes the truth conditions: 'Zayd is just' is true when he is just, false when its opposite ('Zayd is not just') is true, i.e., when the subject is absent but justice is present? (The text is somewhat corrupt). It further states truth conditions involving the opposite, the middle, potentiality, etc. The passage serves as a structured introduction to the logic of categorical propositions, preparing the ground for later discussion of conversion and syllogisms.

Key Terms
قضية حملية
ضد
واسطة
شخصية

Key Takeaway
Predicates are classified by the presence of opposites and middle terms, which determines their logical behavior in categorical propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Truth Conditions of Personal Propositions: The Example of Zayd
Author sets table for personal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth conditions of a personal proposition based on the subject's existence and the predicate's opposite, as illustrated in the table for 'Zayd is just'.

Core Idea
A personal proposition is true when the subject exists and the predicate applies, but its truth also depends on the opposite and middle terms.

The author provides a detailed table for personal propositions, using the example 'Zayd is just' and its negation. This lesson unpacks that table, showing how truth and falsehood vary with subject existence, predicate opposition, and potentiality. Understanding this table is crucial for analyzing singular statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition and its negation
The author uses 'Zayd is just' and 'Zayd is not just'. Write down both forms. Recognise that 'just' has an opposite 'unjust', and there may be a middle term (e.g., partially just).
The table applies to any personal proposition, but the example is concrete.
2
Determine the opposite and middle for the predicate
For 'just', the opposite is 'unjust'. Determine if there is a middle term between just and unjust (e.g., moderate justice?). The author's classification applies here.
This step uses the classification from the previous lesson.
3
Consider subject existence
The subject 'Zayd' may exist or not exist. The truth conditions depend on this. According to the table, 'Zayd is just' is true if Zayd is just (exists and has justice). It is false when its opposite is true, i.e., when Zayd does not exist but justice exists? The text is ambiguous but indicates truth in some cases of non-existence.
The table also mentions truth when the opposite is false, etc. Carefully parse the conditions.
4
Apply the truth conditions to both affirmative and negative
For 'Zayd is not just', the passage says 'it is true in every case where its opposite is false'. That is, when 'Zayd is just' is false, the negation is true. Also consider cases of potentiality and non-receptivity.
The table seems to outline the truth conditions for both forms in a compact manner.

Parsing Truth Conditions for Categorical Propositions
Author defines truth conditions based on subject existence and predicate opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the factors that determine truth and falsehood of a categorical proposition according to the author, and identify which proposition is more general in truth based on those factors.

Core Idea
Truth of a proposition depends on subject existence, predicate opposition, and the presence of a middle.

In this passage, the author unpacks the logical foundations of truth and falsehood before discussing propositional relations. He systematically breaks down the components: subject existence, predicate with or without an opposite, and the possibility of a middle ground (wāsiṭa). By understanding these modalities, you can compare propositions by their truth scope and prepare for the square of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
Every proposition (qaḍiyya) has a subject (mawḍūʿ) and a predicate (maḥmūl). Note that these are the basic building blocks.
2
Classify the subject's existence
The subject is either existent (mawjūd) or non-existent (maʿdūm), taken as non-existent. This affects truth conditions.
Non-existent subjects are considered in logic, e.g., 'The phoenix is immortal' can be judged.
3
Examine the predicate's opposition
Determine if the predicate has an opposite (ḍidd). If it does, check if there is a medium (wāsiṭa) between them. Examples: blindness and sight have a medium (dim sight?), but black and white have no medium between them in some contexts.
The author gives examples: blindness and sight have a medium (the limb not receptive), black and white in relation to the soul have no medium.
4
Enumerate the possible states
When the subject exists, the predicate can be in it, its opposite, the medium, both potentially, or neither actually nor potentially. List these five states.
5
Apply the generality rules
A proposition that is true in all cases where another is true and more is 'more general in truth' (aʿam ṣidqan) and 'more specific in falsehood' (akhaṣ kadhiban). Conversely, a proposition more general in falsehood is more specific in truth. Compare two propositions using these criteria.
This prepares for the square of opposition as the author says 'as will come'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الصدق والكذب من هذه القضايا وما هو المتقابل منها وما يلزم من صدقه الكذب أو من كذبه الصدق من غير عكس، وقبل هذا فلا بدّ من بيان جهات الصدق والكذب. فنقول: إن كل قضية لا بد فيها من موضوع و محمول، والموضوع إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً مأخوذاً من حيث هو معدوم، والمحمول إما أن يكون له ضد أو لا يكون له ضد فإن كان له ضد فإما أن يكون بينهما واسطة أو لا يكون، وإن كان بينهما واسطة فإما أن يكونا موجودين في الموضوع بالقوة كـ (العمى والبصر) في الجزء الذي أوانه غير قابل ولا لواحد منها كـ السواد) والبياض بالنسبة إلى النفس، فإن كان الموضوع موجوداً وأخذ بإزائه ما يحمل عليه فإما أن يكون هو فيه، أو ضده، أو الواسطة، أو يكونا فيه بالقوة، أو لا يكونا فيه ولا بالقوة، فما كان يصدق من هذه القضايا في ما يصدق فيه غيرها من الجهات وزيادة فهي أعم صدقاً وأخص كذباً، وما كانت أعـم كذباً فهي أخص صدقاً، وما كان أخص صدقاً من غيره فمقابله أعم من مقابل ما هو أخص منه كما يأتي.
On truth and falsehood of these propositions, and what is their contrary, and what follows from its truth the falsehood or from its falsehood the truth without inversion, and before that we must explain the modalities of truth and falsehood. We say: Every proposition must have a subject and a predicate. The subject is either existent or non-existent, taken insofar as it is non-existent. The predicate either has an opposite or does not have an opposite. If it has an opposite, then either there is a medium between them or not. And if there is a medium between them, then either both exist in the subject potentially, like (blindness and sight) in the limb that is not receptive to either, like blackness and whiteness in relation to the soul. Then, if the subject is existent and what is predicated upon it is taken, then either it is in it, or its opposite, or the medium, or both exist in it potentially, or neither exists in it actually nor potentially. So whatever among these propositions is true in what another is true in and more, that is more general in truth and more specific in falsehood. And whatever is more general in falsehood, it is more specific in truth. And whatever is more specific in truth than another, its opposite is more general than the opposite of that which is more specific than it, as will come.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of truth conditions for categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that before discussing truth and falsehood and the logical relations between propositions, we must explain the 'modalities' (جهات) of truth and falsehood. He then breaks down the components: every proposition has a subject and predicate. The subject can be existent or non-existent (considered as non-existent). The predicate may or may not have an opposite. If it does, there may or may not be a middle ground (واسطة). He gives examples: blindness and sight cannot coexist in a limb that is not capable of sight; similarly blackness and whiteness cannot be in the soul simultaneously. Then he lists the possible states: the subject exists and the predicate truly applies (هو فيه), or its opposite applies, or the middle applies, or both are potential, or neither is actual nor potential. From these, he derives a principle: a proposition that is true in all cases where another is true and more is 'more general in truth and more specific in falsehood'. Conversely, a proposition that is more general in falsehood is more specific in truth. This is a precursor to the square of opposition. The passage ends with 'as will come,' indicating this is setting up the tables.

Key Terms
جهات الصدق والكذب
قضية
أعم صدقاً
أخص كذباً
ضد
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does it mean for a proposition to be 'أعم صدقاً' (more general in truth) than another?
A) It is true in all cases where the other is true and more.
B) It is true only when the other is false.
C) It has a broader subject term.
D) It is more likely to be true in real-world situations.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a categorical proposition is determined by the existence of its subject and the relation of its predicate to that subject, including whether the predicate has an opposite and a middle ground, and these conditions allow comparison of generality in truth and falsehood.

Propositions
intermediate
15 min
Logical Relations among Six Proposition Types
Author defines truth conditions and entailments for six categorical proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six types of categorical propositions (simple, modified, privative) and determine the truth entailments among them using the rules of subalternation and opposition.

Core Idea
Six proposition types form a square of opposition with distinct subaltern and diagonal entailments.

This lesson unpacks the logical relations among simple, modified, and privative categorical propositions. You will learn how their truth conditions interact, which ones are contradictory, which are subalterns, and how diagonal pairs relate. Mastering these relations is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially when analyzing theological and legal statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six proposition types
Learn the definitions and examples: simple affirmative ('Zaid is just'), simple negative ('Zaid is not just'), modified affirmative ('Zaid is non-just'), modified negative ('Zaid is not non-just'), privative affirmative ('Zaid is unjust'), privative negative ('Zaid is not unjust').
2
Understand truth conditions
Note the truth conditions given: e.g., 'Zaid is not non-just' is true whether Zaid exists or not, but false when justice exists. These conditions determine the relations.
Focus on the examples in the passage.
3
Find contradictory opposites (horizontal)
On each horizontal line, an affirmative and its corresponding negative are contradictories: they cannot be both true or both false. For example, the simple affirmative is opposite to the simple negative.
See the passage: 'كل طبقة ... فمتقابلان لا يجتمعان في الصدق ولا في الكذب'.
4
Map vertical subaltern relations (right and left)
On the right side (simple affirmative → modified negative → privative negative), each lower proposition is more general in truth (true in more cases) than the one above. On the left side (simple negative → modified affirmative → privative affirmative), the top is more general. Study the entailments given.
The passage lists entailments like 'from truth of simple affirmative follows truth of modified negative'.
5
Analyze diagonal relations
The diagonal between the simple affirmative (top right) and the two affirmatives on the left (modified and privative) is contrariety: they cannot both be true but can both be false. Similarly, the diagonal between modified negative and privative affirmative is contrariety. The diagonal between simple negative (top left) and the two negatives on the right is subcontrariety: cannot both be false but can both be true.
The passage explains these with examples like the stone being neither blind nor sighted.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the logical relationships among six types of categorical propositions: simple affirmative (الموجبة البسيطة) like 'Zaid is just', simple negative (السالبة البسيطة) like 'Zaid is not just', modified affirmative (الموجبة المعدولة) like 'Zaid is non-just', modified negative (السالبة المعدولة) like 'Zaid is not non-just', privative affirmative (الموجبة العدمية) like 'Zaid is unjust', and privative negative (السالبة العدمية) like 'Zaid is not unjust'. The author begins with examples illustrating truth conditions. For instance, 'Zaid is not non-just' (a modified negative) is true whether Zaid exists or not, but false when justice exists, etc. Then he establishes the horizontal contradictory opposites: each affirmative has its corresponding negative as its contradictory, and they cannot both be true or false together. Next, he explains vertical (subaltern) relations on the right and left sides. On the right side (starting from the top: simple affirmative, then modified negative, then privative negative), each lower proposition is more general in truth than the one above (i.e., true in more cases) and more specific in falsehood. So the privative negative is more general in truth than the modified negative, which is more general in truth than the simple affirmative. On the left side (simple negative, modified affirmative, privative affirmative), the relation is reversed: the higher proposition is more general, so the simple negative is more general in truth than the modified affirmative, and so on. From these subaltern relations, entailments follow: e.g., truth of the simple affirmative entails truth of the modified negative, and falsehood of the privative negative entails falsehood of the modified negative. The author then discusses diagonal relations. He shows that the diagonal between the simple affirmative (top right) and the modified and privative affirmatives (left side) is a relation of contrariety: they cannot both be true at the same time, but they can both be false (when the subject does not exist). Similarly, the diagonal between the modified negative and privative affirmative is also contrariety: they cannot both be true (due to entailments) but can both be false (when the subject is neither, e.g., a stone is neither blind nor sighted). The diagonal between the simple negative (top left) and the modified and privative negatives is the opposite: they cannot both be false at the same time (subcontrariety), i.e., they can both be true but not both false. This is because the simple negative is more general in truth, so its falsehood entails falsehood of its subalterns, etc. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear: the author is laying out the full square of opposition for these six propositions.

السالبة البسيطة
الموجبة المعدولة
السالبة المعدولة
الموجبة العدمية
السالبة العدمية

Key Takeaway
The six proposition types form a square of opposition with specific subaltern and diagonal relations that govern their logical entailments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (simple affirmative), determine the truth values of the modified negative ('The world is not non-temporally originated') and the privative negative ('The world is not nontemporal') assuming the original is true. Explain using the subaltern relations from the passage.
?
Task 2: In a debate about the attributes of Allah, a mutakallim states 'Allah is not unjust' (privative negative). What does this entail about the truth of 'Allah is not non-just' (modified negative) and 'Allah is just' (simple affirmative) according to the passage's rules?

Propositions

Truth Conditions for Quantified Propositions
Author exhaustively lists subject modes for truth analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the modes of the subject that must be considered when determining the truth of a quantified proposition, and explain why this enumeration is necessary.

Core Idea
The truth of quantified propositions depends on the modes of the subject's parts.

The previous lesson introduced quantified propositions (universals and particulars). Now the author deepens the analysis: understanding when a universal or particular is true requires looking beyond the mere quantifier. You must examine the subject term itself, because its parts may exist in different modes (e.g., potential vs. actual, just vs. unjust). This lesson unpacks the exhaustive list of possible combinations that a logician must consider before declaring a proposition true or false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author states that quantified propositions need an expansion (بسط) of the aspects of truth and falsehood in all or some parts of the subject. This means truth evaluation is not just about the quantifier but about the internal composition of the subject.
The phrase 'أما المحصورات فتفتقر في بيان العموم والخصوص فيها إلى بسط ما أشرنا إليه' signals a shift to a more detailed analysis.
2
List the basic modes of the subject
The passage enumerates modes: عادلاً (just), جائراً (unjust), مختلطاً (mixed), بالقوة (potentially), لا بالقوة ولا بالفعل وهو موجود (neither potential nor actual but existing), لا بالقوة ولا بالفعل وهو معدوم (neither potential nor actual and non-existent). These describe states of each part of the subject.
The exact meaning of some terms is uncertain due to OCR corruption, but the logical point is that the subject's parts can have different ontological or qualitative statuses.
3
Recognize the combinatorial enumeration
The author then lists many combinations: all parts are just, all are unjust, some just and some mixed, etc. This shows that to determine truth, you must consider every possible distribution of modes across the parts of the subject.
This is an exhaustive enumeration typical of Islamic logic manuals when analyzing conditions for a proposition to hold.
4
Connect to truth conditions of quantified statements
For a universal affirmative 'Every S is P' to be true, every part of S must be P, but 'P' may apply differently depending on the mode. For example, if some parts are potential and others actual, the truth of the proposition may require that P holds in both modes. This analysis prevents hasty judgments.
In Islamic logic, such detailed treatment is especially important for modal propositions and for arguments in kalam about the attributes of God or created things.

As for quantified propositions, they require, in explaining generality and particularity in them, an expansion of what we indicated regarding the aspects of truth and falsehood in all parts of the subject or some of them. For it is either that it is all just, or all unjust, or all mixed, or all potential, or all neither potential nor actual and existing, or all neither potential nor actual and non-existent, or some of it just, or some of it unjust, or some of it just and some of it mixed, or some of it just and some of it potential, or some of it just and some of it neither potential nor actual and existing, or some of it just and some of it neither potential nor actual and non-existent, or some of it just and some of it unjust and some of it mixed, or some of it just and some of it unjust and both potential, or some of it just and some of it unjust and some of it potential and existing, or some of it just and some of it unjust and some of it neither potential nor actual and non-existent, or some of it just and some of it mixed and some of it potential, or some of it just and some of it mixed and some of it neither potential nor actual and existing, or some of it just and some of it mixed and some of it neither potential nor actual and non-existent, or some of it just in potential and some of it neither potential nor actual and existing, or some of it just and some of it potential and some of it neither potential nor actual and non-existent.

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of analysis for quantified propositions in logic. The author states that to understand the relations of generality and particularity among quantified propositions, one must expand upon the aspects of truth and falsehood in each part of the subject or some of its parts. The subject can be categorized into various modes: just, unjust, mixed, potential, actual, non-existent, etc. The enumeration lists possible combinations of these modes across the subject's parts. This detailed breakdown is necessary for determining the truth conditions of propositions like 'All S are P' or 'Some S are not P', especially when the subject is not homogeneous. In the Islamic logical tradition, such analysis is crucial for establishing the logical relations (e.g., subalternation, contrariety) in the square of opposition, particularly when dealing with modal or material aspects.

Key Terms
المحصورات (al-mahṣūrāt) - quantified propositions
الموضوع (al-mawḍūʿ) - subject
الصدق والكذب (al-ṣidq wa al-kadhib) - truth and falsehood
بالقوة (bi-l-quwwa) - potentially
عادلاً (ʿādilan) - just (possibly a corruption for a technical term)

Key Takeaway
Truth conditions for quantified propositions depend on a detailed modal analysis of the subject's parts, not just the quantifier.

Contradictory of Universal Affirmative Propositions
Author presents a table of universal affirmative propositions with their contradictory particular negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of a universal affirmative proposition in simple form and verify that they have opposite truth values.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative and its particular negative are contradictories, never both true or both false.

The author presents the fundamental logical relation of contradiction for universal affirmative propositions. Using the example 'Every human is just', he shows that its contradictory is the particular negative 'Not every human is just'. Understanding this relation is essential for all logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the given proposition as universal affirmative
Check the quantity and quality: the proposition uses the universal quantifier 'every' (kull) and is affirmative (is). Example: 'Every human is just' is universal affirmative.
2
Form the contradictory
Keep the same subject and predicate, but change quantity to particular and quality to negative. For 'Every human is just', the contradictory is 'Not every human is just' (particular negative).
3
Verify truth value opposition
If the universal affirmative is true (all subjects have the property), the contradictory is false; if the universal affirmative is false (not all have the property), the contradictory is true. This one-to-one opposite truth holds for all contradictories.

Source Text
