ولما كانت المحصورات إما متناقضة أو متضادة أو داخلة تحت المتضادة وكانت أحكامها مما تختلف وجب أن نرسم لبيان كل قسم منها لوحاً، ولتكن البداية بالمتناقضة منها، وهي إما أن تكون الكليات منها موجبات أو سوالب فإن كانت موجبات فأحكامها في التقابل والتعاند والتلازم طولاً وعرضاً وقطراً كأحكام الشخصية هكذا: كل إنسان هو عدل: موجبة كليّة بسيطة تصدق إذا كان الكل عادلين وتكذب في البواقي. ليس كل إنسان عدلاً: سالبة جزئية بسيطة تكذب إذا كان الكل عادلين وتصدق في البواقي. كل إنسان هو غير عادل: كلية موجبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم معدومين أو بعضهم عادلاً أو كلهم عادلين وتكذب في ما وراء ذلك. ليس كل إنسان هو غير عادل: جزئية سالبة معدولة تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم معدومين أو بعضهم عادلاً أو كلهم عادلين وتصدق في ما سوى ذلك. كل إنسان هو جائر: كلية موجبة عدمية تصدق إذا كان الكل جائرين، وتكذب في البواقي. ليس كل إنسان جائراً: جزئية سالبة عدمية تكذب إذا كان الكل جائرين، وتصدق في البواقي. واعتبر أحكامها بنفسك تجده على ما أسلفناه في الشخصيات.
And since the restricted [propositions] are either contradictory or contrary or subcontrary, and their rulings differ, it was necessary to draw a table for each type for clarification. Let the beginning be with the contradictories. They are either universal affirmatives or universal negatives. If they are affirmatives, then their rulings in opposition, contrariety, and implication vertically, horizontally, and diagonally are like the rulings of singulars. Thus: Every human is just: universal affirmative simple, true if all are just, false otherwise. Not every human is just: particular negative simple, false if all are just, true otherwise. Every human is non-just: universal affirmative modified, false if they are all non-existent or some just or all just, and false otherwise. Not every human is non-just: particular negative modified, true if they are all non-existent or some just or all just, and true otherwise. Every human is unjust: universal affirmative privative, true if all are unjust, false otherwise. Not every human is unjust: particular negative privative, false if all are unjust, true otherwise. And consider their rulings yourself, you will find it as we have explained for singulars.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the logical square for universal affirmative propositions and their contradictory particular negatives. The author first introduces the classification of restricted propositions (المحصورات) into contradictory (متناقضة), contrary (متضادة), and subcontrary (داخلة تحت المتضادة). He then focuses on the contradictory pairings for universal affirmatives. Using the example of 'human' and 'just', he presents three types of universal affirmatives: simple (بسيطة), modified (معدولة) with a negated predicate, and privative (عدمية) with a privative predicate. For each, he gives the truth condition (صدق/كذب) and states the contradictory particular negative. The pattern is that the universal affirmative and its contradictory particular negative have opposite truth values in all cases. The author notes that this parallels the rules for singular propositions discussed earlier.

Three Kinds of Universal Affirmative Propositions
Author presents a table of universal affirmative propositions with their contradictory particular negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple, modified, and privative universal affirmative propositions and recall their truth conditions as given by the author.

Core Idea
Universal affirmatives vary by predicate type, changing truth conditions.

The author classifies universal affirmative propositions into three kinds based on the predicate: simple (بسيطة), modified (معدولة), and privative (عدمية). Each has a distinct truth condition. This classification helps in understanding how propositions relate in the logical square and is used in Islamic logic to analyze statements about attributes.

Guided Path
1
Examine the predicate of the universal affirmative
Look at the predicate term: if it is a simple attribute (e.g., 'just'), the proposition is simple. If it is a negated attribute (e.g., 'non-just'), it is modified. If it indicates the absence of a quality (e.g., 'unjust'), it is privative.
In Arabic, the modified form uses 'غير' (non-) while the privative uses a different word or pattern indicating lack.
2
Apply the truth condition from the author
For simple: true if all subjects have the attribute, false otherwise. For modified: false if subjects are non-existent or any have the attribute (i.e., false if any subject is just), and false otherwise as the author states. For privative: true if all subjects have the privation (e.g., all are unjust), false otherwise.
The author gives specific truth tables; memorize them for each type.
3
Note the pattern for contradictories
Each universal affirmative has a corresponding particular negative of the same type. For example, the contradictory of the modified universal affirmative 'Every human is non-just' is the modified particular negative 'Not every human is non-just'.

Relations of Simple and Modal Negatives
Author contrasts the affirmative and negative universal cases for simple-modal relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a universal negative simple proposition is more general, more specific, or in a relation of contrariety with its corresponding modified and privative negatives.

Core Idea
In negative universals, the simple proposition may be more general or more specific than its modal forms.

In the affirmative case, the simple proposition is always more general than the modified and privative, and they are contraries. But when the universal is negative, this relation reverses: the simple negative may be either more general or more specific than the modified and privative negatives, and no contrariety holds between them. This lesson unpacks this asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Recall the affirmative rule
For affirmative universals, e.g., 'Every human is just' (simple) is more general than 'Every human is non-just' (modified) and 'Every human is unjust' (privative). They are contraries: both cannot be true, but both can be false.
2
Identify the negative forms
Write the three negative forms: simple negative = 'No S is P'; modified negative = 'No S is not-P'; privative negative = 'No S is non-P'. Notice that the modified and privative negatives are logically equivalent to 'Every S is P' (the simple affirmative).
3
Compare the simple negative to the modal negatives
Since the modal negatives are equivalent to the simple affirmative, the relation between the simple negative and the modal negatives mirrors the relation between 'No S is P' and 'Every S is P'. These are contradictories, so one is true when the other is false. Therefore, the simple negative may be more general (if the simple affirmative is false) or more specific (if the simple affirmative is true) depending on the subject and predicate.
Example: For 'No human is a prophet', the simple negative is true; the modified negative ('No human is not-a-prophet' = 'Every human is a prophet') is false. Here the simple negative is more general (applies to all humans, while the modal applies to none).
4
Determine contrariety
Because the simple negative and the modal negatives are logically equivalent to contradictory pairs, they cannot be both true or both false at the same time. Hence there is no contrariety (ta'anud) in truth or falsehood between them. This is the key point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت الكليات منها السوالب فالحكم في نسبة البسيطتين إلى المعدولتين والعدميتين، وفي نسبة الموجبة البسيطة قطراً إلى الموجبتين المعدولة والعدمية وفي نسبة السالبة البسيطة قطراً إلى السالبتين المعدولة والعدمية بخلاف ما حققناه، إذ البسيطتان هاهنا قد تكون أعم مما تحتها، وقد تكون أخص كما تعرفه فلا السالبة البسيطة أعم مما تحتها ولا مقابلها أخص مما تحته، ولزم ألا تعاند لا في الصدق ولا في الكذب بين كل واحد من البسيطين وما تحت مقابله، وأما ما وراء ذلك من الأحكام طولاً وعرضاً.
And if the universals among them are negatives, then the ruling regarding the relation of the two simples to the two modifieds and the two privatives, and regarding the relation of the simple affirmative diagonally to the two (modified and privative) affirmatives, and regarding the relation of the simple negative diagonally to the two (modified and privative) negatives, is contrary to what we established, because the two simples here may be more general than what is under them, and may be more specific as you know; so the simple negative is not more general than what is under it, nor is its opposite more specific than what is under it, and it follows that there is no contrariety in truth or falsehood between each of the two simples and what is under its opposite. And beyond that, [there are] rulings vertically and horizontally.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the relations between simple, modified, and privative propositions when the universal is negative. The author explains that unlike the affirmative case, the simple negative (e.g., 'No human is just') is not necessarily more general than the modified negative (e.g., 'No human is non-just') or the privative negative (e.g., 'No human is unjust'). In fact, the simple may be more general or more specific. Consequently, there is no contrariety (تعاند) in truth or falsehood between the simple and the modified/privative under its opposite. The author implies that the full set of vertical and horizontal relations follows from this principle, but does not enumerate them here.

Key Takeaway

Truth Conditions of Particular Propositions
Author enumerates particular propositions with truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of particular propositions (simple, modified, privative) by applying the conditions given in the text.

Core Idea
The truth of a particular proposition hinges on just one instance of the predicate, but modified and privative predicates shift what counts as an instance.

In this lesson, we examine the detailed truth conditions for six propositions presented by the author. These include three particular affirmatives and their universal negative contradictories. By understanding these conditions, you will be able to analyze any such proposition systematically. This knowledge is foundational for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition form
Look at the predicate: if it is positive (just) it is simple; if it is negated (non-just) it is modified; if it is privative (unjust) it is privative. Also note whether it is affirmative or negative and universal or particular.
The passage uses 'just', 'non-just', 'unjust' as examples.
2
State the truth condition for simple particular
For 'Some people are just', it is true if at least one person is just, regardless of the rest. It is false only if no one is just.
This is the simplest case.
3
Understand modified particular
For 'Some people are non-just' (modified), the predicate is 'non-just', meaning the property of not being just. It is true if at least one person is not just. It is false only if everyone is just (or non-existent).
Note that 'non-just' includes both unjust and average.
4
Understand privative particular
For 'Some people are unjust' (privative), the predicate is 'unjust', a specific contrary to just. It is true if at least one person is unjust. It is false only if no one is unjust (i.e., all are just or average or non-existent).
Privative predicates often have narrower truth conditions than modified.
5
Relate to the contradictory universal
Each particular affirmative has a universal negative as its contradictory. Their truth values are opposite. For example, 'No people are just' is the contradictory of 'Some people are just'. If one is true, the other is false.
Use this to double-check truth conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعض الناس هو عادل: موجبة جزئية بسيطة تصدق إذا كان البعض عادلاً والآخرون كيف كانوا، وتكذب إذا لم يكن من الناس عادلاً كيف كانوا.
لا واحد من الناس هو غير عادل: كليّة سالبة معدولة تصدق إذا كان الكل معدومين أو عادلين، وتكذب في البواقي.
لا واحد من الناس جائراً: كليّة سالبة عدمية تصدق إذا كان الكل جائرين، وتكذب في البواقي.
لا واحد من الناس عادلاً: كلية سالبة بسيطة تكذب في ما يصدق نقيضها وهو إذا كان بعضهم والباقي كيف كانوا، وتصدق في ما يكذب فيه نقيضها وهو إذا لم يكن فيهم عادلاً كيف كانوا.
بعض الناس هو غير عادل: موجبة جزئية معدولة تكذب في ما يصدق فيه نقيضها وهو إذا كان الكل عادلاً أو معدوماً، وتصدق في البواقي وهو ما كذب فيه نقيضها.
بعض الناس جائر: موجبة جزئية عدمية تصدق في ما يكذب فيه نقيضها وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين أو كلهم متوسطين أو معدومين أو بعضهم جائرين، وتكذب في البواقي وهو ما يصدق فيه نقيضها.

None of the people is unjust: universal negative privative, true if all are unjust, false otherwise. Some people are non-just: particular affirmative modified, false in what its contradictory is true, i.e., if all are just or non-existent, and true otherwise, i.e., what its contradictory is false. Truth Conditions of Simple, Modified, and Privative Propositions
Author defines truth conditions for each type of categorical proposition and their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify categorical propositions as simple, modified, or privative and determine their truth conditions based on the subject's existential and predicate state.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth depends on whether it is simple, modified, or privative—not just on subject-predicate correspondence.

In this passage, the author lays out the precise truth conditions for three types of categorical propositions: simple (بسيطة), modified (معدولة), and privative (عدمية). Unlike the standard square of opposition, these propositions have nuanced relations where the simple proposition can be either more general or more specific than its modified and privative counterparts. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly evaluating propositions in logical arguments, especially in Islamic sciences where such distinctions arise in definitions and rulings.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three types
First, understand the difference: simple (بسيطة) affirms or denies a predicate without modification (e.g., 'Zayd is just'); modified (معدولة) uses a negated predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is not-just'); privative (عدمية) uses a privative predicate indicating absence (e.g., 'Zayd is unjust' meaning lacking justice). Note the shift in meaning between negated and privative.
The author uses 'just' (عادل) and 'unjust' (جائر) as examples.
2
Identify the quantity and quality
Each proposition can be universal (كلية) or particular (جزئية), affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة). For example, 'Some people are just' is a particular simple affirmative. 'No person is unjust' is a universal negative modified.
The passage provides examples for each.
3
Apply the truth condition from the passage
Using the author's definitions: For a particular simple affirmative like 'Some people are just', it is true if at least one person is just (others can be anything), false if no one is just. For a universal negative modified like 'No person is unjust', it is true if all people are nonexistent or all are just; false otherwise. Pay attention to the existential condition: the modified proposition covers nonexistent subjects.
The author explicitly states these for each example given.
4
Practice with the remaining types
Repeat for the privative propositions. For instance, a privative negative (سالبة عدمية) like 'No person is unjust' (though the passage uses a different example) would be true when all are just or nonexistent, false otherwise. The passage indicates the privative negative is more general in truth than the modified negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية. وإن كانت الكليات منها السوالب فالحكم في نسبة البسيطتين إلى المعدولتين والعدميتين، وفي نسبة الموجبة البسيطة قطراً إلى الموجبتين المعدولة والعدمية وفي نسبة السالبة البسيطة قطراً إلى السالبتين المعدولة والعدمية بخلاف ما حققناه، إذ البسيطتان هاهنا قد تكون أعم مما تحتها، وقد تكون أخص كما تعرفه فلا السالبة البسيطة أعم مما تحتها ولا مقابلها أخص مما تحته، ولزم ألا تعاند لا في الصدق ولا في الكذب بين كل واحد من البسيطين وما تحت مقابله، وأما ما وراء ذلك من الأحكام طولاً وعرضاً. بعض الناس هو عادل: موجبة جزئية بسيطة تصدق إذا كان البعض عادلاً والآخرون كيف كانوا، وتكذب إذا لم يكن من الناس عادلاً كيف كانوا. لا واحد من الناس هو غير عادل: كلية سالبة معدولة تصدق إذا كان الكل معدومين أو عادلين، وتكذب في البواقي. لا واحد من الناس جائراً فيه عادلاً: لا واحد من الناس عادلاً كلية سالبة بسيطة تكذب في ما يصدق نقيضها وهو إذا كان بعضهم والباقي كيف كانوا وتصدق في ما يكذب فيه نقيضها وهو إذا لم يكن فيهم عادلاً كيف كانوا. بعض الناس هو غير عادل موجبة جزئية معدولة تكذب في ما يصدق فيه نقيضها وهو إذا كان الكل عادلاً أو معدوماً، وتصدق في البواقي وهو ما كذب فيه نقيضها. بعض الناس جائر: سالبة كلية عدمية تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم موجبة جزئية عدمية تصدق في ما أو بعضهم جائرين والباقي كيف كانوا، يكذب فيه نقيضها وهو ما إذا كانوا وتصدق إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين أو كلهم أو بعضهم جائرين، وتكذب في متوسطين أو معدومين أو خلطاً. البواقي وهو ما يصدق فيه نقيضها. وقطراً فعلى ما عرف، وليكن اللوح المفروض لهذه القضايا هكذا: وهذه الطبقات وإن كانت كل واحدة منها مقابلة لما سامتها عرضاً فلما كانت الموجبة البسيطة والسالبتان تحتها قد تشترك في الصدق عند كون الكل عادلين وتصدق البسيطة إذا كان البعض عادلاً فقط وتكذب فيه السالبتان، وتصدق السالبتان إذا كان الكل معدومين وتكذب فيه الموجبة البسيطة إذا كانت الموجبة أعم منها من وجه وأخص من وجه وكانت السالبة البسيطة المقابلة للموجبة البسيطة في نسبتها إلى ما تحتها أيضاً كذلك، إذ قد بينا أن ما كان أعم من غيره فمقابله أخص من مقابل ذلك الغير، وما كان أخص من غيره فمقابله أعم من مقابل ذلك الغير فمن جهة أن الموجبة البسيطة أخص مما تحتها يجب أن يكون مقابلها أعم من مقابل ما تحتها، ومن جهة أنها أعم مما تحتها يجب أن يكون مقابلها أخص من مقابل ما تحتها، ولزم أن لا يمتنع الجمع في الصدق من الموجبة البسيطة وما سامتها قطراً من الموجبتين، ولا الجمع في الكذب بين السالبة البسيطة وما سامتها قطراً من السالبتين لأن ذلك إنما كان في ما تقدم لضرورة أن الموجبة البسيطة والسالبة متقابلتان، وأن ما تحت الموجبة فأعم منها، وما تحت السالبة فأخص منها، ومن ضرورة صدق الأخص صدق الأعم، ومن كذب الأعمّ كذب الأخص، فلو تصور الاجتماع بين الموجبات في الصدق لكانت السالبة البسيطة صادقة مع صدق ما قابلها من الموجبة البسيطة، ضرورة أن الموجبة المعدولة والعدمية أخص من السالبة البسيطة، وللزم من ذلك صدق ما قابل الموجبتين المعدولة والعدمية، مع فرض صدقهما؛ لأن ما قابلها أعم مما فرض صدقه مع صدقها من الموجبة البسيطة، ويلزم من صدق الأخص صدق الأعم، وعلى هذا النحو كان امتناع اجتماع السوالب في الكذب. وأما هاهنا فقد بان أنه ليست الموجبة البسيطة أخص مما تحتها، ولا مقابلها أعم مما تحته، وأما السالبة العدمية فأعمّ صدقاً من السالبة المعدولة؛ إذ هي في ما تصدق فيه السالبة المعدولة وهو إذا كان الكل معدومين أو عادلين، وتصدق في ما لا تصدق فيه السالبة المعدولة وهو إذا كان الكل متوسطين أو بالقوة أو كلهم تصدق.
The first art: On categorical propositions. And if the universals among them are negatives, then the ruling regarding the ratio of the two simple to the two modified and the two privative, and regarding the ratio of the simple affirmative diagonally to the two modified and privative affirmatives, and regarding the ratio of the simple negative diagonally to the two modified and privative negatives, is contrary to what we verified, for the two simples here may be more general than what is under them, and may be more specific, as you know. So the simple negative is not more general than what is under it, nor is its opposite more specific than what is under it. Consequently, there is no contradiction in truth nor in falsehood between each of the two simples and what is under its opposite. And as for beyond that of judgments longitudinally and laterally. Some people are just: a particular simple affirmative is true if some are just and the rest however they are, and false if there is no just person among people however they are. No person is unjust: a universal negative modified is true if all are nonexistent or just, and false in the remaining cases. No person is unjust? contains just: No person is just: a universal simple negative is false in what its contradictory is true, which is if some are and the rest however they are, and true in what its contradictory is false, which is if there is no just person among them however they are. Some people are unjust: a particular modified affirmative is false in what its contradictory is true, which is if all are just or nonexistent, and true in the remaining cases, which is what its contradictory is false in. Some people are unjust: a universal privative negative is false if all are? particular privative affirmative is true in what? or some are unjust and the rest however they are, its contradictory is false in what if they are? and true if they are all just or all or some are unjust, and false in the intermediate or nonexistent or mixed. The remaining is what its contradictory is true in. And diagonally as known, and let the assumed table for these propositions be like this: And these layers, although each of them is opposite to what is parallel to it laterally, now when the simple affirmative and the two negatives under it may share in truth when all are just, and the simple is true if some are just only and the two negatives are false in it, and the two negatives are true if all are nonexistent and the simple affirmative is false in it, when the affirmative is more general in one respect and more specific in another respect, and the simple negative opposite to the simple affirmative in its relation to what is under it is also like that, since we have shown that whatever is more general than something else, its opposite is more specific than the opposite of that other, and whatever is more specific than something else, its opposite is more general than the opposite of that other. So from the aspect that the simple affirmative is more specific than what is under it, its opposite must be more general than the opposite of what is under it, and from the aspect that it is more general than what is under it, its opposite must be more specific than the opposite of what is under it. Consequently, there is no impossibility of combining in truth between the simple affirmative and what is diagonal to it from the two affirmatives, nor of combining in falsehood between the simple negative and what is diagonal to it from the two negatives, because that was only in what preceded due to the necessity that the simple affirmative and the simple negative are opposites, and that what is under the affirmative is more general than it, and what is under the negative is more specific than it, and from the necessity of the truth of the more specific follows the truth of the more general, and from the falsehood of the more general follows the falsehood of the more specific. For if combination among affirmatives in truth was imagined, then the simple negative would be true along with the truth of what is opposite to it from the simple affirmative, because the modified and privative affirmatives are more specific than the simple negative, and from that would follow the truth of what is opposite to the two modified and privative affirmatives, assuming their truth; because what is opposite to them is more general than what was assumed true along with their truth from the simple affirmative, and from the truth of the more specific follows the truth of the more general. And in this manner was the impossibility of combining negatives in falsehood. As for here, it has become clear that the simple affirmative is not more specific than what is under it, nor is its opposite more general than what is under it. And as for the privative negative, it is more general in truth than the modified negative, for it is true in what the modified negative is true, which is when all are nonexistent or just, and it is true in what the modified negative is not true, which is when all are intermediate or potential or all? true.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the truth conditions and relations between simple (بسيطة), modified (معدولة), and privative (عدمية) categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that the ruling regarding the ratio of the simple propositions to the modified and privative ones, especially concerning universals that are negative, is contrary to what was previously verified. He notes that the two simple propositions (affirmative and negative) can be either more general or more specific than the propositions under them (i.e., the modified and privative ones). Consequently, there is no direct contradiction in truth or falsehood between each simple proposition and what is under its opposite (the opposite of the simple proposition). The author then provides examples: 'Some people are just' is a particular simple affirmative, true if some are just (others can be anything), false if no one is just. 'No person is unjust' is a universal negative modified, true if all are nonexistent or all are just, false otherwise. 'No person is just' is a universal simple negative, false when some are just (its contradictory true), true when none are just (its contradictory false). 'Some people are unjust' is a particular modified affirmative, false when all are just or nonexistent (its contradictory true), true otherwise. A privative negative example is given but the OCR is disrupted. The author then discusses diagonal relations (قطراً) and suggests a table. He explains that because the simple affirmative can be more general in one respect and more specific in another relative to the modified/privative propositions under it, the opposite of the simple affirmative (the simple negative) also has a varied relation. This leads to the conclusion that it is not impossible for the simple affirmative to combine in truth with the two affirmatives diagonal to it (modified and privative affirmatives), nor for the simple negative to combine in falsehood with the two negatives diagonal to it. In the previous analysis, the opposite was true because the simple affirmative was always more general than its subalternates, and the simple negative was always more specific. Now the relationship is more complex. Finally, the author clarifies that the privative negative is more general in truth than the modified negative, as it is true in more cases (e.g., when people are intermediate or potential). The passage is a rigorous exposition of the logical behavior of these proposition types, crucial for understanding complex syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

According to the passage, when is a universal negative modified proposition (like 'No person is unjust') true?
A) When all people are just.

Key Takeaway
Simple, modified, and privative propositions have distinct truth conditions that depend on the subject's existential status and the predicate's nature.

Propositions
advanced
10 min
Diagonal (قطراً) Relations Between Simple and Modified/Privative Propositions
Author defines truth conditions for each type of categorical proposition and their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a simple proposition can combine in truth or falsehood diagonally with a modified/privative proposition of the same quality, based on the variability of generality.

Core Idea
The simple proposition is not always more specific than its modified counterparts, allowing diagonal co-truth and co-falsehood.

The author introduces a crucial nuance: the simple affirmative may be more general or more specific than the modified/privative affirmatives under it. This leads to a different logical behavior compared to the standard square of opposition. Specifically, it becomes possible for a simple affirmative to be true along with a modified or privative affirmative diagonally, and for a simple negative to be false along with a modified or privative negative diagonally. Understanding this is key to accurately mapping the truth relations among these proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard square of opposition
In the classical square, the simple affirmative is always more specific than its subalternates (e.g., 'Every A is B' implies 'Some A is B'). The simple negative is always more general. This forces specific contradiction and contrariety relations.
2
Identify the diagonal (قطراً) relation
Diagonal here means the relationship between a simple proposition and a modified/privative proposition of the same quality (both affirmative or both negative) that are not directly opposite. For example, the simple affirmative and the modified affirmative are diagonal to each other.
The author uses the term 'قطراً' to denote this relation.
3
Understand why the relation is not fixed
The passage says: 'the two simples here may be more general than what is under them, and may be more specific.' This means that the simple affirmative can sometimes be more general than the modified/privative affirmatives, and sometimes less. Thus, the usual subalternation does not hold.
Consequently, the simple negative can also be more specific or more general relative to the modified/privative negatives.
4
Apply the diagonal combination rule
Because of this variability, it is possible for a simple affirmative to be true simultaneously with a modified/privative affirmative (e.g., when all are just). Similarly, a simple negative can be false simultaneously with a modified/privative negative. The author concludes: 'there is no impossibility of combining in truth between the simple affirmative and what is diagonal to it from the two affirmatives, nor of combining in falsehood between the simple negative and what is diagonal to it from the two negatives.'
This is contrary to the situation in the previous analysis where such combination was impossible.

The first art: On categorical propositions. And if the universals among them are negatives, then the ruling regarding the ratio of the two simple to the two modified and the two privative, and regarding the ratio of the simple affirmative diagonally to the two modified and privative affirmatives, and regarding the ratio of the simple negative diagonally to the two modified and privative negatives, is contrary to what we verified, for the two simples here may be more general than what is under them, and may be more specific, as you know. So the simple negative is not more general than what is under it, nor is its opposite more specific than what is under it. Consequently, there is no contradiction in truth nor in falsehood between each of the two simples and what is under its opposite. And as for beyond that of judgments longitudinally and laterally. Some people are just: a particular simple affirmative is true if some are just and the rest however they are, and false if there is no just person among people however they are. So from the aspect that the simple affirmative is more specific than what is under it, its opposite must be more general than the opposite of what is under it, and from the aspect that it is more general than what is under it, its opposite must be more specific than the opposite of what is under it. Consequently, there is no impossibility of combining in truth between the simple affirmative and what is diagonal to it from the two affirmatives, nor of combining in falsehood between the simple negative and what is diagonal to it from the two negatives, because that was only in what preceded due to the necessity that the simple affirmative and the simple negative are opposites, and that what is under the affirmative is more general than it, and what is under the negative is more specific than it, and from the necessity of the truth of the more specific follows the truth of the more general, and from the falsehood of the more general follows the falsehood of the more specific. For if combination among affirmatives in truth was imagined, then the simple negative would be true along with the truth of what is opposite to it from the simple affirmative, because the modified and privative affirmatives are more specific than the simple negative, and from that would follow the truth of what is opposite to the two modified and privative affirmatives, assuming their truth; because what is opposite to them is more general than what was assumed true along with their truth from the simple affirmative, and from the truth of the more specific follows the truth of the more general. And as for the privative negative, it is more general in truth than the modified negative, for it is true in what the modified negative is true, which is when all are nonexistent or just, and it is true in what the modified negative is not true, which is when all are intermediate or potential or all? true.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the truth conditions and relations between simple (بسيطة), modified (معدولة), and privative (عدمية) categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that the ruling regarding the ratio of the simple propositions to the modified and privative ones, especially concerning universals that are negative, is contrary to what was previously verified. He notes that the two simple propositions (affirmative and negative) can be either more general or more specific than the propositions under them (i.e., the modified and privative ones). Consequently, there is no direct contradiction in truth or falsehood between each simple proposition and what is under its opposite (the opposite of the simple proposition). The author then provides examples: 'Some people are just' is a particular simple affirmative, true if some are just (others can be anything), false if no one is just. 'No person is unjust' is a universal negative modified, true if all are nonexistent or all are just, false otherwise. 'No person is just' is a universal simple negative, false when some are just (its contradictory true), true when none are just (its contradictory false). 'Some people are unjust' is a particular modified affirmative, false when all are just or nonexistent (its contradictory true), true otherwise. A privative negative example is given but the OCR is disrupted. The author then discusses diagonal relations (قطراً) and suggests a table. He explains that because the simple affirmative can be more general in one respect and more specific in another relative to the modified/privative propositions under it, the opposite of the simple affirmative (the simple negative) also has a varied relation. This leads to the conclusion that it is not impossible for the simple affirmative to combine in truth with the two affirmatives diagonal to it (modified and privative affirmatives), nor for the simple negative to combine in falsehood with the two negatives diagonal to it. In the previous analysis, the opposite was true because the simple affirmative was always more general than its subalternates, and the simple negative was always more specific. Now the relationship is more complex. Finally, the author clarifies that the privative negative is more general in truth than the modified negative, as it is true in more cases (e.g., when people are intermediate or potential). The passage is a rigorous exposition of the logical behavior of these proposition types, crucial for understanding complex syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what allows a simple affirmative and a modified/privative negative to be true together diagonally?
A) Because the simple affirmative is always more general than the negatives.
B) Because the simple affirmative is always more specific than the negatives.
C) Because the simple affirmative is not necessarily more specific than the negatives, and their generality relation varies.
D) Because the simple affirmative and the negatives have opposite truth values.

Key Takeaway
The diagonal relation between simple and modified/privative propositions allows for co-truth and co-falsehood because the simple proposition's generality is not fixed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the author's examples, construct a scenario where a simple affirmative and a modified negative are both true diagonally. The passage indicates this happens when all people are just: 'Some people are just' (simple affirmative) is true, and 'No person is unjust' (modified negative) is also true. Confirm this using the truth conditions from the previous lesson.
?
Now consider the case when all people are nonexistent. Is the simple affirmative true? (false) Are the two negatives true? (true, since modified negative is true when all are nonexistent). So here the diagonal combines truth? Actually the simple affirmative is false while the negatives are true, so it's not a case of diagonal co-truth. Identify a case from the passage where diagonal co-truth does not occur.
